2001 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Davis v. Franchise Tax Board


Civ. No. 16487


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


71 Cal. App. 3d 998; 139 Cal. Rptr. 797; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1678



July 26, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 22, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 260656, Charles W. Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs taxpayers appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which sustained defendant state tax board's denial of a refund to plaintiffs.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs taxpayers brought suit against defendant state tax board when it denied plaintiffs the option of income averaging their California income tax when they moved from the state. Plaintiffs appealed contending Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 18243 was unconstitutional and violated U.S. Const. art. IV, § 2, the privilege and immunities clause. The court affirmed holding that the state had validly treated citizens and non-citizens differently. The court held that to have done so, defendant had to show a valid independent reason for the disparity, which defendant did, by illustrating defendant's need to make certain the tax reflects an individual's true income.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment which sustained defendant state tax board's denial of a refund to plaintiffs taxpayers. The court held that plaintiffs were not entitled to resident tax treatment because they were non-residents and the disparity of treatment was constitutional because defendant advanced a valid independent reason for the disparity.

 

2002 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Swearington


Crim. No. 29332


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


71 Cal. App. 3d 935; 140 Cal. Rptr. 5; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1672



July 25, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 11, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A190028, William A. Ross, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), after a jury convicted him of two felony counts of indecent exposure under Cal. Penal Code § 314, pursuant to the recidivist provision.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of two counts of indecent exposure under Cal. Penal Code § 314, after he walked through a college campus building wearing nothing but tennis shoes, stopping to pose for two women. Appellant's prior felony convictions under § 314 converted the offenses to felonies. Appellant sought review. The court reversed, finding that the trial court erred by failing to instruct the jury on the lesser-included offense of disorderly conduct because both offenses required "lewd" conduct in a public place. Evidence of similar incidents in which defendant was sighted nude was only relevant to the issue of sexual intent, not for the purposes of showing identity, characteristic method, or motive. Defendant had not contested the identity issue, evidence of other acts did not establish a motive on defendant's part, and the characteristic method issue was another way of describing identity. The recidivist provision of Cal. Penal Code § 314 was still valid, and the proper punishment was under Cal. Penal Code § 18.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, holding that the trial court should have instructed the jury as to the lesser-included offense of disorderly conduct. The evidence of other similar acts by defendant was only admissible to show the sexual intent element, and the recidivist provision of the indecent exposure statute was still valid.

 

2003 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Medrano


Crim. No. 8962.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD Dist.


71 Cal. App. 3d 823; 138 Cal. Rptr. 322; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1661; 96 L.R.R.M. 2672



July 19, 1977 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted Aug. 12, 1977 (see 78 C.A.3d 198)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant union organizers appealed a judgment from the municipal court (California) which convicted them of criminal trespass under Cal. Penal Code § 602(n).


OVERVIEW: A municipal court jury found defendant union organizers guilty of violating Cal. Penal Code § 602(n) by trespassing upon a farm labor camp leased to a farm labor contractor. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction of one defendant and reversed judgment as to the other. California's Agricultural Labor Relations Act of 1975 (ALRA), Cal. Lab. Code § 1140 et seq., did not divest the municipal court of jurisdiction in this case, the court held, because the ALRA specifically excluded farm labor contractors from its coverage pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code § 1140.4(c). Thus, the court concluded, the farm labor contractor was definitionally incapable of an unfair labor practice. Noting that the ALRA provided practicable alternative channels of communication, the court determined that union access to the labor contractor's camp was not necessary to the fulfillment of the inhabitants' rights under Cal. Lab. Code § 1152. However, finding no evidence from which a jury could reasonably infer that any peace officer requested one of the defendants to leave the private property, the court reversed his conviction.


OUTCOME: The court found that the state labor regulations did not preempt prosecution of defendant union organizers for criminal trespass. However, finding the evidence insufficient to support one the conviction as to one defendant, the court reversed the judgment as to him and affirmed as to the other defendant.

 

2004 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Silverton


Crim. No. 28211.Crim. No. 30496.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


71 Cal. App. 3d 790; 139 Cal. Rptr. 584; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1656



July 15, 1977.


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Hg. den. September 8, 1977, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Appellate Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14  [*1] Rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the lower court (California) convicting him of conspiracy to violate criminal laws under Cal. Penal Code § 182, subd. 2, operating a home-finding agency without a license under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1310, and advertising without a license under Cal. Civ. Code § 224p.


OVERVIEW: On appeal from convictions of conspiracy to violate criminal laws under Cal. Penal Code § 182, subd. 2, operating a home-finding agency without a license under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1310, and advertising without a license under Cal. Civ. Code § 224p, the court reversed the first two convictions and upheld the advertising without a license conviction. The court held that defendant held no license or permit to run an adoption service, and that the business of defendant was obtaining children for adoption. While U.S. Const. amend. I correlated the right of free speech and press with the right of a private citizen to place an advertisement, it did not mean that any advertisement was appropriate. The court was required to balance the interests of the state in restricting advertisements with the right of free speech and press. The prosecution stated that Cal. Civ. Code § 224p was a part of the regulatory scheme for adoptions and regulation was a valid exercise of the state's police power. The court held that when the legislature repealed a criminal statute, the dismissal of a pending criminal proceeding charging such conduct was required.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions of operating a home-finding agency without a license and conspiracy to violate criminal laws. The court affirmed a conviction of advertising without a license. Defendant's petition for a writ of habeas corpus was denied. The court concluded that the statute upon which the reversed convictions were based was repealed and dismissal was required.

 

2005 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Youngblood v. Board of Supervisors


Civ. No. 14893.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


71 Cal. App. 3d 655; 139 Cal. Rptr. 741; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1646



July 12, 1977 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] as modified August 10, 1977, on denial of petition for rehearing
Hg. granted Nov. 3, 1977 (see 22 C.3d 644)



DISPOSITION: The petition for rehearing is denied August 10, 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant homeowners sought review of a judgment from the trial court (California) that dismissed its petition for writ of mandate under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1085, and Cal. Govt. Code § 65860(a) and (c), after sustaining the demurrer of respondent county board supervisors.


OVERVIEW: Appellants sought review of judgment sustaining respondent county board supervisors' demurrer and dismissing appellants' petition for writ of mandate. Appellants claimed that respondent disregarded a mandatory duty imposed by Cal. Gov't Code §§ 65860(c) to change an agricultural use zoning ordinance to conform to the applicable community general plan. Appellants sought to compel respondent to hold further hearings to obtain such compliance. The court reversed the trial court dismissal and remanded, concluding the trial court erred in sustaining the demurrer based on the reasoning that the refusal to rezone was a discretionary legislative act. The court determined where, as in the present case, the statute defined specific duties, the course of conduct the governing body was required to take became mandatory and eliminated any element of discretion. The court elaborated that respondent was required to amend a zoning ordinance into community plan conformity when it became inconsistent with the general plan by reason of amendment. The court indicated if respondent did not rezone within a reasonable time, a writ could issue.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment that dismissed appellants' petition for writ of mandate after sustaining respondent county board supervisors' demurrer because respondent was required by statute to amend zoning ordinance at issue to conform with a community plan. The court held that if respondent failed to discharge such statutorily imposed mandatory duty within a reasonable time a writ compelling such action would issue.

 

2006 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Piazza Properties, Ltd. v. Department of Motor Vehicles


Civ. No. 38945


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


71 Cal. App. 3d 622; 138 Cal. Rptr. 357; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1643



July 12, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 8, 1977, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 8, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 285859, John M. Brenner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The portion of the judgment appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) denying its claim against respondent California Department of Motor Vehicles for a recovery of licensing fees erroneously paid as time barred under Cal. Veh. Code § 42232.


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporation's predecessor sued respondent California Department of Motor Vehicles to obtain recovery of licensing fees on construction vehicles not subject to fees under Cal. Veh. Code § 42231. The trial court held that certain vehicles were not subject to the fees, but ruled that appellant's claim was barred by the three year statute of limitations in § 42242. The court affirmed the trial court's order. In so ruling, the court rejected appellant's argument that the four year statute of limitations period set forth Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 338(4) for the state to recover overpayments to taxpayers applied, noting that the right of an individual to sue the state is governed by statute, including any limitations period. The court also rejected appellant's argument that the filing period should not have commenced until appellant was aware of the error in the fees, noting that the period commenced when the state's obligation for the faulty fee was incurred. Finally, the court held that respondent was not estopped from denying appellant's claim because of its error, noting that appellant had a duty to correct the error within three years under § 42232.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the portion of the judgment appealed from because appellant corporation's claim to recover improperly paid licensing fees was barred by the applicable statute of limitations.

 

2007 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Alvarez v. Wiley


Civ. No. 3063


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


71 Cal. App. 3d 599; 139 Cal. Rptr. 550; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1640



July 11, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 5, 1977, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 15, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 81885, Jay R. Ballantyne, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California) that dismissed appellant's class action seeking to enjoin respondents' alleged failure to treat heroin addicted inmates.


OVERVIEW: Appellant's son died as an inmate in jail, due to congestive heart failure brought on by the son's heroin addiction. Appellant filed a class action for equitable relief on behalf of heroin addicts who were or would in the future be incarcerated in the jail and to have declared and enjoined, as unconstitutional, the practices of respondents in failing to provide adequate medical treatment for admittees to the jail. The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant's action. The court held that a class action was inappropriate because appellant was not a member of the class. The court also held that appellant could not sue in his own right for the deprivation of his son's rights apart from his remedy under the wrongful death cause of action. The court held that appellant's claim failed to allege an action for damages for the wrongful death of his son.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant's class action based on the failure to supply treatment to appellant's son in jail because appellant lacked standing to either bring a class action on behalf of heroin addicted prison inmates, or to assert the deprivation of his son's civil rights.

 

2008 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Charyn


Crim. No. 16066


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION One


71 Cal. App. 3d 355; 139 Cal. Rptr. 524; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1619



June 30, 1977 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified July 5 and July 8, 1977
Hg. den. Sept. 8, 1977, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Appellate Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; Rules 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an institutionalized person, sought a writ of habeas corpus to a California trial court. The trial court had disapproved the recommendation that petitioner be released on parole, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 7375(c), from the institution in which she had been confined since being found not guilty of murder by reason of insanity.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, an institutionalized person, pleaded not guilty, by reason of insanity, to murder, and was committed to a hospital to Cal. Penal Code § 1026. After a period of treatment, petitioner presented uncontroverted testimony at a parole hearing that her release posed no health or safety danger, and that she would benefit from parole. Nonetheless, the trial court disapproved of the recommendation, and denied parole. Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 7375(c), claiming abuse of discretion. The court agreed. It held that uncontradicted testimony that is not inherently improbable cannot be arbitrarily disregarded, and should have been accepted as true, unless the trial court found that the witness was not telling the truth, or his testimony was inherently improbable due to inaccuracy, uncertainty, lapse of time, interest, or bias. Because the lower court record did not set out precise grounds upon which the parole denial was based, and because due process required such specificity, the court remanded, directing the trial court to conduct further hearings, and to specifically state its reasons for further rulings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's disapproval of a recommendation, made by the medical director of a mental institution, that petitioner, an institutionalized person, be released on parole. The court found that the absence from the trial court's order of the precise grounds on which the lower court relied in denying parole required remand, for further hearing and consideration, so that an adequate record could be made.

 

2009 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Van Hook


Crim. No. 15824


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION One


71 Cal. App. 3d 264; 139 Cal. Rptr. 343; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1611



June 29, 1977


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. August 25, 1977, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in Official Appellate Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14 Rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution sought review of an order of the Contra Costa County Superior Court (California), which suspended the execution of defendant's sentence and granted him probation after defendant pleaded guilty to robbery and possession of a firearm by an ex-felon within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to robbery and possession of a firearm by an ex-felon. He admitted to use of a firearm within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5, requiring the imposition of mandatory additional punishment. The trial court sentenced him to probation, finding that while defendant used a firearm within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5, as specifically alleged in the information, he did not use a firearm within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 1203.06, requiring that he be denied probation. The court reversed, holding that the trial court erred in granted defendant probation because "use" within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5 was not different from "use" within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 1203.06. It was reasonable to assume that the legislature intended "use" to mean the same for Cal. Penal Code §§ 12022.5 and 1203.06, and the information, by alleging use of a firearm, within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5, adequately informed defendant of the charge against him. Defendant was afforded adequate notification that there was a possibility he would not have been eligible for probation.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed. Defendant was erroneously granted probation, because probation had to be denied based on his "use of a firearm," which he had admitted to as part of his plea agreement. The trial court was directed to enter judgment in accordance with the court's opinion, or in its discretion, upon defendant's motion, to vacate defendant's pleas of guilty to all allegations.

 

2010 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Conservatorship of Chambers


Civ. No. 39978, Crim. No. 16233


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


71 Cal. App. 3d 277; 139 Cal. Rptr. 357; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1612



June 29, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 29, 1977, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 25, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, No. 22035, Thomas N. Healy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appointing the conservator is affirmed and the writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant schizophrenic sought relief from an order by the Superior Court of Solano County (California), granting respondent guardian's petition for appointment as conservator under the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 5000 et seq. Appellant also filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus to obtain release from his allegedly unlawful confinement at a state hospital, attacking the constitutionality of the statute.


OVERVIEW: An investigation report recommended conservatorship of appellant schizophrenic as he was gravely disabled as a result of a mental disorder under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5008. After consulting with counsel, appellant waived an adversarial hearing. On appeal from an order appointing a conservator and confining appellant to a state hospital, the court held that in the context of the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act (Act), Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 5000 et seq., the gravely disabled standard was constitutionally precise and, as it required a causal link between a specifically defined and diagnosed mental disorder and an inability to care for one's basic needs, the term was neither constitutionally vague nor overbroad. The court held that as appellant was represented by counsel and waived his right to file a motion for a trial following the conservator appointment, the lower court was not required to voir dire appellant of his right to a jury trial and confrontation. The trial court was entitled to conclude that there was a voluntary, knowing, and intelligent waiver and retroactive application of the on-the-record voir dire under Cal. Prob. Code § 1754.1 was not constitutionally required.


OUTCOME: The order appointing a conservator was affirmed and appellant schizophrenic's petition for writ of habeas corpus was denied, because appellant's waiver of his right to jury trial on the issue of grave disability was voluntary, knowing, and intelligent, and was not constitutionally defective for lack of an on-the-record voir dire.

 

2011 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Fox v. Los Angeles


Civ. No. 48896


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Five


70 Cal. App. 3d 885; 139 Cal. Rptr. 180; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1578



June 21, 1977 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Aug. 18, 1977 (see 22 C.3d 792)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged an order of the Los Angeles Superior Court (California), which found for respondent taxpayer that appellant's practice of illuminating the windows of city hall in a cross configuration violated the establishment clause of U.S. Const. amend. I and granted a preliminary injunction restraining the practice.


OVERVIEW: Respondent taxpayer sought to enjoin appellant city from practicing its usual custom of illuminating the windows of city hall in a cross configuration at Christmas. The trial court found that there was a reasonable probability that respondent would be successful in establishing that appellant's practice violated the establishment clause of U.S. Const. amend. I and granted a preliminary injunction restraining the practice. On appeal, the court reviewed the evidence based on application of a tripartite test. It concluded that the illumination had a secular purpose of promoting peace and good fellowship, that it did not have the primary effect of promoting religion, and that it did not foster an excessive entanglement between government and religion. Therefore, the court reversed the trial court's judgment and held that appellant's practice of temporarily illuminating the windows in city hall in the form of a cross did not violate the establishment clause of U.S. Const. amend. I.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment, which granted a preliminary injunction in favor respondent taxpayer, because appellant city's practice of illuminating the windows of city hall in a cross configuration did not violate the First Amendment.

 

2012 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Institute of Governmental Advocates v. Younger


Civ. No. 48818


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


70 Cal. App. 3d 878; 139 Cal. Rptr. 233; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1577



June 21, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-110052, Harry L. Hupp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Fair Political Practices Commission challenged the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which issued a preliminary injunction prohibiting respondent state from enforcing the Political Reform Act of 1974, Cal. Gov't. Code § 86202, as interpreted by appellant, against respondent lobbyists.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Fair Political Practices Commission construed the Political Reform Act of 1974, Cal. Gov't. Code § 86202 to prevent lobbyist from making recommendations to employers as to which political contributions they should make. Respondent lobbyists successfully sought an injunction to bar respondent state from enforcing § 86202 as interpreted by appellant. On appeal, the court affirmed the injunction, noting that appellant had correctly interpreted the section. The court held, however, that as interpreted the statute constituted an unconstitutional violation of the freedom of speech. The court reasoned that the guarantee of freedom of speech prohibited not only governmental action directly restricting speech, but also action that had a "chilling effect" on legitimate speech.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the issuance of the preliminary injunction, holding that appellant Fair Political Practices Commission's interpretation of the statute constituted an invalid invasion of the right of free speech guaranteed by both the federal and state constitutions.

 

2013 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Witt v. Morrow


Civ. No. 14789


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


70 Cal. App. 3d 817; 139 Cal. Rptr. 161; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1570



June 17, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 11, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 367104, James L. Focht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment and order are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city councilman challenged an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) enjoining him from participating in decisions regarding a particular shopping center due to a conflict of interest.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city councilman was an employee of a company that was attempting to purchase a shopping center in appellant's city. The city obtained an injunction against appellant, prohibiting him from participating in decisions regarding that shopping center because of a conflict of interest. Appellant sought review of the injunction and the court affirmed. The court held that the Political Reform Act of 1974 (Act) prohibited state and local government officials from participating in decisions where it appeared that the outcome would likely benefit a corporation by whom he or she was employed. The court found that decisions on the development of land across the street from the shopping center could affect the value of the company's investment and thus, under the Act, appellant was properly enjoined from participating in any decisions regarding the shopping center.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of injunction holding that, because appellant city councilman was employed by a company who was planning to buy a shopping center, decisions by appellant regarding development of land in that area could affect the value of that company's investment.

 

2014 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Marasco v. Wadsworth


Civ. No. 48496


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Four


70 Cal. App. 3d 731; 139 Cal. Rptr. 143; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1557



June 15, 1977 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Sept. 8, 1977 (see 21 C.3d 82)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff challenged the decision of the court, which upheld the California trial court's decision sustaining the demurrer of one defendant, the driver of the car in which plaintiff's decedent suffered fatal injuries, in plaintiff's wrongful death action, and following review by the California Supreme Court, the case was transferred back to the court for reconsideration.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff challenged the decision of the trial court, which sustained the demurrer of one defendant, the driver of the car in which plaintiff's decedent suffered fatal injuries, in plaintiff's wrongful death action. On reconsideration after the California Supreme Court transferred the case back to the court, the court affirmed, holding that defendant driver could not be added to the complaint under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 474 as one of the Doe defendants named in the original complaint, where defendant driver's true identity was known from the outset, the original complaint failed to state any cause of action against her as a fictitiously named defendant, where it sought damages against another named defendant and Does I through X as owners of the vehicle colliding with defendant driver's vehicle, and stated no cause of action against Does XI through XX, and the statute of limitations, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340(3), had run on any cause of action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the demurrer against plaintiff in her wrongful death action, holding that one defendant, the driver of the car in which plaintiff's decedent suffered fatal injuries, could not be added to the complaint where her identity was know from the outset, the original complaint failed to state a cause of action against her as a fictitiously named defendant, and the statute of limitations had run on any cause of action.

 

2015 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Heffner


Crim. No. 30197


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


70 Cal. App. 3d 643; 139 Cal. Rptr. 45; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1548



June 13, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Inglewood Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. M-122499, William R. Clay, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Municipal Court for the Inglewood Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of a violation of Cal. Penal Code §12031(a) after construing a Taser to be a firearm within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 12031(e).


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed a judgment which convicted him of possession of a loaded firearm under Cal. Penal Code § 12031(a). On appeal the court affirmed the judgment and found that a Taser was a firearm within the meaning of § 12031(a) and a loaded firearm within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 12031(e). The court found that the Taser was a firearm because it was designed as a weapon. Second, the court determined that the Taser's met the component of being expelled by force or combustion because the Taser operated by electrical ignition of smokeless powder and because its barbed contactors could have been considered a barrel within the ordinary meaning of the term. Consequently, the Taser was a firearm within section 12031(a). The court found that a Taser was a loaded firearm under Cal. Penal Code § 12031(e) because, upon insertion of the barbed contactors, the Taser was loaded. The court found the due process notice requirements were met by Cal. Penal Code § 12031 and the average person would have interpreted the Taser to be a loaded firearm.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendant of carrying a loaded firearm because the weapon defendant was carrying, a Taser, was a firearm and a loaded firearm within the meaning of the criminal statute.

 

2016 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Michael D.


Crim. No. 16740


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


70 Cal. App. 3d 522; 140 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1536



June 9, 1977 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a writ of habeas corpus issue. The orders granting letters of guardianship to Dorothy Murphy are vacated, and petitioner is ordered discharged from any detention made pursuant to such orders. Petitioner is remanded to the juvenile court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mentally disabled minor sought review of an order from the Division Supervisor of Family and Children Services, City and County of San Francisco (California), which appointed petitioner a temporary guardian who then committed petitioner to a mental hospital under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6000(b).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner mentally disabled minor applied for a writ of habeas corpus and sought review of his involuntary commitment to a mental hospital pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6000(b). The trial court ordered that petitioner be committed. On review the court found that petitioner had been released from the mental hospital but the court did not consider the matter moot and proceeded to determine the matter on its merits. The court granted petitioner's writ of habeas corpus and directed that the trial court's order, which granted letters of guardianship, be vacated and petitioner be discharged and remanded to juvenile court. The court found that petitioner could not be voluntarily committed by a guardian under the provisions of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6000 because the legislature had denied the juvenile court the authority to commit such a ward, and the court could not circumvent the law by appointing a guardian to do so. The court found that under the Lanterman-Petris-Short-Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 5350-5370, petitioner would first have to be found gravely disabled by a jury, then a conservator appointed, and then only the conservator could seek commitment of petitioner.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner mentally disabled minor's writ of habeas corpus, ordered that the trial court vacate their order which granted letters of guardianship, discharged petitioner from any detention, and remanded petitioner to juvenile court. The court held that petitioner could not have been committed to a state mental hospital without compliance with the necessary statutory procedures to ensure that petitioner's rights were not violated.

 

2017 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Bill v. Williams


Civ. No. 40013


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


70 Cal. App. 3d 531; 139 Cal. Rptr. 19; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1537



June 9, 1977

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, candidate and associated individuals, appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), which denied declaratory and injunctive relief sought against respondent county clerk. Appellants challenged the requirement that appellant candidate pay a filing fee, or in lieu thereof submit a petition that contained the signatures of 1,758 registered voters, to qualify for a place on the ballot as a candidate.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, candidate and associated individuals, challenged the requirement that candidates pay a filing fee, or in lieu thereof submit a petition containing the signatures of 1,758 registered voters, to qualify for a place on the ballot as a candidate for the office of supervisor. The trial court denied appellant's preliminary injunction. On appeal, the court affirmed the denial of the preliminary injunction, holding that the "in lieu" petition procedure did not establish a denial of equal protection because it did not purport to discriminate between the residents of counties similarly situated. Further, the court held that gathering the requisite signatures from a pool of qualified signers was not an unreasonably burdensome or impractical undertaking for a reasonably diligent county supervisorial candidate for the third district.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant candidate's and appellant individual's preliminary injunction, holding that the "in lieu" petition procedure for qualification on the ballot as a candidate did not establish a denial of equal protection because it did not purport to discriminate between the residents of counties similarly situated.

First City Bank v. Franchise Tax Board


Civ. No. 38627


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


70 Cal. App. 3d 444; 139 Cal. Rptr. 12; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1529



June 7, 1977

* Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 667342, William E. Mullins, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff bank challenged the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), that entered a summary judgment denying its claim for a tax refund paid to respondent board.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff bank paid a preference tax under protest on the ground that the additional tax on banks was not a tax measured by net income and was therefore in conflict with former Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 16, and the provisions of the Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 23186. The trial court in an action for summary judgment rejected plaintiff's contentions and granted defendant board summary judgment. Plaintiff sought review of that judgment. The court found that there was no conflict with the constitutions or statutory intent because the tax on preference income was calculated by a lower percentage and there was no cumulative affect of that tax, which did not exceed the statutory maximum under § 23186. The summary judgment was affirmed.

The taxes levied did not exceed the maximum rate as some preference income was taxed at a reduced rate.

Wyshak v. Wyshak


Civ. No. 49741


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


70 Cal. App. 3d 384; 138 Cal. Rptr. 811; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1524



June 3, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 21, 1977, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 28, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. D 552208, Nancy Belcher Watson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former husband challenged a decision by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), in an action by respondent former wife to collect an arrearage of spousal support. At issue was the trial court's denial of appellant's motion to recall a writ of execution and to quash the writ and levy, as well as the order for his appearance.


OVERVIEW: Respondent former wife obtained a writ of execution and levied on property belonging to appellant former husband. In addition, respondent obtained an order for the appearance of respondent as a judgment debtor to be examined concerning his property. The trial court denied appellant relief from respondent's collection efforts and appellant sought review on the basis that the post-judgment procedure that allowed petitioner to obtain the writ and levy execution upon his property was violative of the due process guaranteed to him by U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV. In affirming the trial court, the court held that the California postjudgment procedures for enforcement, by execution, of judgments providing for installment support payments were reasonable and were not violative of due process. The court explained that respondent had a postjudgment execution hearing, a hearing on noticed motion to recall the writ of execution and to quash the writ and levy, and order for appearance of judgment debtor. Respondent failed to contest the amount due under the judgment, offer facts concerning appellant's status, or claim that payment had been made.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision, which denied appellant former husband relief from respondent former wife's efforts to collect an arrearage of spousal support. The court held that the postjudgment procedures for enforcement, by execution, did not deny appellant his rights to procedural due process.

Lilli Ann Corp. v. City and County of San Francisco


Civ. Nos. 36199, 38099


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


70 Cal. App. 3d 162; 138 Cal. Rptr. 759; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1503



May 31, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 30, 1977, and the Petition of the City and County of San Francisco for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 11, 1977. Mosk, J., did not Participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 584690 and 588757, Clayton W. Horn and Robert J. Drewes, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in favor of the City and County of San Francisco and against Lilli Ann Corporation is reversed with directions to the trial court to amend its findings of fact and conclusions of law in accordance with the principles set forth above, to show the correct assessed value, if any, which escaped assessment in any of the taxable years in question.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The case, consolidated for argument and decision, resulted from two decisions in the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco granting one taxpayer relief and denying another taxpayer relief in their challenges to the validity of taxes paid on personal property.


OVERVIEW: This case involved two appeals, which were consolidated for the purposes of argument and decision, arising out of challenges to the validity of taxes paid to the City of County of San Francisco by each of the taxpayers. The taxes in question were assessed in 1967 as escaped assessments under Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 531, on the basis of reappraisals and reassessments of personal property for the years 1964, 1965, and 1966. The reappraisals were the result of the "Knoff Mandate," which followed a grand jury investigation and finding of improprieties associated with the assessment practices in those years. The taxpayers argued that the city could not assert a right to collect taxes from those among the disfavored taxpayers who allegedly received special treatment through assessments lower than generally imposed on business inventories and equipment, because they were still assessed higher than the general ratio to actual market value of assessments of all taxable property within the city and county. The court generally agreed and affirmed the judgment in favor of one of the taxpayers and reversed the judgment against the other taxpayer.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of one taxpayer but declined to permit recovery of anything more than simple interest. The court also reversed the judgment against the other taxpayer and directed the trial court to amend its findings and correct the assessed values for the taxable years in question.

People v. Sandoval


Crim. No. 29171


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


70 Cal. App. 3d 73; 138 Cal. Rptr. 609; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1495; 99 A.L.R.3d 765



May 25, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 128855 VP, Raymond R. Roberts, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to reflect credit for 455 days served. The clerk of the superior court is ordered to enter the credit on the judgment. As modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A preliminary hearing was held on charges arising out of a purse snatching. Defendant was bound over to answer on one count of robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 211. On appeal, defendant contended that the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) erroneously denied him credit for time served as a condition of probation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the trial court erroneously denied him credit for time served as a condition of probation. The court of appeal affirmed the judgment as modified to reflect credit for time served as a condition of probation and in custody awaiting proceedings on the violation. Credits for presentence time should have been granted as defendant served one year as a condition of probation, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5. Section 2900.5, as recently amended, was applicable to limit the maximum duration of a California Youth Authority (CYA) commitment and that the judgment had to be modified accordingly. By its express terms, the statute applied to all felony and misdemeanor convictions and provides for credit for the time a defendant has spent in custody as a condition of probation and awaiting proceedings related to the same conduct. Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5(a), (b). Moreover, commitment to the CYA constituted pronouncement of sentence. The term of incarceration in the CYA institution as a result of the commitment qualified as a term or period of imprisonment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment as modified to reflect credit for time served as a condition of probation and in custody awaiting proceedings on the violation. Defendant should have received credit for days served in the California Youth Authority.

San Francisco Police Officers Assn. v. City and County of San Francisco


Civ. No. 39863


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


69 Cal. App. 3d 1019; 138 Cal. Rptr. 755; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1485; 95 L.R.R.M. 3371



May 24, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 703313, John E. Benson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants City and County of San Francisco (California) sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), granting plaintiffs, police officers association, declaratory judgment restraining defendants' enforcement of the oath provision of City and County of San Francisco Charter § 8.345.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs police officers association filed a complaint for injunctive relief against defendants City and County of San Francisco (California) to restrain enforcement of the oath provision of City and County of San Francisco (California) Charter § 8.345. The trial court granted a preliminary injunction, and defendants sought review. The court affirmed, finding that, although a public employer had a right to require its employees acknowledge receipt of a copy of § 8.345, it did not follow that the employer may require an oath acknowledging receipt. The court found that the constitutional provision and implementing legislation for oaths of public officers, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 1360-1369, and oaths for public employees, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 3103-3109, which prohibited the requirement of any other oath, declaration, or test as a qualification for office or employment, preempted the field.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the preliminary injunction for plaintiffs police officers association to enjoin defendants City and County of San Francisco (California) from enforcing an oath required by city charter, because the oath was preempted by statutory oath provisions.

American Motors Sales Corp. v. New Motor Vehicle Bd.


Civ. No. 15971


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


69 Cal. App. 3d 983; 138 Cal. Rptr. 594; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1483



May 23, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 15, 1977. Regan, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 4, 1977. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 254420, Irving H. Perluss, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant New Motor Vehicle Board of the State of California sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), granting respondent automobile manufacturer a writ of administrative mandate and ruling that Cal. Veh. Code §§ 3060, 3066 violated the due process provisions of Cal. Const. art. I, §7, and U. S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state administrative board challenged the trial court's judgment granting respondent automobile manufacturer a writ of administrative mandate holding that Cal. Veh. Code §§ 3060, 3066 violated the due process provisions of U. S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1, and Cal. Const. art. I, §7. After respondent had terminated an automobile franchise for failure to develop a sufficient sales volume, the dealer successfully pursed an administrative remedy under the Dealers Day in Court Act (Act), 15 U.S.C.S. § 1221 et seq. (1974), causing respondent to seek and obtain the writ. On appeal, the court held that §§ 3060-3069 of the Act violated constitutional due process by mandating that appellant be composed of nine members, four of whom were required to be automobile dealers while none were required to be automobile manufacturers. Because dealer-members of respondent had an inherent economic stake in every franchise termination case that came before respondent and because respondent determined whether good cause existed to terminate a franchise, a pecuniary conflict of interest existed. The court affirmed the trial court's grant of a writ of mandate to respondent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of a writ of administrative mandate in favor of respondent automobile manufacturer because a state statute compelling the appointment of four car dealers to respondent, a nine-member administrative board whose purpose was to adjudicate disputes between car dealers and manufacturers, violated state and federal constitutional due process guarantees.

 

2024 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Reyna v. San Francisco


Civ. No. 39236


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


69 Cal. App. 3d 876; 138 Cal. Rptr. 504; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1472



May 19, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 698003, John E. Benson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), that sustained a general demurrer of a codefendant without leave to amend, and the trial court stipulated that a similar ruling had been deemed to have been made with respect to respondent city.


OVERVIEW: Appellant parents challenged the trial court's order that sustained the general demurrer of a codefendant of respondent city, and the trial court's stipulation that the demurrer was deemed to have been made with respect to respondent, and appellants asserted that they should have been able to maintain a wrongful death action on behalf of their unborn child. The court affirmed and found that an unborn child was not included under the ambit of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 377. The court found that prior cases and legislative history did not identify an unborn child as a person under the statute. The court noted that the language of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 29 that conferred a limited cause of action on an unborn child did not impact the language of the wrongful death statute. The court did not find any constitutional violation in the classification of parents of minors and parents of unborn children, because the wrongful death statute was intended to redress pecuniary loss. The court found that parents of unborn children suffered mental anguish caused by death that was not compensable by the statute. The court found that the classification was rationally related to a state interest.


OUTCOME: An order that sustained a general demurrer was affirmed because the wrongful death statute did not include unborn children in its definition as a person, because another statute conferring a limited cause of action on unborn children did not effect the wrongful death statute, and because the classification of parents of minors and parents of the unborn was rationally related to the state interest of redress for pecuniary loss.

In re Williams


Crim. No. 9143


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


69 Cal. App. 3d 840; 138 Cal. Rptr. 384; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1468



May 17, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 22, 1977. Sullivan, J., * participated therein. Mosk, J., Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


* Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The Adult Authority is directed to grant parole consideration to petitioner without regard to the five-year mandatory minimum period of ineligibility therefor imposed by Health and Safety Code section 11350. The petition having served its purpose, it is denied and the order to show cause is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought habeas corpus relief from an order from the Court of Appeal for the Second Appellate District (California), which affirmed petitioner's conviction for possession of heroin in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350, with a prior narcotic conviction. Petitioner argued that the mandatory minimum term of imprisonment was cruel and unusual punishment.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted and sentenced to prison for possession of heroin in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350, with a prior narcotic conviction, and pursuant to that statute petitioner was ineligible for parole consideration for the minimum five-year mandatory term of imprisonment. Petitioner claimed that the mandatory minimum sentence was unconstitutional because it was cruel and unusual punishment. On review the court noted that it had visited the issue before, and had noted that the legislature intended the sentences for crimes that involved delivery and sale of narcotics should have been more severe than mere possession of drugs. The court held that the provision that precluded a second offender from parole consideration without statutory regard to the existence of such mitigating circumstances as possible addict status and the quantity of narcotics involved for the current as well as the prior offense was cruel and unusual punishment in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 17. The court directed the adult authority to grant petitioner parole consideration without regard to the mandatory minimum imposed by Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350(b).


OUTCOME: The court granted the requested relief and directed the adult authority to grant parole to petitioner without regard to the mandatory minimum imposed by the challenged statute because the provision that provided for a more severe sentence for possession than for sale or delivery was unconstitutional because it was cruel and unusual punishment.

People v. Booker


Crim. No. 29948


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


69 Cal. App. 3d 654; 138 Cal. Rptr. 347; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1452



May 11, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-310911, Walter R. Evans, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: In summary, the record in this case exhibits ample evidence to support appellant's convictions and shows that he was treated with scrupulous fairness by the police and the trial court and given able representation by his appointed counsel. There is no indication that any of his constitutional rights have been violated. The judgments of conviction are modified to impose a life sentence instead of the penalty of death, and as so modified are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted defendant of four counts of first degree murder and kidnapping for the purpose of robbery and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of four counts of first degree murder and kidnapping for the purpose of robbery. Defendant was sentenced to life imprisonment after the court commuted his sentence from the death penalty upon a holding that certain death penalty statutes were unconstitutional. On appeal, defendant contended that there was no evidence to prove deliberate and premeditated murder, that he was denied effective assistance of counsel, that his confessions were involuntary, and that he was improperly excluded from trial. The court held that use of the first taped confession, which was admissible as defendant was Mirandized and understood his rights, coupled with the testimony of three eyewitnesses, were sufficient to convict defendant of deliberate and premeditated murder. The court held that in view of defendant's violent behavior in both the courtroom and while in custody, the trial court had the right to exclude defendant from the trial, and that defendant did not carry his burden of proving ineffective assistance of counsel. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment modifying the sentence to life.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and modified the order of the trial court convicting defendant of four counts of first degree murder and kidnapping for the purpose of robbery. The court commuted defendant's sentence to life imprisonment after death penalty statutes were found unconstitutional, but held that eyewitness testimony and confession tape secured after defendant had been Mirandized supported defendant's conviction.

Cooper v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 49246


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


69 Cal. App. 3d 529; 138 Cal. Rptr. 229; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1441



May 4, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C11241, Richard Leslie Wells, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner sought review of a declaratory judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) in favor of respondent county. Appellant asserted that the trial court, on remand, disregarded the appellate court's interpretation of Cal. Pub. Res. Code §§ 21169 and 21170.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowner began construction of a recreational complex after his application for rezoning was approved by respondent county. Thereafter, respondent landowner filed a complaint for declaratory relief, challenging the constitutionality of the zone-change ordinance, and the legislature passed the California Environmental Quality Act of 1970 (CEQA), Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21000 et seq., which required that an environmental impact report (EIR) be prepared prior to any change in zoning. To ameliorate the hardships created by the new requirements, the legislature enacted Cal. Pub. Res. Code §§ 21169 and 21170, limiting the retroactive application of CEQA. When the trial court's initial declaratory judgment in favor of respondent was reversed by the appellate court, the trial court on remand again granted a declaratory judgment in favor of respondent landowner, holding that appellant was not entitled to relief under §§ 21169 and 21170. Appellant sought review again, asserting that the trial court disregarded the law of the case as set forth in the appellate opinion. The court disagreed, held that the trial court properly applied the law of the case, and affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondents because it held that the trial court correctly applied the law of the case as set forth in the initial appellate opinion. The court held that appellant landowner was not entitled to the retroactive application of a statute limiting the hardships imposed by new environmental regulations.

People v. Tanner


Crim. No. 15777.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


69 Cal. App. 3d 469; 138 Cal. Rptr. 167; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1437



May 2, 1977 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted July 21, 1977 (see 23 C.3d 16)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of the order of the trial court (California), which placed respondent defendant on probation following his conviction for robbery, after striking, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1385, an allegation that respondent had used a firearm, and holding that Cal. Penal Code § 1203.06 could not constitutional prescribe a mandatory prison sentence.


OVERVIEW: Following a trial by jury, respondent defendant was found guilty of robbery in the first degree. The jury also found that he had used a firearm in the commission of the offense. The trial court originally sentenced respondent to prison. However, in contravention of Cal. Penal Code § 1203.06, which prescribed a mandatory prison sentence, the trial court suspended the execution of sentence and placed respondent on probation by striking the use allegation pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1385 and by holding that § 1203.06 unconstitutionally invaded the judicial function of sentencing. The state sought review and the appellate court reversed and directed the trial court to execute the original sentence. § 1203.06 was constitutional given that the Legislature possessed the exclusive power to enact criminal statutes and to designate the punishments therefor, including probation. Further, pursuant to § 1203.06, the Legislature intended to impose a mandatory prison sentence without the possibility of probation upon criminals who used a firearm in the commission of a robbery. Also, the trial court had no power, under § 1385, to circumvent the legislative intent expressed in § 1203.06.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the orders striking the finding of the jury that respondent defendant used a firearm, suspending execution of sentence and granting probation to respondent. The statutory scheme mandated the imposition of a prison term and the trial court was directed to execute the sentence previously imposed.

Neihaus v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 16518


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


69 Cal. App. 3d 340; 137 Cal. Rptr. 905; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1424



April 27, 1977 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition for a peremptory writ of mandate and/or prohibition is denied. The temporary stay of proceedings issued by this court on January 6, 1977, is dissolved and the order to show cause is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Nevada resident sought a peremptory writ of mandate or prohibition after respondent superior court denied his motions to quash service in the personal injury action of real party in interest, a California resident.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest, a California resident, was injured in an automobile accident in Nevada with petitioner Nevada resident. Real party filed a complaint in California against petitioner. Respondent superior court granted petitioner's motion to quash service of the summons. Real party filed a writ of attachment on petitioner's insurance carrier in California. Respondent denied petitioner's motion to quash. Petitioner's counsel filed an answer with a disclaimer that he did not intend to be subject to personal jurisdiction. Respondent denied petitioner's motions to quash and to reconsider. The court denied petitioner's request for a writ of mandate or prohibition, dissolved the temporary stay, and discharged the show cause order. The court held that respondent did not err in denying petitioner's motion to quash, because he was subject to personal jurisdiction by his general appearance. The court held that petitioner's remedy for unauthorized representation was a motion to withdraw the appearance. The court applied a supreme court decision retroactively to find that respondent had no quasi in rem jurisdiction over the insurer's obligation to indemnify.


OUTCOME: The court dissolved the temporary stay, discharged the show cause order, denied the writ request of petitioner Nevada resident's, and held that respondent superior court properly denied the motion to quash and exercised in personam jurisdiction over petitioner, because he made a general appearance in the personal injury claim of real party in interest, a California resident.

 

2030 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Kortum v. Alkire


Civ. No. 38947


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


69 Cal. App. 3d 325; 138 Cal. Rptr. 26; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1422



April 26, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 26, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 153970, Jackson C. Davis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) granting summary judgment to defendants, a city manager, assistant city manager, chief of police, and two police officers, on plaintiffs' claims that police department policies authorized the use of deadly force to apprehend nonviolent felony suspects in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 196, 197, and 837 (1872) and Cal. Penal Code § 835a (1957).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayers filed an action against defendants, a city manager, assistant city manager, chief of police, and two police officers. Plaintiffs alleged that certain of the city police department's policies, which illegally allowed deadly force in the apprehension of nonviolent felony suspects, constituted an unlawful expenditure of tax funds and an unlawful waste of public property in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 196, 197, and 837 (1872), and Cal. Penal Code § 835a (1957) (the statutes). The lower court denied plaintiffs' request for injunctive relief and granted summary judgment to defendants, and the court affirmed. It noted that while the statutes did not distinguish between violent and nonviolent felonies and appeared to authorize the use of deadly force in arresting an attempted or actual nonviolent felon, the courts interpreted the statutes more strictly to prohibit the use of deadly force against a fleeing felony suspect unless the suspected felony was violent. The police department's policies conformed to the statutes and their interpretations by authorizing deadly force only against violent felonies. Thus, the statutes were neither unconstitutional nor invalid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment to defendant, a city manager, assistant city manager, chief of police, and two police officers, on plaintiff taxpayers' action alleging that certain police department policies illegally authorized deadly force to apprehend nonviolent felony suspects. Statutory law prohibited the use of deadly force against nonviolent felony suspects, and the policies of the police department conformed to the law.

 

2031 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Grant v. Adams


Civ. No. 38810


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


69 Cal. App. 3d 127; 137 Cal. Rptr. 834; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1408



April 20, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 193321, Robert E. Carey, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Since neither appellant nor ACSA has been able to persuade us that the board of trustees acted beyond its powers in making the change that it did in appellant's employment status, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which sustained a demurrer to his petition for a writ of mandate without leave to amend and dismissed the cause in favor of respondent board of trustees for the school district when appellant sought reinstatement and damages after a demotion.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee challenged the dismissal of his case in favor of respondent board of trustees for the school district when appellant sought reinstatement and damages after a demotion from principal to a teaching position. Appellant contended that his transfer from his position as principal to a classroom teaching assignment deprived him of property and liberty in violation of the due process guarantees of the U. S. Const. amend. XIV. The court held that in order to have a property interest in public employment, an individual must have a legitimate claim of entitlement to it. Appellant had no constitutionally protected property right to his job as principal. Appellant alleged that the reason stated for his transfer, reduction of the administrative staff, was not supported by the facts. The court conceded that an employer who was obliged to reduce his reasons for terminating a particular employment relationship to writing might be more cautious in seeing that his discretion was wisely exercised. The court held, however, that appellant was unable to persuade them that respondent acted beyond its powers in changing appellant's employment status, and affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against appellant employee and in favor of respondent board of trustees for the school district when appellant sought reinstatement and damages after a demotion. The court held that in order to have a property interest in public employment, an individual must have a legitimate claim of entitlement to it, and appellant had no constitutionally protected property right to his job as principal.

 

2032 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


City and County of San Francisco v. Evankovich


Civ. Nos. 39753, 40133


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


69 Cal. App. 3d 41; 137 Cal. Rptr. 883; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1400; 95 L.R.R.M. 3403; 96 L.R.R.M. 3370



April 19, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied May 19, 1977, and the petitions of the defendants and appellants for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied June 30, 1977. Sullivan, J., * participated therein. Bird, C. J., Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.


* Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 704211, John E. Benson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, labor unions and their officers, appealed an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted a preliminary injunction to plaintiff city after limited evidentiary hearings, despite Ca. Civ. Proc. Code § 527.3, and that prohibited defendants from calling or inducing a strike of public employees, picketing public facilities, or otherwise interfering with public services.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff city obtained a preliminary injunction against defendants, various unions and their officers, prohibiting them from calling a strike of public employees, picketing, or otherwise interfering with public services. The injunction was issued on a verified complaint with limited evidentiary hearings. The court affirmed the granting of the preliminary injunction, and held that newly enacted Ca. Civ. Proc. Code § 527.3 did not change existing law and bar courts from enjoining strikes by public employees, even though its language appeared to contradict existing law. The court held that in the absence of specific legislative authority, public employees still did not have the right to strike and courts could bar related activities that interfered with legitimate state interests. The injunction was not a decision on the merits and could be granted on a verified complaint with limited evidence, and while better specificity in the complaint was desirable, the allegations of interference with essential governmental services were sufficient. Any failures of plaintiff to mediate or abide by its charter were not "unclean hands" and could be raised in another forum.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the granting of a preliminary injunction to plaintiff city, which prohibited defendants, labor unions and their officers, from calling a strike, picketing, or interfering with public services because existing law remained unchanged by recent civil procedure amendments so that public employees still did not have the right to strike. A preliminary injunction was properly issued on a verified complaint with limited evidence.

 

2033 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Los Altos Property Owners Assn. v. Hutcheon


Civ. No. 38366


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


69 Cal. App. 3d 22; 137 Cal. Rptr. 775; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1397



April 18, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 16, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. P28316, William A. Ingram, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order dismissing plaintiffs' second cause of action is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a taxpayer group and an individual taxpayer, sought reversal of an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which dismissed their suit to enjoin defendants, a school district trustees and others, from proceeding with a proposed school consolidation plan. The trial court sustained defendants' demurrer and ruled that the suit did not state a cause of action under Ca. Civ. Proc. Code § 526a or common law.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, a taxpayer group and individual taxpayers, sought to enjoin defendants, school district trustees and others, from implementing a school consolidation plan. Plaintiffs alleged defendants had failed to consider far less costly alternatives that would provide the same or better benefits. The trial court sustained defendants' demurrer because Ca. Civ. Proc. Code § 526a did not name school districts, so plaintiffs did not have standing or a cause of action under § 526a and waste was not actionable by a taxpayer in a representative capacity at common law. The court reversed, holding that § 526a was liberally construed so that it applied to a school district, an agency of the state. The court also held that because plaintiffs alleged that defendants would be expending public funds on a plan that cost a great deal more than alternative plans considered, without a finding of any additional public benefit such allegations went beyond a mere difference in judgment between plaintiffs and defendants and were sufficient to state a cause of action for waste under § 526a. These rulings made the common-law issue moot.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's dismissal on demurrer, holding that plaintiff taxpayers had standing and had stated a cause of action against defendants, school district trustees and others, because the statute authorizing such suits against any county, town, city, or city and county of the state was to be liberally construed and plaintiffs alleged wasteful expenditures of public funds that went beyond a mere difference of opinion.

 

2034 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Gregory M.


Civ. No. 17121


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


68 Cal. App. 3d 1085; 137 Cal. Rptr. 756; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1394



April 14, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. J-84246, Raymond F. Vincent, Judge, and Gilbert N. Mueller, Referee.



DISPOSITION: The order denying the application for rehearing is reversed and the court is directed to enter an order granting the application.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juvenile sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which granted defendant probation, with the conditions that he participate in the community work program and make restitution. Prior to the dispositional hearing, a burglary allegation against defendant had been found true at a jurisdictional hearing, and defendant's request for a rehearing had been denied.


OVERVIEW: Defendant juvenile was charged with burglary, and the allegation was found to be true beyond a reasonable doubt at a jurisdictional hearing. Defendant's request for a rehearing was denied. Defendant was granted probation at a dispositional hearing and was required to pay restitution. Defendant appealed. The court reversed the order of the trial court that denied defendant's application for a rehearing. The court found that defendant filed a timely notice of appeal because it was filed within 60 days after the date the order after judgment was served. The scope of the court's review included the entire proceeding, which culminated in the order granting probation. The court held that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 252, which required the review of the entire transcript, was violated because the transcript reviewed by the trial judge in ruling on the request for rehearing did not include defense counsel's argument. A referee's presiding at a juvenile jurisdictional hearing did not violate defendant's due process rights as long as there was a procedure available for a bona fide judge to review the referee's determinations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order that denied defendant juvenile's application for rehearing after a burglary allegation was found true. The trial court erroneously denied defendant's request for a rehearing because the transcript, which the trial judge reviewed in ruling on the request, did not include defense counsel's argument. Defendant timely filed a notice of appeal, and the scope review included the whole proceeding.
