San Francisco Fire Fighters v. City and County of San Francisco


Civ. Nos. 36882, 38479


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


68 Cal. App. 3d 896; 137 Cal. Rptr. 607; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1376; 95 L.R.R.M. 2835; 95 L.R.R.M. 3069



April 5, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of San Francisco Fire Fighters for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 2, 1977. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 673085 and 689354, Ira A. Brown, Jr., and Joseph G. Kennedy, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The "Judgment on Award" (described as an "order" in the City's notice of appeal) entered January 15, 1975, in the City's superior court action numbered 673085, is reversed.

The "Judgment" entered September 4, 1975, in the City's superior court action numbered 689354, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff firefighters union sought review of the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which held that a signed memorandum of understanding with defendant city, which provided for arbitration of grievances, was a misguided delegation of authority under defendants' charter.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff firefighters union and defendant city signed a memorandum of understanding, as a binding agreement, which provided for arbitration of grievances concerning terms and conditions of employment. Defendant contended that the authority of the fire commission to prescribe its own reasonable rules and regulations was made exclusive by defendant's charter, and that such power could not be assigned or delegated to another. The court affirmed the order of the trial court and held that the powers conferred upon public agencies and officers which involved the exercise of judgment or discretion were in the nature of public trusts and could not be surrendered or delegated to subordinates in the absence of statutory authorization. The court concluded that neither defendant's mayor, nor its board of supervisors, nor its fire commissioner, had the authority to approve the memorandum's provisions for arbitration of grievances concerning the fire commission's rules and regulations making powers entrusted to him by defendant's charter.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order, which held that the signed memorandum of understanding between plaintiff firefighters union and defendant city, which provided for arbitration, was a misguided delegation of authority under defendant's charter. The court held that defendant could not delegate public officers powers to subordinates because defendant lacked statutory authorization.

 

2036 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Crookham v. Smith


Civ. No. 38763


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


68 Cal. App. 3d 773; 137 Cal. Rptr. 428; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1362



March 31, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 310910, Richard W. Rhodes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) which found that he was not entitled to seek contribution from respondent mother for child support payments to the county as expressly prohibited by Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11350.1.


OVERVIEW: The district attorney brought an action on behalf of the county for child support support of a minor child against appellant father, who then sought contribution from respondent mother. Respondent filed a motion for summary judgment, which the trial court granted. Affirming that decision, the court held that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11350.1 expressly prohibited any cross-complaints for contribution, finding that it did not follow that because § 11350 imposed liability on both parents for support of a deserted child that the one parent could require contribution from the other parent of any support he was required to pay for the child. Although both parents were obligated to the child and to the county for the child's support, they were not obligated to each other. In its discretion, the county decided to bring the support action against appellant alone. It's failure to pursue respondent also did not give appellant a right to contribution from respondent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, finding that appellant father was not entitled to seek contribution from respondent mother for child support payments to the county.

Jara v. Municipal Court for San Antonio Judicial Dist.


Civ. No. 49245


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Four


68 Cal. App. 3d 673; 137 Cal. Rptr. 533; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1355



March 30, 1977 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 26, 1977 (see 21 C.3d 181)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant indigent, defendant in a civil case, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's petition for writ of mandamus to compel the lower court to appoint an interpreter at the expense of real party in interest County of Los Angeles.


OVERVIEW: Appellant indigent, a defendant in a civil case, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandamus to compel a lower court to provide an interpreter at public expense. Respondent found petitioner had made an adequate showing of indigency, but concluded that it did not have the power to provide an interpreter at public expense. On appeal, the court reversed, finding that appellant's due process right of access to the courts was infringed where the courts failed to appoint an interpreter for appellant, given that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 185 required that all judicial proceedings be conducted in English. Real party in interest County of Los Angeles failed to demonstrate a compelling state interest to justify the infringement, as financial savings did not justify an otherwise unconstitutional state action. In a civil action where a defendant was indigent and did not speak or understand English, the due process and equal protection provisions of the federal and state Constitutions required that an interpreter be appointed for such defendant at public expense.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, finding that appellant indigent's due process right of access to the courts was infringed where the courts failed to appoint an interpreter for appellant. Real party in interest County of Los Angeles failed to demonstrate a compelling state interest to justify the infringement, as financial savings did not justify an otherwise unconstitutional state action.

Gonzales v. State of California


Civ. No. 37800


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


68 Cal. App. 3d 621; 137 Cal. Rptr. 681; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1351



March 30, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 26, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 307013, Harry W. Low, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff class sought review of an order by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which dismissed plaintiffs' action, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 581, following the sustaining of defendant state's demurrer to plaintiffs' complaint that alleged defendant was wrongfully withholding plaintiffs' money.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff class filed suit against defendant state on behalf of themselves and others who had or would have convictions for drunk driving declared unconstitutional, and alleged that defendant was wrongfully withholding money received as fines. The trial court dismissed the action following the sustaining of defendant's demurrer to plaintiffs' complaint with leave to amend. The court affirmed on appeal and held that dismissal was proper because the complaint filed by plaintiffs was objectionable on one of the grounds of uncertainty raised by demurrer and plaintiffs' failed to amend. Specifically, the court held that in order for plaintiffs to have stated a cause of action they had to have pled that the prior convictions were vacated or set aside in the court in which the prior conviction was obtained and the sentence imposed. Their failure to clearly set forth how or in what manner the convictions were declared unconstitutional rendered the complaint subject to demurrer for uncertainty. A similar infirmity was found in the failure of the complaint to set forth when the alleged causes of action arose and when the setting aside of the previous convictions occurred.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of dismissal, because the complaint filed by plaintiff class was objectionable on one of the grounds of uncertainty raised by demurrer and plaintiffs failed to amend. Specifically, plaintiffs failed to clearly set forth how or in what manner their convictions were declared constitutionally invalid, when the alleged causes of action arose, and when the setting aside of the previous convictions occurred.

Kennedy v. South Coast Regional Com.


Civ. No. 47682


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


68 Cal. App. 3d 660; 137 Cal. Rptr. 396; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1354



March 30, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. WEC38244, Laurence J. Rittenband, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying the peremptory writ is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, residents of Venice, California, appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their peremptory writ of mandate and held that a permit issued by respondent regional commission to respondent developer was issued validly with proper notice in accordance with Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 27420(b).


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, residents of Venice, California, claimed that respondent regional commission issued a permit to respondent developer that was void because the public notice of the hearing on the application did not comply with the requirements of Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 27420(b). On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment denying the peremptory writ requested by petitioners and upholding the permit issued by respondent regional commission. The notice required by § 27420(b) was merely notice of the nature of the proposed development and not of each and every aspect of the development. The court held that due process did not require any particular notice. The court found that notice of the pending construction of an eight-unit apartment at the site reasonably put any recipient on inquiry as to the likely inclusion of elements which might be inconsistent with the long-range conservation and management of the natural resources of the coastal zone. Any inquiry into the matter would patently have disclosed the facts stated in the application that three lots were involved and that the plan for the development was inconsistent with the continued existence of a tree and dwelling.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment denying the peremptory writ requested by petitioners, residents of Venice, California because due process did not require any specific type of notice. The notice that respondent regional commission gave of the hearing satisfied due process requirements and the permit issued to respondent developer was valid.

Naismith Dental Corp. v. Board of Dental Examiners


Civ. No. 38277


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


68 Cal. App. 3d 253; 137 Cal. Rptr. 133; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1316



March 21, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 20, 1977, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 19, 1977. Bird, C. J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 461237-3, Spurgeon Avakian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant dentist sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied his petition for writ of mandate against respondent Board of Dental Examiners after respondent refused to grant him permission to open a third dental office pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 1658.1. Appellant claimed the statute denied him due process and equal protection.


OVERVIEW: Appellant dentist was refused permission to open a third dental office by respondent Board of Dental Examiners, because such action would violate Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 1658.1, which required that the dentist spend at least 50 percent of his time at each office. Appellant sought a writ of mandate against respondent from the trial court, claiming § 1658.1 violated his due process and equal protection rights, but his petition was denied. The court affirmed the trial court's denial of appellant's writ of mandate. The court held that § 1658.1 did not violate appellant's due process rights, because it was not arbitrary and had a reasonable relationship to a legitimate state purpose, i.e., maintaining a close relationship between the dentist and his employees and patients. The court also held that § 1658.1 did not deny appellant equal protection of the laws. The court applied the "rational relationship" test instead of the "close scrutiny" test, because the classification was neither suspect nor touched on fundamental interests. Under such standard, § 1658.1 bore a rational relationship to a conceivable state purpose.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment denying appellant dentist's petition for writ of mandate on due process or equal protection grounds. The court held that there was a public purpose in the statute, and the statute bore a rational relationship to a conceivable legitimate state purpose. The court also dismissed appellant's argument that the law was unwise, holding that it was not the court's province to make such determination.

Wilson v. County of Santa Clara


Civ. No. 38283


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


68 Cal. App. 3d 78; 137 Cal. Rptr. 78; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1300



March 15, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 317634, John T. Racanelli, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the case is remanded to the trial court with directions to overrule the defendants' demurrer and allow the defendants a reasonable time within which to file an amended return on the merits.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff deputy sheriff appealed a decision from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) which dismissed plaintiff's petition for writ of mandate commanding defendants, county, sheriff, board of supervisors members, and county executive, to approve plaintiff's request for all overtime spent in off-duty hours meeting the training requirements mandated by Cal. Health & Safety Code § 217 (1972) and to pay plaintiff for such overtime.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff deputy sheriff sought a writ of mandate commanding defendants, a county, its sheriff, members of its board of supervisors, and its county executive, to approve plaintiff's request for overtime spent in off-duty hours meeting the training requirements mandated in Cal. Health & Safety Code § 217 (1972) and to pay plaintiff for such overtime. Defendants demurred on the ground that plaintiff failed to state a cause of action. The trial court sustained the demurrer. The court reversed, finding that although § 217 was neutral on the issue of whether a trainee should be compensated for the training, general contract principles demonstrated that plaintiff had stated a cause of action for overtime pay or for compensating time off. The court also noted that the provisions of the Fair Labor Standards Act, 29 U.S.C.S. § 201 et seq., controlled plaintiff's right to compensation in the absence of any provision in the contract of employment to the contrary because from the allegations of the complaint it appeared that attendance at the training sessions was outside of regular working hours, that the attendance was involuntary, and that the training was directly related to the job.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment. The court held that plaintiff deputy sheriff stated a cause of action under general contract principles for overtime pay or for compensating time off for off-duty hours spent meeting certain mandated training requirements of plaintiff's employment. The court noted that the principles of Fair Labor Standards Act controlled plaintiff's right to compensation in the absence of a contract to the contrary.

Coffineau v. Fong Eu


Civ. No. 48310


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


68 Cal. App. 3d 138; 137 Cal. Rptr. 90; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1306



March 15, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 6, 1977, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. The petition of the plaintiffs and appellants for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 12, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C133540, Jerry Pacht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to the superior court to enter judgment denying a peremptory writ of mandate. Appellants and cross-respondents shall recover their costs on appeal as against respondents and cross-appellants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The parties sought review from a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held that Cal. Gov't Code § 34507 was unconstitutional, and issued a peremptory writ of mandate commanding that defendants should not refrain from accepting a petition to cause an election regarding city's name change to be placed on the ballot, which required only a simple majority to be determinative.


OVERVIEW: The trial court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing that defendants were to accept plaintiffs' petition to cause an election regarding the name change of the city to be placed on the ballot requiring only a simple majority to be determinative. The trial court held that Cal. Gov't Code § 34507 was unconstitutional because it denied general law cities and their electors equal protection of the laws. On appeal, the court stated that in a chartered city, the change of name of a city was manifestly a municipal affair and the matter was in the hands of the residents of the home rule city. However, in the case of a general law city, the determination of the requirements as to a change of name was within the province of the legislature. Thus, the court held that the legislature's determination that a two-thirds favorable vote was required was not unreasonable. The legislature had broad discretion in determining the extent of the vote to be required to effect such a change. The court held that Cal. Gov't Code § 34507 was not arbitrary and that plaintiffs were not entitled to the relief they sought. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court with directions to the trial court to enter judgment denying a peremptory writ of mandate. The legislature's determination that a two-thirds favorable vote was required to effect the name change of a general law city was not unreasonable.

 

2045 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Atlantic Richfield Co. v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 48117


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


68 Cal. App. 3d 105; 137 Cal. Rptr. 84; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1303; 57 Oil & Gas Rep. 117



March 15, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 13, 1977, and the petition of all the respondents for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 2, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C933174, 933175, 954872 and 954873, William H. Levit, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which issued judgment for defendants in plaintiffs' suit for a refund of taxes paid on oil and gas interests after the trial court concluded that Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 107.2. and 107.3 were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 107.2 and 107.3 declared that any result of litigation eliminating a taxpayer's right to exclude royalties in valuing possessory interests in oil and gas leases would be prospective in operation. The new method of valuation would be applicable only to leases entered into after the tax year in which the litigation began. The trial court found §§ 107.2 and 107.3 unconstitutional because they were unnecessary to alleviate hardship and violated the full value assessment requirement. The court reversed and remanded the judgment for defendants in plaintiffs' action for a refund of taxes paid on oil and gas interests. The trial court was required to analyze the enactments according to their reasonableness in light of the situation presented to the legislature, but failed to do so. The legislature acted reasonably in providing that the changed method of valuation of possessory interests in oil and gas leases was prospective only. The legislature's action was permissible in the context of the case before the court. Once the legislature concluded that a hardship might result by a court decision it could go ahead and pass its prospective legislation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the judgment for defendants in plaintiffs' action for a refund of taxes paid on oil and gas interests because the trial court erred in concluding that statutes that made elimination of a taxpayer's right to exclude royalties in valuing possessory interests in oil and gas interests prospective in operation were unconstitutional. The statutes were reasonable to alleviate potential hardships to such taxpayers.

 

2046 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Dept. of Transporttion v. Desert Outdoor Advertising, Inc.


Civ. No. 14514


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


68 Cal. App. 3d 440; 137 Cal. Rptr. 221; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1334



March 14, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified March 25, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 333184, Robert W. Conyers and Louis M. Welsh, Judges.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant advertising corporation appealed the grant of summary judgment in favor of plaintiff California Department of Transportation from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), regarding two highway displays. Plaintiff also appealed from the trial court's judgment which dismissed its action as to two other displays.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff California Department of Transportation filed a suit against defendant advertising corporation seeking an order that defendant remove four advertising displays, pursuant to the Outdoor Advertising Act (Act), Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 5200 et seq. The trial court on summary judgment ordered that two displays be removed. However, it dismissed plaintiff's action at trial as to the others and declared Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 5412 unconstitutional. Both parties appealed. The court of appeal reversed summary judgment. There were factual issues in dispute as to whether the signs were in a business area, thus creating an exception pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 5407, 5408, that they be removed pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 5405. The court also reversed the finding as to the remaining signs and found that § 5412, the compensation section of the Act, did not deny equal protection. The forced removal of signs that violated the Act was within the state's police power to eliminate a nuisance, which did not constitute the unauthorized taking of property. The Act had the rational purpose of maintaining federal highway funds.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment in favor of Plaintiff California Department of Transportation ordering the removal of two advertising displays reversed due to a genuine material dispute as to their location. Judgment in favor of defendant advertising corporation on the remaining two displays reversed as well. Plaintiff had the authority to remove some signs without compensation under its police powers to eliminate a nuisance.

 

2047 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Jacobs v. State Bar of California


Civ. No. 38476


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Two


67 Cal. App. 3d 972; 136 Cal. Rptr. 920; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1290



March 10, 1977 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 26, 1977 (see 20 C.3d 191)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney sought review of a decision by the San Francisco Superior Court (California), which denied appellant's request that respondent state bar be enjoined from investigating into appellant's private affairs, absent a showing of probable cause demonstrating that such disclosure is relevant to an investigation properly being conducted by respondent.


OVERVIEW: Appellant attorney filed a complaint and an affidavit and points and authorities in support of the issuance of a temporary restraining order and preliminary injunction against a professional misconduct investigation by respondent state bar. Appellant alleged in the complaint that respondent's investigation of appellant constituted an abuse of process and an invasion of appellant's privacy. The lower court denied appellant relief. On review, the court affirmed the lower court's judgment. In reaching its decision, the court held that the lower court had jurisdiction to consider appellant's application for a preliminary injunction. Moreover, the court held that the lower court did not abuse its discretion in denying the injunction. The court explained it was inherent in appellant's acceptance of his right to practice law that when charges were made against him that he must submit to having those charges investigated even if to some extent what would be a right to privacy in one not an attorney is violated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that appellant attorney was not entitled to an injunction against respondent state bar's investigation of appellant's private affairs. The court held that respondent's investigation did not constitute an abuse of process or an invasion of privacy. Lastly, the court concluded that the lower court's decision to deny appellant injunctive relief was not an abuse of discretion.

Rice v. Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board


Civ. No. 40507Civ. No. 40523


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Four


67 Cal. App. 3d 985; 137 Cal. Rptr. 213; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1291



March 10, 1977 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 16, 1977 (see 21 C.3d 431)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, in their respective capacities as the director of the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control and a wholesale distributor of alcoholic beverages, filed petitions for writs of review of a decision by respondent, Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board, which found that the retail price maintenance provisions of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 23000, et seq., were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Respondent, Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board, found that the retail price maintenance provisions were unconstitutional in view of the recent amendment of the Sherman Antitrust Act and that the provisions violated the equal protection clauses of the state and federal constitutions. In so holding, respondent reversed a disciplinary order of the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control, which suspended a license. Petitioners, in their respective capacities as the director of the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control and a wholesale distributor of alcoholic beverages, filed petitions for writs of review. The court reversed respondent's decision and held that the retail price fixing required by Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 24750 and 24755 did not violate the Sherman Act or the equal protection clauses of the federal and state constitution. The court found that petitioner, wholesale distributor of alcoholic beverages, had no standing to file a petition for writ of review, because it was not a party to the underlying action.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the decision by respondent, Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board, which held that the retail price maintenance provisions of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Act, were unconstitutional, and affirmed the decision of the Department of Alcoholic Beverage in the underlying case. The court held that petitioner, wholesale distributor of alcoholic beverages, lacked standing to request review.

 

2049 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Robinson v. Varela


Civ. Nos. 48891, 48953


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


67 Cal. App. 3d 611; 136 Cal. Rptr. 783; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1257



February 28, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 19, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. NWC 48233, NWC 48694, Philip F. Jones, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders of the superior court vacating the default judgment and dissolving the writ of attachment are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tenant challenged the orders of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that vacated a default judgment and dissolved a writ of attachment that he had obtained in his actions against defendant subtenant arising out of their subtenancy agreement. Plaintiff contended that the trial court abused its discretion when it set aside the default and that it did not have the power to dissolve the writ of attachment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff tenant filed an unlawful detainer action and a separate rent action against defendant subtenant arising out of defendant's agreement to sublet and make improvements on the property, provided that plaintiff would obtain a 10-year extension of the underlying lease. Plaintiff obtained a default judgment in the unlawful detainer action and a writ of attachment in the rent action, both of which were vacated upon reconsideration by the trial court. Plaintiff appealed and the court affirmed. The trial court did not abuse its broad discretion under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 473 when it set aside the default as there was evidence that defense counsel's failure to file a timely answer was excusable. Further, defendant provided a meritorious defense by denying most of the complaint's allegations and by raising issues concerning the validity of the sublease and the failure of plaintiff to obtain the required extension of the underlying lease. These issues also supported the trial court's dissolution of the writ of attachment under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 538.4 because defendant had a reasonable probability of asserting a successful defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order vacating the default judgment in the unlawful detainer action and the dissolution of the writ of attachment because the trial court did not abuse its discretion in making these rulings due to defendant's reasonable probability of asserting a successful defense.

In re George G.


Civ. No. 48511


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


68 Cal. App. 3d 146; 137 Cal. Rptr. 201; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1307



February 25, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified March 15, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A9316, Lester E. Olson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment appealed from is reversed and remanded for further proceedings not inconsistent with the opinion expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) terminating their parental rights on the ground of voluntary abandonment and awarding custody to respondent department of adoptions.


OVERVIEW: Appellants parents' rights to their two minor children were terminated for voluntary abandonment, and custody was awarded to respondent department of adoptions. Appellants sought review, and the court reversed, finding that appellants were denied due process and that there was no voluntary abandonment. The trial court's ruling denying appellants access to the source of information in a probation report, which was the sole evidence of abandonment of one of the children, deprived them of the right to adequate cross-examination. This due process violation was not harmless and required reversal as to one child. As to the other child, appellants did not voluntarily relinquish custody of the baby, who was left in the hospital for observation for detoxification symptoms. The involuntary taking of the baby and later actions by the parties precluded a determination that appellants abandoned their baby.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the judgment terminating appellants' parental rights because appellants were denied due process, and the record did not support a claim of abandonment.

San Diego Unified Port Dist. v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 16142


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


67 Cal. App. 3d 361; 136 Cal. Rptr. 557; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1231; 7 ELR 20668



February 18, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 15, 1977, and the petitions of the Port District and the real parties in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied April 28, 1977. Mosk, J., and Clark, J., were of the opinion that the petitions should be granted. The United States Supreme Court denied a petition for writ of certiorari on October 3, 1977.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged, and the petition for a peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant port district, operator of an airport, requested a peremptory writ, seeking to overturn of an order of the San Diego Superior Court (California), which overruled a general demurrer to causes of action brought by plaintiff homeowners in nuisance, negligence, trespass, and failure to obtain a state operating permit.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff homeowners brought related actions against defendant port district, the operator of an airport, seeking damages for nuisance, negligence, trespass, and failure to obtain a state operating permit. The trial court overruled defendant's general demurrer. Defendant requested a peremptory writ, seeking to overturn the trial court's ruling on the demurrer. The court denied the petition for a peremptory writ, holding that the general demurrer at issue was properly overruled. The court found that because the allegations in the complaint sought recovery for harm caused by proprietary actions and omissions as well as for harm caused by aircraft in flight, they were broad enough to state a cause of action. The court limited recovery, however, for tort damages, if any, that arose out of the operation of the airport itself and not those caused by aircraft in flight. The court said that to authorize recovery from the proprietor of an airport for injuries caused by aircraft in flight would permit local liability for conduct within exclusive federal control. The court noted that federal control over aircraft in flight was intensive and exclusive.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant port authority's petition for a peremptory writ. The court held that the trial court properly overruled defendant's general demurrer to causes of action brought by plaintiff homeowners in nuisance, negligence, trespass, and failure to obtain a state operating permit because the allegations were broad enough to state causes of action.

 

2052 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Peerless Stages, Inc. v. Santa Cruz Met. Transit Dist.


Civ. No. 39198


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


67 Cal. App. 3d 343; 136 Cal. Rptr. 567; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1229



February 18, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 14, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 56265, Harry F. Brauer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant common carrier sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which granted respondent transit district's demurrer on its action to recover damages for inverse condemnation for losses it had sustained by reason of competition from a bus system operated by respondent.


OVERVIEW: Appellant common carrier operated a passenger bus service between San Jose and Santa Cruz. Respondent transit district began operating a bus route in direct competition with appellant's route. Appellant alleged that it had a property right in its route pursuant to Cal. Const. art. I, § 19, and Cal. Const. art. XII, § 5, which entitled it to compensation for its losses. Appellant did not meet the definition of an existing system under Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 98300 that respondent must offer to buy if competing for patronage, but appellant alleged that the definition was arbitrary and invalid. The trial court granted respondent's demurrer. The court affirmed and found that freedom from competition was not a compensable property right under Cal. Const. art. I, § 19 and Cal. Const. art. XII, § 5 did not bestow any rights of compensation on appellant. The court concluded that appellant was not deprived of equal protection by Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 98300 because the legislature provided different rules for different transit districts. Each district applied only to a given area and each act was approved by the voters of the area and applied uniformly to the area in question.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that granted respondent transit district's demurrer. The court found that appellant common carrier did not have a compensable property right in its bus route under the California Constitution and that the statute that governed the transit district did not deprive appellant of equal protection of the law.

United Stanford Employees v. Board of Trustees


Civ. No. 38618


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


67 Cal. App. 3d 319; 136 Cal. Rptr. 553; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1227



February 17, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 18, 1977, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 14, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. P-28878, William A. Ingram, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, university employees union and others, challenged the order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which dismissed their class action challenging the imposition of parking fees by respondent university under a plan adopted pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 21113.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, university employees union and others, filed a class action challenging the imposition of parking fees by respondent university under a plan adopted pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 21113. The trial court sustained respondent's general demurrer without leave to amend and dismissed. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that the parking registration fee was not a tax, The court stated that nonpayment of the perfectly legitimate contractual registration fee was an infraction. The court next held that § 21113 was broad enough to include a fee as a condition, which when violated constituted an infraction. Section 21113 was not a regulation. Last, the court held that the enforcement of the prohibitions of § 21113 by process of law was a reasonable and orderly sanction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court dismissing appellant university employees union's class action suit because the statute authorizing the imposition of parking fees by appellant was not a regulation or a tax, but instead a perfectly legitimate contractual registration fee, the nonpayment of which was an infraction.

Shuffer v. Board of Trustees


Civ. No. 48959


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


67 Cal. App. 3d 208; 136 Cal. Rptr. 527; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1219



February 16, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NW C 41525, Raymond R. Roberts, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment appealed from is reversed. The trial court is directed (1) to consolidate all pending matters involving the subject matter of this dispute and (2) to conduct an evidentiary hearing, at which time petitioner will have an opportunity to present his proof.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner graduate student challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed his writ of mandate petition to compel respondent Trustees of the California State University and Colleges and university officials to grant him a master of arts degree in guidance from one of the state universities. The trial court sustained respondents' demurrer and dismissed the petition when petitioner failed to amend.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner graduate student was an approved masters candidate at a state university controlled by respondent Trustees of the California State University and Colleges and university officials. He claimed that respondents arbitrarily required his attendance at a course section different than his enrolled section. He petitioned for mandate to compel acceptance in his enrolled course. A demurrer was sustained and petitioner's request for a dismissal without prejudice was granted. He was given an incomplete grade and did not receive his degree. Petitioner then sued for an injunction claiming civil rights violations. The action was still pending when petitioner requested a writ of mandate to compel respondents to grant him a masters degree. A demurrer was sustained and the petition was dismissed for failure to amend. The court reversed and ordered all pending actions between the parties to be consolidated. Petitioner's allegations of discrimination and lack of equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV stated a cause of action. The trial court was directed to conduct an evidentiary hearing on petitioner's claims. The doctrine of res judicata did not bar petitioner's action.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and directed the trial court to consolidate all pending actions involving the dispute between petitioner graduate student and respondent Trustees of the California State University and Colleges and university officials and to hold an evidentiary hearing. Petitioner's relief was not barred by res judicata. Petitioner stated a cause of action for discrimination and lack of equal protection under the Fourteenth Amendment.

Gonzales v. State


Civ. No. 37800


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION One


67 Cal. App. 3d 139; 136 Cal. Rptr. 487; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1212



February 15, 1977 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted Mar. 16, 1977 (see 68 C.A.3d 621)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant drivers sought review of an order of the trial court (California), which granted their ex parte motion to dismiss their action against respondent state, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code 581, following the sustaining of respondent's demurrer to appellants' amended complaint with leave to amend.


OVERVIEW: Appellant drivers challenged the trial court's order that granted their ex parte motion to dismiss their action pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 581, after respondent state's demurrer to appellants' amended complaint was sustained with leave to amend, and appellants failed to take further action on the complaint. The court affirmed and found that the trial court sustained respondent's demurrer on one ground of appellant's original complaint and did not overrule respondent's demurrer on the other grounds of the original complaint and respondent was not estopped from relitigating the same grounds in the amended complaint. The court held that it affirmed the trial court's ruling on demurrer if there was any ground raised by respondent that required demurrer. The court found that where drunk driving convictions were declared unconstitutional because of a collateral attack to avoid an enhanced punishment, the conviction still stood with no obligation to return the fines. The court noted that appellant's complaint did not detail if the convictions were declared unconstitutional due to direct or collateral attack and respondent's demurrer on the ground of uncertainty was sustained.


OUTCOME: An order that granted an ex parte motion to dismiss was affirmed because respondent state was not estopped from relitigating issues in an amended complaint, because the court had to sustain the trial court's sustaining of a demurrer if there was one valid ground that required a demurrer, and because successful collateral attacks on the constitutionality of drunk driving convictions did not result in the return of fines.

 

2056 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Metromedia, Inc. v. San Diego


Civ. No. 14591


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION One


67 Cal. App. 3d 84; 136 Cal. Rptr. 453; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1207



February 11, 1977 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 19, 1977 (see 23 C.3d 762)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the grant of summary judgment in favor of appellee companies regarding the constitutionality of a city ordinance that banned billboards.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city argued that the trial court erred when it determined that a city ordinance was unconstitutional and enjoined the enforcement of the ordinance. The city ordinance in question prohibited the erection and maintenance of all off-premise advertising displays within the entire limits the city. Appellee companies were engaged in the outdoor advertising business. The court noted that the legislative body of a city had broad discretion to determine what was reasonable in endeavoring to protect the health, safety, morals and general welfare of its citizens, but that the ordinances it enacted to accomplish these aims must have been reasonable in object and not arbitrary in operation. The court stated that the enforcement of the ordinance would require the removal of hundreds of signs having a conceded value in the millions of dollars and bring about a substantial private loss. The court also stated that the ordinance also conflicted with Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 5226 which permitted such signs within designated areas. Affirmed.


OUTCOME: Trial court's grant of summary judgment in favor of appellee companies and enjoined the enforcement of a city ordinance was affirmed. The ordinance which prohibited all billboards was too broad to have been within appellant city's police powers.

 

2057 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Garcia


Crim. No. 15661


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


67 Cal. App. 3d 60; 136 Cal. Rptr. 461; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1204



February 11, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 8, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 71517, E. Warren McGuire, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the writ of habeas corpus is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which granted defendant's petition for a writ of habeas corpus. Defendant argued that because the evidence was the same for his arrest as for his codefendant's, and because the arrest had been determined to be invalid when the codefendant pursued his appeal, defendant's guilty pleas had to be set aside.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to one count of burglary and the people dismissed three counts of forgery pursuant to a plea bargain. Defendant never filed a motion to suppress evidence pertaining to his arrest. Charged with the same four counts, his codefendant filed a motion to suppress, which was denied. Codefendant was convicted on all counts and was sentenced to prison. Codefendant's convictions were reversed because the reviewing court determined that the arrest of the two men was without probable cause; codefendant's motion to suppress should have been granted. Defendant then sought a writ of habeas corpus, which the trial court granted because of the ruling in the codefendant's case. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that because defendant had had adequate opportunity to challenge the validity of his arrest in the trial court on a direct appeal, but had chosen not to do so, habeas corpus was inappropriately granted. The court held that habeas corpus may have been applicable had defendant's guilt been in question, but defendant freely admitted his guilt, making it inapplicable.


OUTCOME: The order granting defendant's petition for a writ of habeas corpus was reversed because defendant failed to seize the opportunity to challenge his arrest as the "fruit of the poisonous tree" at some stage prior to his conviction. Having failed to do so, habeas corpus was not an appropriate remedy available to him.

Kinnamon v. Staitman & Snyder


Civ. No. 49426


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


66 Cal. App. 3d 893; 136 Cal. Rptr. 321; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1188



February 8, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 7, 1977.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed, after appellee attorneys' general demurrer was sustained, a complaint filed against appellees for intentional infliction of emotional distress, which had alleged that appellees, in violation of Cal. Bar. R., Prof. Conduct R. 7-104, had threatened appellant with criminal prosecution to obtain advantage in a civil case.


OVERVIEW: Appellee attorneys were retained by a client to collect a check given by appellant that had been dishonored for insufficient funds. Appellees sent a letter to appellant that allegedly threatened appellant with criminal prosecution in order to secure payment of the obligation represented by the check. Appellant filed a complaint against appellees that alleged that appellee attorneys, in violation of Cal. Bar. R., Prof. Conduct R. 7-104, had threatened appellant with criminal prosecution to obtain advantage in a civil case, that the threat was made with the intent of inflicting emotional distress, and that severe emotional distress resulted. OUTCOME: The judgment of the court below, which dismissed appellant's intentional infliction of emotional distress action against appellees, was reversed. The court found that appellant's complaint had sufficiently stated a cause of action for intentional infliction of emotion distress where appellees were alleged to have threatened appellant with criminal prosecution to obtain an advantage in a civil case.

 

2059 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Pacific Gas & Electric Co. v. Mounteer


Civ. No. 40359


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


66 Cal. App. 3d 809; 136 Cal. Rptr. 280; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1179



February 7, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 7, 1977. Tobriner, J., and Sullivan, J., + did not participate therein.


+ Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Delta Judicial District of Contra Costa County, No. 2387, Manuel C. Rose, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, driver and passenger, challenged a judgment from the Municipal Court for the Delta Judicial District of Contra Costa County (California), in an action for negligence, where the trial court had awarded respondent public utility an award for the destruction of their utility pole by appellants.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, driver and his passenger, struck a roadside utility pole with their automobile. Replacement of the pole was required. Appellants admitted liability but contested the damage claim, asserting the right to credit for depreciation of the pole. The trial court awarded respondent damages in the amount claimed by respondent. The appellate department of Contra Costa County affirmed, and certified the case to secure uniformity of decision in an area of the law in which many cases, each for moderate amount, aggregated to a substantial sum each year. Appellants contended that depreciation should have been deducted from the value of the pole in computing damages. The court affirmed, and held that a single pole had little or no ascertainable commercial value or fixed service life as an individual entity. Its real value lay in its use with many other such poles in sustaining an entire integrated transmission system. The legislature, in adopting Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 7952 had recognized these problems and established the cost of repair, without depreciation allowance, as the measure of damages for negligent injury to or destruction of individual poles of a transmission line.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment from the appellate department, affirming the judgment from the trial court awarding full damages to respondent utility company for replacement of their utility pole damaged by appellants, driver and his passenger. The legislature had provided recovery without depreciation in such an action as the measure of damages for negligent injury to or destruction of individual poles of a transmission line.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant disabled person sought review from a decision by the trial court (California), which found him to be a gravely disabled person pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §5350. Appellant claimed that the trial court erred when it refused to instruct the jury that it had to find him gravely disabled by a unanimous verdict.

Goleta Educators Assn. v. Dall'Armi


Civ. No. 48919


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


68 Cal. App. 3d 830; 137 Cal. Rptr. 324; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1369



January 26, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 24, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 109961, John T. Rickard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court is reversed and remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee organization challenged the order of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which denied its petition for a peremptory writ of mandate to compel respondent board to fix a retroactive salary increase and order it to be paid.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee organization proposed a 12 percent cost of living salary increase for the 1975-1976 school year. Respondent board countered with a proposal to retain the prior year's salary schedule. A panel convened for fact finding. Respondent then approved a six percent salary increase effective back to the beginning of the school year. In the meantime, the employees were paid based on the prior year's salary schedule. Appellant petitioned for a writ of mandate to compel respondent to fix a retroactive salary increase and order it to be paid. The trial court denied the petition because it found the increase would constitute an unconstitutional grant of extra compensation for services already rendered in violation of the Cal. Const. art. IV, § 17, and Cal. Const. art. XI, § 10. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded because it found that the salaries of appellant's members were not determined while negotiations were ongoing, and the retroactive salary increase was not unconstitutional or extra compensation for services already rendered by appellant's members.


OUTCOME: The denial of appellant employee organization's petition for a peremptory writ of mandate to compel respondent board to order a retroactive pay increase was reversed and remanded because the salaries of appellant's members were undetermined while negotiations were ongoing, and the retroactive salary increase did not constitute unconstitutional extra compensation for services already rendered.

 

2063 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Queen of Angels Hospital v. Younger


Civ. No. 44551


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


66 Cal. App. 3d 359; 136 Cal. Rptr. 36; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1133



January 25, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 23, 1977, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 6823, James D. Tante, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, non-profit hospital and religious order, and defendant attorney general appealed an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which, in an action filed by plaintiffs for declaratory relief, found valid plaintiff non-profit hospital's lease of its building to a third party and payment of a finder's fee but found invalid plaintiff non-profit hospital's payment of a pension to plaintiff religious order.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff non-profit hospital held its assets in trust for the purpose of operating a hospital. It then leased its building to a third party, and with the rent proceeds, planned to establish outpatient clinics. In compromise of a monetary claim for past services, plaintiff agreed to pay a pension to the employees of plaintiff religious order. Plaintiffs then filed a declaratory relief action against defendant attorney general, regarding the validity of the lease and pension agreements. The trial court found the lease valid, as well as an agreement to pay a finder's fee to an attorney, but found the pension agreement invalid. Both parties appealed. The appellate court reversed, holding that under the articles of incorporation, plaintiff non-profit hospital's purpose was to operate a hospital, not outpatient clinics. Although plaintiff non-profit hospital could use some of the rental profits to run outpatient clinics, it could not cease operation of a hospital. Because the attorney was not required to have a broker's license, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 10133(c), payment of a finder's fee was valid. The pension agreement, based on a claim for past services, was invalid.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's order that validated plaintiff non-profit hospital's lease of its building to a third party, in a declaratory action filed by plaintiffs, non-profit hospital and religious order, against defendant attorney general, regarding its lease, finder's fee, and pension agreement because plaintiff non-profit hospital was bound by its articles of incorporation to operate a hospital.

 

2064 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Lucas v. County of Monterey


Civ. No. 39389


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


65 Cal. App. 3d 947; 135 Cal. Rptr. 707; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1103



January 17, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied March 17, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 70070, William L. Stewart, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying appellant's claim for refund of taxes paid under protest is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant possessory interest holder challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which denied recovery of taxes paid under protest to respondent county pursuant to Cal. Tax. & Rev. Code § 5136.


OVERVIEW: Appellant possessory interest holder paid a tax under protest to respondent county for a possessory interest in a harbor berth and challenged the lower court's judgment that denied a claim for refund. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment and held that appellant had a valuable possessory interest which was defined as property under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 104. The court found that U.S. Const. art. XIII, § 1 and Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 107 required that it be taxed. The court held that appellant's reliance on Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 107.4 that purportedly excluded from the definition of possessory interest any berth, wharf, etc., when such specific facility was also used intermittently by others, even though such possession, claim, or right to use such facility was paramount to any use by others, was misplaced because the effect of this legislation was to reclassify berths or wharves containing the noted characteristics, so that they were no longer considered property and were therefore tax-exempt. The court held that such an effect ran counter to the mandate of U.S. Const. art. XIII, § 1, that all property not specifically excluded in the Constitution, was to be taxed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment denying appellant possessory interest holder's claim for refund of taxes paid under protest to respondent county. The court held that appellant had a valuable possessory interest in a harbor berth, which was defined as property and required to be taxed by the U.S. Constitution.

 

2065 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Chambers v. Municipal Court


Civ. Nos. 36891, 38697


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


65 Cal. App. 3d 904; 135 Cal. Rptr. 695; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1099



January 14, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of the Plaintiffs and Respondents for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied March 9, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, Nos. 458513-8, 472080-5, George W. Phillips, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: State appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which issued writs of prohibition restraining the municipal court from entertaining criminal proceedings against defendants for allegedly refusing to disperse in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 416.


OVERVIEW: State appealed from the trial court's issuance of writs of prohibition restraining the municipal court from entertaining criminal proceedings against defendants for allegedly refusing to disperse as required under Cal. Penal Code § 416. The superior court issued writs after defendants had demurred to the face of the criminal complaints against them. On appeal, the court construed the language in § 416 to indicate that proof of intention to commit overt acts that are themselves violent or that tend to incite others to violence was requisite to criminal liability under the first part of the statute. The court found that the unlawful act provision in § 416 must be interpreted as reaching conduct that the state may legitimately suppress. Further the court held that § 416 empowered a public official to demand dispersal only where there was probable cause to believe that the purpose of an assembly was unlawful, according to the facts and circumstances of each individual case. The court reversed the superior court's judgments.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's judgments to issue a writ of prohibition because a public official was empowered to demand dispersal when there was probable cause to believe that the purpose of an assembly was unlawful.

Isbell v. County of Sonoma


Civ. No. 38824


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION One


65 Cal. App. 3d 764; 135 Cal. Rptr. 516; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1085



January 7, 1977 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Mar. 9, 1977 (see 21 C.3d 61)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, laypersons who signed confessions of judgment, sought review of a judgment of the Sonoma County (California) Superior Court that held that the California confession of judgment procedure was not, on its face, unconstitutional and void as a denial of due process and that judgments obtained in conformance with Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1132 et seq. were not per se invalid.


OVERVIEW: When plaintiffs were found to have obtained public benefits to which they were not entitled, they signed confessions of judgment. Defendant filed the abstracts of judgment with the county recorder. One such abstract attached as a lien when one plaintiff bought a house and the county attempted collection of the other judgment. Plaintiffs brought suit for declaratory relief challenging the statutory procedure for confessions of judgment in Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 1132 through 1135, alleging that the procedure failed to comply with the Due Process Clause of the U.S. Const. amend. XIV and the Cal. Const. art. I, § 13. The court held that the procedure was not, on its face, unconstitutional and void as a denial of due process, and that judgments obtained in conformance with §§ 1132 through 1135 were not per se invalid. On appeal, the court agreed with the trial court that the procedure was not unconstitutional but modified the judgment to provide that the stipulated facts were insufficient to permit a determination that the plaintiffs did, or did not, voluntarily, intelligently, and knowingly waive their procedural due process rights. The court affirmed the judgment as modified.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment to provide that the stipulated facts were insufficient to permit a determination of whether the plaintiffs voluntarily, intelligently, and knowingly waived their procedural due process rights. As modified, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment that the confession of judgment procedure was not unconstitutional.

Scott v. Family Ministries


Civ. No. 47576


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


65 Cal. App. 3d 492; 135 Cal. Rptr. 430; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2231



December 30, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a Rehearing were Denied January 27, 1977, and the Petitions of All the Appellants for a Hearing by the Supreme Court were Denied February 23, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C 121839 and AD 88745, Lester E. Olson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Each party to bear their own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent religious organization sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment in favor of appellant prospective adoptive parents in their action seeking injunctive relief restraining respondent from placing certain Asian children for adoption under respondent's religious guidelines and respondent's relinquishment of custody of the children.


OVERVIEW: Appellant prospective adoptive parents tried to adopt a refugee child from Cambodia through respondent religious organization. Respondent, who was a state licensed private adoption agency, refused to approve appellants' application for adoption because appellants were not members of the evangelical Protestant church. Appellants filed an action for injunctive relief, and the trial court entered judgment in favor of appellants on its claims for injunctive relief, restraining respondents from placing the children for adoption and ordering the relinquishment of custody by respondent but refused to award appellants attorneys fees. Both parties appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court stated that the activity of private-licensed adoption agencies constituted state action. Accordingly, the court concluded that Cal. Admin. Code tit. 22, § 30643, in order to be given constitutional construction, had to be interpreted to preclude imposition of religious requirements upon prospective adoptive parents beyond the religious matching provisions of the Cal. Admin. Code tit. 22, § 30643. Further, the court refused attorneys fees to appellants given the lack of authority for the award.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment for appellant prospective adoptive parents, holding that activity of private-licensed adoption agencies constituted state action. Thus, in order pass constitutional muster, state statutes had to be interpreted to preclude imposition of respondent religious organization's religious requirements upon prospective adoptive parents to the extent those requirements exceeded statutory religious matching provisions.

Renba Lil v. Kortz


Civ. No. 47846


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


65 Cal. App. 3d 467; 135 Cal. Rptr. 287; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2228



December 29, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-145425, Norman R. Dowds, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting a preliminary injunction is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, chief of police and related persons, sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), granting plaintiff corporation a temporary restraining order and a preliminary injunction against enforcement of Long Beach Municipal Code § 4195 et seq. relating to nudity of waiters, waitresses, and entertainers in establishments which serve food or beverages.


OVERVIEW: Defendant chief of police and related persons sought review of the trial court order granting plaintiff corporation a temporary restraining order and a preliminary injunction against enforcement of Long Beach Municipal Code § 4195 et seq. (municipal code) relating to nudity of waiters, waitresses, and entertainers in establishments which served food or beverages. The court found that the specific definition of "theater" contained in the municipal code invaded the area reserved to the state in Cal. Penal Code § 318.5 and Cal. Penal Code § 318.6 and was therefore invalid. The court found that the municipal code definition imposed additional requirements and was in conflict with state law, but that the definition was easily severable from the rest of the municipal code section in question. The remainder of the municipal code was thus enforceable, and therefore the trial court had erred in restraining enforcement of the entire municipal code section. Thus, the court reversed the trial court order granting the preliminary injunction, but remanded because plaintiff's claim that its establishment was a theater was not determined below.


OUTCOME: The order granting a preliminary injunction against enforcement of the municipal ordinance relating to nudity in establishments which served food or beverages was reversed, on grounds that the trial court erred in restraining enforcement of the entire statute when only the definition of theaters was invalid. The case was remanded to adjudicate plaintiff corporation's claim that its establishment constituted a theater exempt from the ordinance.

Henderson v. Mann Theatres Corp.


Civ. No. 48810


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


65 Cal. App. 3d 397; 135 Cal. Rptr. 266; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2221



December 28, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied February 23, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 113808, William P. Hogoboom, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff lessors sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), holding that a lease clause providing for payment of rent in gold coin was prohibited by the Joint Resolution of 1933, 31 U.S.C.S. § 463, that the joint resolution was not repealed by the Par Value Modification Act, Pub. L. No. 93-110, 87 Stat. 353, Cong., H.R. No. 6912 (1973), and that defendant lessee need not pay rent in gold coin.


OVERVIEW: The parties' predecessors in interest entered into a ground lease, with rent to be paid in gold coin or its equivalent in lawful money. Plaintiff lessors filed an action seeking increased lease payments based on a reading of the gold clause in the lease as a contract for payment in gold coin as a commodity. Plaintiffs argued that the Joint Resolution of 1933 (joint resolution), 31 U.S.C.S. § 463, which enacted the monetary change referred to as "going off the gold standard," was repealed by the 1973 amendment of the Par Value Modification Act (1973 Act), Pub. L. No. 93-110, 87 Stat. 353, Cong., H.R. No. 6912, and that the repeal revived the gold clause in the lease. The trial court ruled in favor of defendant lessee, finding that the gold clause of the lease was not a contract for payment in gold as a commodity, but was a contract for payment of money, and that defendant was not obligated to pay the rent in gold coin. The trial court held that the joint resolution was not repealed, expressly or impliedly, by the 1973 Act. The court affirmed, holding that the trial court did not err in concluding that the gold clause provision was a contract for the payment of money.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of defendant lessee, holding that the trial court did not err in concluding that the gold clause in the lease was a contract for the payment of money, not gold, because the gold monetary standard was not reinstated by the 1973 amendment of the Par Value Modification Act.

Anacker v. Sillas


Civ. No. 49116


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


65 Cal. App. 3d 416; 135 Cal. Rptr. 537; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2223



December 28, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 21, 1977, and Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied February 23, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-142257, Norman R. Dowds, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Director of the Department of Motor Vehicles sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted respondent driver a peremptory writ of mandate and commanded appellant to set aside an order suspending respondent's driving privilege under Cal. Veh. Code § 16070 for failure to establish proof of financial responsibility.


OVERVIEW: Respondent driver was injured in an automobile accident and failed to provide proof of automobile liability insurance as required by Cal. Veh. Code § 16070. Appellant Director of the Department of Motor Vehicles suspended respondent's driving privileges for failure to establish proof of financial responsibility. The court reversed the lower court's writ of mandate ordering appellant to set aside the suspension, holding that the statutory language requiring proof of financial responsibility referred to any person involved in an accident, rather than to any person other than the driver whose driving privilege was at issue. The court also rejected respondent's equal protection arguments, concluding that a driving privilege was not a fundamental right of the kind which demanded heightened judicial scrutiny, and holding that respondent failed to demonstrate the invalidity of the legislature's system of randomly checking the status of drivers' financial responsibility.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment granting respondent driver's writ of mandate because the suspension of respondent's driving privileges by appellant Director of the Department of Motor Vehicles was proper where respondent failed to provide proof of financial responsibility as required by statute.

American Airlines, Inc. v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 48897


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


65 Cal. App. 3d 325; 135 Cal. Rptr. 261; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2215



December 27, 1976

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county agencies appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which gave a refund of property taxes paid by plaintiff airlines for property leased from defendant city and held that the course of assessing taxes by defendant tax assessor was an unlawful and unconstitutional application of Cal. Code Regs. tit. 18, rule 23.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff airlines signed leases with defendant city for real property described as passenger terminal facilities. The leases were for a period of 28 years. None of the leases contained any option to renew. Defendant tax assessor assessed taxes on the entire 28 year term of the lease although the leases had only 18 and 17 years remaining. The issue before the trial court was whether defendant tax assessor properly assessed the possessory interests of defendants county agencies upon the reasonably anticipated term of possession of the leases property as provided in Cal. Code Regs. tit. 18, rule 23 instead of the basis of the remaining terms of defendants' leases. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that the course of assessing taxes by defendant tax assessor was an unlawful and unconstitutional application of rule 23 because plaintiffs did not have a possessory interest in the leased premises following expiration of the terms of the leases as required by Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 107. The court held that defendant tax assessor's methods of assessment were invalid as to what rule 23 permits.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that gave a refund of property taxes paid by plaintiffs airlines for property leased from the city and held that the course of assessing taxes by defendant tax assessor was unlawful and unconstitutional because plaintiffs did not have a possessory interest in the leased premises following expiration of the terms of the leases as required by statute.
