In re Marriage of Harris


Civ. No. 15904


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


65 Cal. App. 3d 143; 134 Cal. Rptr. 891; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2198



December 21, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. D-28907, William L. Murray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband sought review of the order of the superior court of Orange County (California), which denied his motion to terminate spousal support payments and awarded respondent wife attorney's fees and costs. Appellant argued that respondent had remarried or the equivalent, and he was no longer required to pay support.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, by an interlocutory decree, was ordered to pay spousal support for six years. The decree and the marital settlement agreement also provided that the support requirement would not be modifiable on any ground and that the support would terminate upon her remarriage. Appellant contended that the provisions prescribing that the support payments were not modifiable, did not divest the court of jurisdiction to revoke the support order. The court disagreed and affirmed because it found that the parties had agreed that spousal support was to continue for six years and that it was not modifiable on any ground except upon the death of either party or the remarriage of former wife in which cases it would terminate. Such an agreement was specifically authorized by Cal. Civ. Code § 4811(b). The agreed spousal support was therefore not modifiable whether or not facts exist that would have brought Cal. Civ. Code § 4801.5 into play.


OUTCOME: The judgment ordering the continuance of spousal support was affirmed because the marital agreement specified for same and it was not modifiable upon its own terms.

In re Newmann


Civ. No. 17404


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


65 Cal. App. 3d 57; 134 Cal. Rptr. 886; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2190



December 20, 1976 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The order to show cause is discharged and the writ of habeas corpus is denied. The medical director of Patton State Hospital is directed to notify the Superior Court of Los Angeles County forthwith that petitioner is being held subject to the order of the Los Angeles Superior Court in which the conservatorship proceedings are now pending.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus from the order of the Superior Court of San Bernadino County (California), claiming that he was involuntarily confined after being found mentally incompetent to stand trial on criminal charges. The lower court had directed the department of health to evaluate petitioner and recommend a suitable disposition.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was arrested and charged with burglary and attempted robbery. A competency hearing was held and evidence of petitioner's incompetency was received from two qualified medical examiners. He was found incompetent due to mental retardation and was remanded to a state hospital with an order to examine petitioner and report its conclusions and recommendations. The superior court later amended its earlier order upon representation by the public guardian that his office was unable to institute conservatorship proceedings for petitioner and directed the department of health to evaluate petitioner and recommend a suitable disposition. Petitioner contended that his confinement was in violation of certain constitutional safeguards. The court denied the writ because it had been determined that petitioner was not likely to regain his competency in the foreseeable future. Therefore, it followed that the pending criminal proceedings against petitioner could no longer provide a valid basis for petitioner's continued involuntary confinement. The court noted that it appeared that the best interests of petitioner would be served by placing his custody in the care of the court.


OUTCOME: The order to show cause was discharged and the writ of habeas corpus was denied. The medical director of the state hospital was directed to notify the lower court forthwith that petitioner was being held subject to the order of the superior court in which the conservatorship proceedings were then pending.

Weekes v. Oakland


Civ. No. 37873


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION One


64 Cal. App. 3d 907; 134 Cal. Rptr. 858; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2173



December 17, 1976 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Feb. 11, 1977 (see 21 C.3d 386)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, City of Oakland and its treasurer, sought review of a judgment of the trial court (California) which, in response to appellants and intervenors' complaints, declared that appellant city's employees' license fee taxing ordinance was invalid and of no force and effect and permanently enjoined the enforcement of that ordinance.


OVERVIEW: On a challenge by appellant City of Oakland and its treasurer, the court reversed the trial court's judgment that the city's ordinance, codified at Oakland, Cal. Code art. I, §§ 5-1.65 and 5-1.65(a) - (s), was invalid and of no force and effect. The court held that the tax was a license or privilege tax for revenue which appellant was authorized to impose. The ordinance was a valid tax measure authorized by the city charter and the enabling provisions of the state constitution, Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5, which impliedly empowered chartered cities to levy taxes for general revenue purposes; neither constitutional provisions governing the levy of an income tax, nor state legislation preempted appellant's right to levy the tax, and the ordinance did not violate constitutional equal protection because of any alleged discrimination between residents and nonresidents, or because of the exemption of domestic servants. The ordinance did not unlawfully restrict the right of any nonresident to travel and work outside the city in which he resided and did not unlawfully tax governmental employees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment declaring that appellant city's employees' license fee taxing ordinance was invalid because the tax was valid under appellant's authority and charter and was not preempted by the state constitution or state legislation.

 

2077 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Banks


Crim. No. 28944


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


67 Cal. App. 3d 379; 137 Cal. Rptr. 652; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2246



December 16, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A610055, Julius A. Leetham, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction by the Superior Court, Los Angeles Count (California), which found him guilty of involuntary manslaughter, Cal. Penal Code § 192(2), and of using a firearm in the commission of the offense, Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5, after a jury trial.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was involved in an argument among several people and fired shots into the air in an effort to get everyone's attention. Appellant testified that he tripped on a curb, and, as he was getting up, he saw the victim coming from behind a pole, wearing black gloves and holding a gun. Appellant fired, trying to disable and avoid getting shot by the victim. Appellant was convicted of involuntary manslaughter, Cal. Penal Code § 192(2), and of using a firearm in the commission of the offense, Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5, after a jury trial. Upon review, the court reversed, finding the trial court's extemporaneous instruction to the jury that, once the state established the homicide was committed by appellant, appellant had the burden to prove justifiable homicide was prejudicial error. The court found the instruction did not merely require appellant to raise reasonable doubt, but to prove whether or not the homicide was committed by way of self-defense, and it was the state's burden to prove the absence of justification beyond a reasonable doubt. The court remanded for a new trial and noted that Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5 was not applicable to a manslaughter charge.


OUTCOME: The court reversed appellant's conviction, finding the trial court's extemporaneous jury instruction that, once the state proved appellant committed homicide, the burden shifted to him to prove the homicide was justifiable was prejudicial error. The court remanded for a new trial, noting that the charge of using a firearm in the commission of an offense was not applicable to a manslaughter charge.

In re Michele C.


Civ. No. 38413


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


64 Cal. App. 3d 818; 135 Cal. Rptr. 17; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2163



December 14, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 11, 1977, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 11, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 79699, John H. Moskowitz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father challenged an order of the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), which granted the petition of respondent California State Department of Health and declared appellant's daughter free from his custody and control pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 232(a)(4).


OVERVIEW: Appellant father was convicted of murdering his stepdaughter. Three weeks after the child's death, appellant's wife gave birth to a daughter. Respondent California State Department of Health petitioned to declare the child free from appellant's custody and control pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 232(a)(4), which provided for the termination of parental rights when a parent was convicted of a felony that demonstrated his unfitness to have future custody and control of the child. The trial court granted the petition. Appellant argued that because § 232(a)(4) did not require consideration of the current parent-child relationship or appellant's current fitness as a parent. Appellant also alleged that because its sole criterion was the nature of a prior felony conviction, it was an unconstitutional statutory presumption. The court affirmed and held that §232(a)(4) was not unconstitutional. The court held that when § 232(a)(4) was read together with Cal. Civ. Code § 4600, it was clear that the trial court found that an award of custody to appellant would have been detrimental to the child.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that granted respondent California State Department of Health's petition and declared that appellant father's daughter was free from his custody and control. The court found that the trial court had determined that in light of appellant's conviction of murdering his stepdaughter, awarding him custody of his daughter would have been detrimental to the child.

People v. Thomas


Crim. No. 28844


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


65 Cal. App. 3d 854; 135 Cal. Rptr. 644; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2242



December 14, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 610085, Thomas T. Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of inflicting cruel and inhuman corporal punishment on a child in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 273d.


OVERVIEW: Defendant beat his 15-year-old stepdaughter when she refused his sexual advances. He was convicted of violating Cal. Penal Code § 273d, which provided that any person who willfully inflicted upon any child any cruel or inhuman corporal punishment or injury that resulted in a traumatic condition was guilty of a felony. Defendant argued that the statute was unconstitutionally vague and that the victim was not a child within the statute's meaning. The court affirmed the conviction because § 273d was not unconstitutionally vague, and because the legal applicability of § 273d turned on the child's chronological age. The court held that "child" as used in § 273d must be equated with the term "minor" as used in Cal. Civ. Code § 25 and Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 600, and applied to persons under age 18. The court corrected a clerical error in the trial court's written judgment because the trial court did not have jurisdiction to specify detention in a particular prison.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for inflicting cruel and inhuman corporal punishment on a child because the statute he was convicted of violating was not unconstitutionally vague, and because the statute applied to all children under age 18.

Christman v. Franchise Tax Bd.


Civ. No. 48370


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


64 Cal. App. 3d 751; 134 Cal. Rptr. 725; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2158



December 9, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 23, 1976, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 3, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C79546, Robert A. Wenke, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Franchise Tax Board sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which held that respondent individuals were entitled to a tax credit under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 18001, in respondents' suit to recover sums paid under protest to appellant after their pursuit of administrative remedies was exhausted.


OVERVIEW: Respondent individuals brought suit to recover sums paid under protest to appellant Franchise Tax Board after their pursuit of administrative remedies was exhausted. Respondents sought tax credits for taxes they paid to Georgia on income derived from ownership in a corporation. After the trial court held that respondents were entitled to a tax credit under Rev. & Tax. Code § 18001, appellant sought review. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that California rules governed the determination of the nature of the income and that those rules designated the stock in the corporation as the source of respondents' income. The court further held that California law utilized the mobilia sequuntur personam doctrine to locate the stock in California and that the one exception to the doctrine, being the business situs exception, was inapplicable. The court held that the income was derived in California and thus the tax credit provisions of § 18001 could not be used. The court further held that there was no invidious discrimination violative of the equal protection clause, and the stock was not part of interstate commerce.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which granted respondent individuals a tax credit for sums paid to appellant Franchise Tax Board. The court held that the tax credit provisions of the applicable statute could not be used because the income was derived in California. The court held that the stock in the corporation was the source of respondents' income and that the stock was located in California.

 

2084 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Marsille v. City of Santa Ana


Civ. No. 15635


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


64 Cal. App. 3d 764; 134 Cal. Rptr. 743; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2159



December 9, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 5, 1977, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 3, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, Nos. 221050, 221051 and 221052, William L. Murray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. 4



4 Our decision is not altered by the recently decided case of Van Riessen v. City of Santa Monica, 63 Cal.App.3d 193 [133 Cal.Rptr. 618], (petition for hearing pending before the state Supreme Court), because that case is distinguishable from the one before us. There petitioners, nine firemen retired from the City of Santa Monica Fire Department, appealed from the judgment denying their petition for writ of mandate which sought to compel the City of Santa Monica to make monetary payment to each of them for unused sick leave days which had been accumulated prior to retirement. Unlike the situation in the case before us, Van Riessen is concerned with the interpretation of the Santa Monica municipal code and amendments thereto, as well as with the interpretation of certain memoranda of understanding entered into between the City of Santa Monica and the firefighters' union. There is no indication in Van Riessen that the City of Santa Monica had contracted with the Public Employees' Retirement System; presumably that city had not because the court made no reference to the PERS provisions found in the Government Code. Furthermore, while the ex-firemen in the present case are concerned with receiving compensation for their accumulated unused sick leave, they are also concerned with using that unused sick leave to defer their disability retirement dates, an issue not before the Van Riessen court.

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city challenged an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which issued peremptory writs of mandate to compel defendant to compensate plaintiff firemen for their accumulated sick leave when they were awarded disability retirements.


OVERVIEW: After the trial court ordered defendant city to compensate plaintiff firemen for their accumulated sick leave when plaintiffs were awarded disability retirement, defendant argued that it had plenary power under the state constitution to determine its own civil service rules. The court held that Cal. Gov't Code § 21025.2 provided that the retirement of an employee of the Public Employees' Retirement System (PERS) must be delayed until his sick leave, with compensation, expired. The court affirmed because the conditions for application of the statute were met: plaintiffs were entitled to sick leave compensation because it was the intent of the legislature to have PERS members receive full compensation of unused sick leave; state, not municipal law, was applicable; and plaintiffs did not consent to the assignment of their retirement date.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order compelling defendant city to compensate plaintiff firemen for their accumulated sick leave because the state's provision that the retirement of a member of the Public Employees' Retirement System be delayed until his sick leave expired preempted defendant's provisions.

Reece v. Alcoholic Beverage Etc. Appeals Bd.

Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District

December 7, 1976

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, Acting Director of the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control, sought a writ of review for reversal of a decision of respondent, Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board (California), which held Cal. Code Regs. tit. 4, § 58 unconstitutional under equal protection standards.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board argued that Cal. Code Regs. tit. 4, § 58 was unconstitutional because the rule limited the fundamental right of one class of persons to pursue a lawful occupation. The rule disqualified the wife of a law enforcement officer from holding a liquor license. The court held that respondent erred in determining the rule was deficient because it improperly isolated the effect of the rule on a segment of the affected class and did not consider the fact that the rule applied to all spouses of all employees of the department of justice. The court also held that the strict scrutiny test did not apply to the liquor industry because regulation of the industry was related to the public interest. The court annulled respondent's decision because the rule, which prevented conflicts of interest, was rationally related to a justifiable governmental interest.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the decision of respondent Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board holding a rule unconstitutional on the grounds that it prohibited spouses of department of justice employees from holding liquor licenses because the rule was rationally related to a justifiable governmental interest.

 

2086 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Watson


Crim. No. 28450


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION One


64 Cal. App. 3d 515; 134 Cal. Rptr. 675; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2094



December 3, 1976 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] as modified Dec. 15, 1976
Hg. granted Jan. 24, 1977 (see 19 C.3d 646)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prison superintendent challenged a judgment of the trial court (California), which granted respondent prisoner's petition requesting 285 days of time-served credit pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5 for the time he was in the custody of out-of-state authorities while fighting extradition.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to being convicted of murder, respondent prisoner argued that he was entitled to credit for time served out-of state while fighting state extradition proceedings. Reviewing relevant statutes, the court held that the time-served credit granted by Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5 did not apply to time spent voluntarily fighting extradition. In so holding, the court found that defendant had fled from the state after committing the murders and was considered a fugitive from the state's justice, even while he was in custody of out-of state authorities. Additionally, the court held that the time defendant spent in the custody of out-of-state authorities was attributable to his challenge to the extradition and not to the charges arising from the murders; thus, such time did not fall within the ambit of time served pursuant to § 2900.5. As a result, the court held that the trial court erred in awarding respondent credit for the time he was in the custody of out-of-state authorities.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment granting respondent prisoner's petition seeking time-served credit for the time he was in the custody of out-of-state authorities fighting extradition, as state law did not allow credit for such time periods.

Teachers Management & Inv. Corp. v. City of Santa Cruz


Civ. No. 38200


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


64 Cal. App. 3d 438; 134 Cal. Rptr. 523; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2087



December 2, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 28, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 54834, Charles S. Franich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The purported appeal from the order sustaining the demurrer is dismissed. The judgment as to the cause of action for declaratory relief is modified to declare that plaintiffs are not entitled to a determination that the initiative measure is invalid. As so modified, the judgment is affirmed. Respondents will recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporations challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which dismissed their complaint against respondent city that sought declaratory and injunctive relief and claimed that an initiative ordinance of respondent was void.


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporations entered into an agreement with respondent city and the county whereby respondent and the county would construct a convention center and appellant would develop private facilities surrounding it. The voters in respondent city passed an initiative ordinance precluding the city from participating. The trial court sustained respondent's demurrer on the ground, inter alia, that the complaint did not state a cause of action, and the ordinance was not a land use ordinance. The court agreed that the ordinance did not impose any zoning or land use restrictions, it merely precluded respondent's participation in the convention center project. Appellants were still free to construct a convention center complex. Even if there were binding agreements, appellants could not prevent respondent from abandoning or rescinding plans to proceed, although the change in policy could result in liability for breach of contract. However, appellants did not allege a breach of contract. The court found that the order sustaining the demurrer was a correct decision on the merits of the case. Thus the court modified the judgment to declare that appellants were not entitled to relief.


OUTCOME: The order sustaining the demurrer was dismissed. The judgment as to the cause of action for declaratory relief was modified to declare that appellant corporations were not entitled to a determination that the initiative measure was invalid because the initiative did not impose zoning or land use restrictions and did not impair the obligation of contract. As modified, the judgment was affirmed and respondent city recovered costs on appeal.

Crowley v. City and County of San Francisco


Civ. No. 38869


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


64 Cal. App. 3d 450; 134 Cal. Rptr. 533; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2088; 94 L.R.R.M. 2527; 80 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P54,073



December 2, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 20, 1976, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 27, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 697822, William E. Mullins, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to render judgment for appellants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant association challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied relief upon their complaint for an injunction and a writ of mandate requiring respondents, commission and board, to abide by a certain agreement regulating the terms of service of police officers.


OVERVIEW: Appellant association entered into an agreement with respondents, commission and board, in which appellants agreed that it would forgo the power to strike. The agreement also contained police officers' bill of rights. Appellants went on strike and the mayor invoked his emergency powers and entered into an agreement with appellants to end the strike. Subsequently, respondents rescinded its approval of their agreement with appellants because they materially breached the agreement by striking. Appellants commenced an action to compel respondents to abide by the terms and conditions of the original agreement; the trial court denied appellants' request for relief. Appellants challenged the trial court's decision. The court reversed the trial court's judgment denying appellants' relief and directed the trial court to render a judgment for appellants. The court held that in accordance with Cal. Gov't Code § 3505.1, once respondents approved the agreement it was binding on them. The court reasoned that although appellants' strike constituted a breach, the mayor's emergency agreement constituted an attempt to waive respondents' right to rescind the original agreement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment denying appellant association's request for an injunction and writ of mandate requiring respondents, commission and board, to abide by a certain agreement, and directed the trial court to render a judgment for appellants. The court held that although appellants breached its underlying agreement with respondents, the mayor's agreement vitiated respondents' right to rescind.

People v. Perez


Crim. No. 28260


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


64 Cal. App. 3d 297; 134 Cal. Rptr. 338; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2070



November 29, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-320151, Raymond Cardenas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State of California challenged the granting of respondent's Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5 motion to suppress certain drug-related evidence found in respondent's possession when he was arrested for being drunk in a public place, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350, arguing that respondent was, in fact, in a public place of his own will at the time of his arrest.


OVERVIEW: Appellant State of California sought review from a judgment of dismissal following the grant of a Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5 motion by respondent to suppress certain drug-related evidence with respect to the charge of heroin possession in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350. Appellant contended that there was no evidence to support the finding that respondent was not in a public place of his own volition at the time of his arrest for being drunk in a public place. On appeal, the judgment of dismissal was reversed. In support of its ruling, the court held that the only issue in dispute was whether the building hallway in which respondent was arrested for being drunk in a public place, which resulted in the discovery of appellant's heroin, was a public place for statutory purposes. With respect to the instant case, the court noted that the hallway in question was, in fact, a public place as it was a location readily accessible to all those who wished to go there and because there were no locked gates or doors to keep the public from entering.


OUTCOME: The granting of respondent's motion to suppress evidence was reversed as the court found that respondent was in a public place at the time of his arrest, that culminated in the discovery of the drugs, as the location was fully accessible to the public in that it did not have any locks or doors barring entry.

Zhadan v. Downtown L. A. Motors


Civ. No. 47380


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


66 Cal. App. 3d 481; 136 Cal. Rptr. 132; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2245



November 23, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified November 30, 1976. A petition for a rehearing was denied December 8, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C. 61687, Charles H. Older, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting a new trial is reversed. The judgment for plaintiff is reversed.

Each party shall bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant car repair shop appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that awarded plaintiff compensatory and punitive damages for unauthorized service work upon plaintiff's car in violation of the Automotive Repair Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 9884.9. Plaintiff appealed the trial court order granting defendant's motion for a new trial on the ground the punitive damages awarded to plaintiff were excessive.


OVERVIEW: After defendant allegedly performed repair work without authorization and retained plaintiff's car following nonpayment, plaintiff sued for violation of the Automotive Repair Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 9884.9. Plaintiff was awarded both compensatory and punitive damages. The trial court granted defendant's motion for a new trial on the ground that the punitive damages award was excessive. Both parties appealed. The court reversed the order granting a new trial, holding that the order failed to make an adequate specification of reasons as required by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 657. The court then determined that the punitive damage award was excessive and appeared to be the product of the jury's passion or prejudice. The court found that defendant's proposed instruction on punitive damages was properly rejected as it erroneously suggested that there was a fixed ratio between the punitive damages and the actual damages sustained. The court also reversed the judgment for plaintiff concluding that the proper course was to remand for a new trial on all issues where little purpose would be served by remanding for a trial upon the issue of damages alone.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court order granting defendant car repair shop's motion for a new trial on the grounds that the punitive damages award was excessive where the trial court failed to specify its reasons for the order; and also reversed the judgment for plaintiff awarding damages for defendant's violations of the Automotive Repair Act concluding that it was best to remand for a new trial on all the issues rather than on damages alone.

Breed v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 38577


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Director of the Department of the Youth Authority sought a writ of prohibition to restrain respondent Superior Court of Alameda County (California) from enforcing an order which continued juvenile, a real party in interest, under an earlier commitment to the youth authority and released him from custody pending a further hearing. Petitioner also sought a writ of mandate compelling respondent to set aside that order.

Code § 17370.1. After he was ineligible for commitment for a mental disorder, respondent ordered his return to the youth authority under the existing commitment, but the youth authority refused to take him back. Petitioner Director of the Department of the Youth Authority sought a writ of prohibition and mandate to restrain respondent from enforcing the order. The reviewing court denied the writs. The court found that respondent did not act in excess of its authority by releasing the juvenile on parole from the youth authority.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner Director of the Department of the Youth Authority's petitions for writs of prohibition and mandate, and discharged the alternative writ of mandate. The court held that respondent had jurisdiction to return the juvenile to the Department of the Youth Authority under an existing commitment, and respondent trial court did not interfere with the youth authority's discretion.

 

2092 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Spencer v. G. A. MacDonald Constr. Co.


Civ. No. 15213


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


63 Cal. App. 3d 836; 134 Cal. Rptr. 78; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2134; 41 Cal. Comp. Cases 1087



November 16, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 8, 1976 and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 17, 1977. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 198856, Claude M. Owens, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sub-contractor appealed from a judgment against him in the Superior Court of Orange County (California). Plaintiff contended that Cal. Lab. Code §§ 6304 and 6304.5 were unconstitutional and, alternatively, that § 6304.5 should be strictly interpreted. Plaintiff also challenged the trial court's decisions as to the inclusion and exclusion of particular jury instructions on contributory negligence.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sub-contractor was injured while working without a safety harness at a construction sight owned by defendant employer. The personal injury claim against defendant was defeated in a jury trial, and plaintiff appealed. Plaintiff attacked the constitutionality of Cal. Lab. Code §§ 6304 and 6304.5, arguing that their subject matter was not sufficiently related to their stated purpose, violating Cal. Const. art. IV, § 9, and that the legislature was unaware of the legal effect of the statutes, violating Rule 9 of the Joint Rules of the California Legislature. The court held that the statutes were sufficiently related to workmen's compensation to pass constitutional muster and that legislatures are presumed to know the effect of laws they enact. On the application of § 6304.5, the court ruled that the admission of safety orders into evidence in third-party actions by an employee was forbidden. Plaintiff's objections to various jury instructions on contributory negligence were similarly discounted by the court, which held that the issue of negligence was adequately covered by the given instructions, which in turn were supported by substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment against plaintiff sub-contractor was affirmed. The court held that plaintiff sub-contractor's constitutional challenges failed to demonstrate that the subject matter of the statutes in question were unrelated to their purpose or that he should be permitted to introduce safety orders into evidence in a third-party action.

 

2093 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Bartha


Crim. No. 28593


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


63 Cal. App. 3d 584; 134 Cal. Rptr. 39; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2040; 91 A.L.R.3d 759



November 10, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 3, 1977.



DISPOSITION: The petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner fortune teller appealed a conviction from the appellate department of the superior court (California) for fortune telling and that affirmed the misdemeanor conviction as a violation of Los Angeles, Cal. Municipal Code § 43.30.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner fortune teller claimed she was a high priestess of the religion Wicca as her defense to the charge of violating Los Angeles, Cal. Municipal Code § 43.30, which made the business of fortune telling a misdemeanor. The court affirmed the conviction. The court held that petitioner was engaged in the business when she advertised in the local newspaper and with business cards, readings took place in a commercial shop where numerous curios were on sale, and there was a sign over the cash register stating that tarot card readings were available for $ 10.00. Petitioner's religion did not require her to do fortune telling as a business, and the question of fees was not a tenet of her religion, therefore, the practice of the religion itself was not prohibited by the ordinance. Her constitutional right to freedom of speech was not violated by a prohibition against commercial enterprises deemed harmful to the public welfare. The fact that a state statute prohibited fraudulent fortune telling along with other fraudulent gaming devices was not indicative of state preemption of the field that prevented respondent from prohibiting the practice altogether.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed plaintiff fortune teller's misdemeanor conviction and dismissed her petition for writ of habeas corpus because the statute that criminalized fortune telling was not unconstitutionally vague and overbroad, did not interfere with the practice of witchcraft, did not restrict her freedom of speech, and was not preempted by a state law that criminalized fraudulent fortune telling along with several fraudulent gaming devices.

 

2094 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Gai v. City Council


Civ. No. 2689


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


63 Cal. App. 3d 381; 133 Cal. Rptr. 753; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2020



November 4, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 164227, Hollis G. Best, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying the writ of mandate is reversed; the matter is remanded to the trial court with directions to issue a writ of mandate to respondent city council ordering it to grant appellants the 50-cent per hour wage increase for the 1973-1974 fiscal year with interest from July 1, 1973.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants pipefitters and refrigeration fitters challenged an order of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which denied their petition for writ of mandamus to compel appellee city council to adopt a salary resolution amendment which would give them a 50-cent per hour retroactive wage increase.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied appellants pipe and refrigeration fitters' petition for a writ of mandamus to compel respondent city council to grant them a retroactive wage increase. The trial court held that because the wage increase was not being paid to private employees in the city on July 1, 1973 that respondent was powerless to grant appellants the wage increase retroactive to July 1, 1973. Appellants sought review. The court reversed, finding that respondent was required to adopt the salary amendment. The trial court's holding that respondent was powerless to retroactively increase appellants' rate of pay for the 1973-1974 fiscal year ignored the clear mandate of respondent's charter. The charter provided that when a rate of pay was paid in private industry and was certified by the civil service board, the city council had the duty to fix such rate of pay for groups engaged in the city service. The court concluded that there were no irreconcilable conflicts between the provisions of the code and the charter and the provisions could be harmonized to carry out the public policy of providing appellants the same wage rate received by comparable employees in private industry.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment denying the writ of mandate on the basis that respondent city council failed to follow the procedures authorized by its charter and code to insure appellants of their right to comparable wages with private employees which resulted in appellants receiving less than their counterparts in private industry for the 1973-1974 fiscal year.

City of Los Angeles v. Department of Health


Civ. No. 48697


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


63 Cal. App. 3d 473; 133 Cal. Rptr. 771; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2028



November 2, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 19, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C116571, Norman R. Dowds, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city appealed a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which determined that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5116 was controlling over plaintiff's ordinances to the contrary, in favor of defendant California Department of Health. Plaintiff enacted a zoning ordinance which, if valid, would have precluded the location of a facility described in § 5116 in an area zoned for single family residences.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff city was a chartered municipality within the meaning of Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5. It enacted a zoning ordinance containing a definition of single family dwelling that would have precluded the location of a facility described in Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5116 in an area zoned for single family residences. Section 5116 provided that certain state facilities were to be considered a residential use for zoning purposes, and that such homes were a permitted use in all residential zones. Plaintiff sought a declaration that its zoning ordinance was valid despite the state statute. Both plaintiff and defendant California Department of Health moved for summary judgment. The trial court declared that city planning and zoning ordinances contrary to the state statute were invalid. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court concluded that § 5116 was, by its terms, applicable to both chartered and general law cities, and that the statute was part of a statutory scheme of treatment of mentally ill and handicapped persons, which was of statewide concern. The court held that § 5116 was meant to occupy the field to the exclusion of municipal regulation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court declaring that plaintiff city's planning and zoning ordinances that are contrary to the state statute are invalid. The state statute in conflict with the ordinance was part of a statutory scheme of treatment of mentally ill and handicapped persons that was of statewide concern, and the statute was applicable to both chartered and general law cities.

People v. Howard


Crim. No. 28221


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


63 Cal. App. 3d 249; 133 Cal. Rptr. 689; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2008



October 29, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 24, 1976, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 22, 1976.

* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of possession of a concealable firearm by a convicted felon, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12021.


OVERVIEW: Police officers went to an apartment building to investigate tips that defendant was selling heroin and using a certain apartment as a "shooting gallery." The officers entered the building through a locked security door by waiting for someone to open it as they passed through. The officers went immediately to defendant's apartment and identified themselves. Defendant allowed entry to his apartment and consented to a search. The search revealed marijuana and guns, all of which were seized. Defendant was convicted of possession of a concealable firearm by a convicted felon, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12021. No constitutional infringement of privacy occurred when the officers entered through the outside security door and went immediately to defendant's apartment where they had business with him.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction of possession of a concealable firearm by a convicted felon. The court held that defendant's constitutional rights were not violated, substantial evidence supported the conclusion that defendant consented to the search, seizure of the guns was constitutional, and the statute defendant was convicted under was not unconstitutional.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant developer sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied its petition for a writ of administrative mandamus seeking a judgment that it was entitled to an exemption from the provisions of the California Coastal Zone Conservation Act, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 27000 et seq., that had been denied by respondent, North Central Coast Regional Commission.

Pub. Res. Code § 27000 et seq. that had been denied by respondent North Central Coast Regional Commission. Verreos v. City and County of San Francisco


Civ. No. 38224


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


63 Cal. App. 3d 86; 133 Cal. Rptr. 649; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1991



October 27, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 694432, John E. Benson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The purported appeal of the board of supervisors is dismissed, and the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer and defendant city appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which validated defendant's mayor proclaiming a city emergency in a class action brought by plaintiff seeking declaratory and injunctive relief and the issuance of a writ of mandamus and prohibition against the implementation of a strike settlement agreement entered into by defendant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer brought a class action seeking to prohibit the implementation of a strike settlement agreement pursuant to which defendant city had agreed to grant their policemen and firemen a raise in salary after the policemen and firemen commenced illegal strikes which resulted in almost a complete cessation of police and fire protection. Plaintiff alleged that defendant mayor wrongfully declared a state of emergency and that his approval to raise salaries was invalid because the city charter vested sole authority to fix salary rates in defendant's board of supervisors. The trial court rendered a judgment, which took judicial notice of the mayor's emergency proclamation, that a public emergency had existed, and that the emergency proclamation was valid. Both parties appealed. OUTCOME: The appeal of defendant city was dismissed and the court affirmed the judgment because the trial court did not err in finding that plaintiff taxpayer failed to raise any proper basis for attacking defendant mayor's actions in declaring a city emergency as the courts cannot inquire into the motives behind legislative acts.

Douglas v. Unemployment Ins. Appeals Bd.


Civ. No. 38772


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


63 Cal. App. 3d 110; 133 Cal. Rptr. 604; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1992



October 27, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 26, 1976, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 22, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 56436, Gilbert B. Perry, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which denied her request for an administrative writ of mandate directing respondent unemployment insurance appeals board to set aside its decision denying her benefits.


OVERVIEW: Respondent unemployment insurance appeals board denied appellant's application for benefits based on its finding that appellant had voluntarily left her job without good cause within the meaning of Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1256. Appellant filed a petition for an administrative writ of mandate to compel respondent to set aside its decision, which the trial court denied. Appellant challenged the denial, contending primarily that the trial court exceeded its jurisdiction in making findings of fact that differed from respondent's findings of fact. Affirming the trial court's judgment, the court held that the trial court did not exceed its jurisdiction by making findings that differed slightly from those of respondent. The court found that appellant took a voluntary leave of absence for purely personal reasons, after being informed by her employer that a replacement would be hired and that she would be reemployed after the leave only if the replacement proved unsatisfactory. As such, the court concluded that appellant left her work voluntarily and without good cause, which disqualified her for unemployment insurance benefits.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of an administrative writ of mandate to compel respondent unemployment insurance appeals board to set aside its decision to deny appellant's application for benefits. Appellant was disqualified for unemployment insurance benefits because she voluntarily terminated her employment for personal reasons not attributable to the employer.

 

2100 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


McDonald v. Sacramento Medical Foundation Blood Bank


Civ. No. 15546


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


62 Cal. App. 3d 866; 133 Cal. Rptr. 444; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1962



October 18, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 16, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 215717, Irving H. Perluss, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, patient's husband, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which sustained demurrers filed by defendants, doctors, hospital, and nonprofit community blood bank, to the strict liability and breach of warranty causes of action in plaintiff's wrongful death action.


OVERVIEW: A patient died after contracting serum hepatitis from a blood transfusion she received during a surgical procedure. Plaintiff, patient's husband, filed a wrongful death action against defendants, doctors, hospital, and nonprofit community blood bank, alleging causes of action in strict liability, breach of warranty, and negligence. The trial court sustained defendants' demurrers to the strict liability and breach of warranty causes of action. Plaintiff appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that strict liability should not have been applied to true services. Cal. Health & Safety § 1606 foreclosed the characterization of the transaction as a sale. The court found that blood injections and a ready availability of blood were absolutely vital to the medical profession's constant struggle against illness, injury, and disease. The court found that the legislature had weighed the relatively minor risk of hepatitis which the blood recipient must have taken, and had determined that in the absence of negligence, such risk must have been accepted. The court found that strict liability could not have been justified.


OUTCOME: The judgment sustaining the demurrers of defendants, doctors, hospital, and nonprofit community blood bank, to the strict liability and breach of warranty causes of action in a wrongful death action was affirmed because the policy objectives did not apply to transfusion hepatitis cases. Blood injections and a ready availability of blood were absolutely vital to the medical profession's constant struggle against illness, injury, and disease.

Cramer v. Queen of Angels Hospital


Civ. No. 47854


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


62 Cal. App. 3d 812; 133 Cal. Rptr. 339; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1954; 20 U.C.C. Rep. Serv. (Callaghan) 319



October 13, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 9, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 56355, Richard Schauer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff patient challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted defendant hospital's motion for nonsuit in the tort action in which plaintiff sought damages based on the assertion that he had contracted serum hepatitis from a blood transfusion.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted defendant hospital's motion for nonsuit in the tort action that plaintiff patient filed in which he alleged that he contracted serum hepatitis from a blood transfusion. The court affirmed that judgment on appeal. The court determined that under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1606 and its underlying rationale, defendant could not be held liable under the doctrine of strict liability in tort or for breach of warranty. The court also found that § 1606 was in the area of economic regulation, and that it was clothed with a presumption of constitutionality. The court further held that § 1606 had a reasonable relationship to a legitimate state purpose because the production and use of human blood for therapeutic purposes should have been encouraged, and that those who provided those products and were free of fault should not have suffered the economic loss that might otherwise be imposed under strict liability.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of defendant hospital's motion for nonsuit in plaintiff patient's action, which alleged that he contracted serum hepatitis from a blood transfusion. The court found that the statute that granted immunity to defendant reasonably related to the legitimate state purposes of encouraging the therapeutic use of blood, and of protecting providers of that product that were free of fault from strict liability.

People v. Edmondson


Crim. No. 28235


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


62 Cal. App. 3d 677; 133 Cal. Rptr. 297; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1945



October 8, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-311433, Earl F. Riley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found him guilty of assault with a deadly weapon.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his conviction for assault with a deadly weapon. Defendant argued that the crime became a misdemeanor when the trial court committed him to the youth authority and that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 1770 providing for his discharge in two years or upon his 23rd birthday, whichever came later, was unconstitutional in that it restrained him of his liberty for longer than he would have been had he been committed to either jail or prison, and deprived him of equal protection of the laws, and constituted cruel and unusual punishment. The court held that defendant was not committed to the youth authority for a term potentially longer than the maximum term of imprisonment which might have been imposed upon an adult committing the same offense and § 1770 did not authorize the authority to maintain control over him in excess of the maximum term of imprisonment permitted by statute. Thus, the court perceived no violation of appellant's equal protection guarantees and his commitment, until he reached his 23rd birthday, was not cruel and unusual punishment. The court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for assault with a deadly weapon. The court held that defendant was not committed to the youth authority for a term longer than the maximum term of imprisonment for an adult committing the same offense and the court perceived no violation of defendant's equal protection guarantees.

People v. Gottfurcht


Crim. Ext. No. 76-198


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


62 Cal. App. 3d 634; 133 Cal. Rptr. 270; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1939



October 7, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 27, 1976. Jefferson (Bernard), J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 2, 1976. McComb, J., did not participate therein. Mosk, J., and Clark, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. 813881, Brian D. Crahan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of dismissal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged an order of the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), which responded to defendant's demurrer by dismissing his prosecution for allegedly violating Los Angeles Mun. Code § 12.12 by having unauthorized businesses operate in his hotel.


OVERVIEW: The trial court dismissed defendant's criminal prosecution for allowing unauthorized businesses to operate in his hotel in response to his demurrer to the complaint that was filed against him. The court reversed that dismissal on appeal, determining that Los Angeles Mun. Code § 12.12 was only concerned with the use to which defendant could put his real property. The court further concluded that zoning ordinances had long been sustained as legitimate exercise of the police power, and that, as a hotel or apartment hotel, only incidental businesses that were conducted as a service to persons living therein were allowed. The court additionally held that the limitations imposed on defendant dealt with how he held himself out to the public, and not who could enter the premises to avail themselves of the goods or services offered. The court also reasoned that the true character of the business conducted by defendant had to be determined as a factual matter at the trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the criminal prosecution of defendant for having unauthorized businesses in his hotel. The court held that the zoning ordinance that defendant allegedly violated only dealt with how he held himself out to the public, and not who could avail themselves of his hotel's goods or services, and held that a trial was required to determine the true character of his business.

Hall v. Bureau of Employment Agencies


Civ. No. 14460


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


64 Cal. App. 3d 482; 138 Cal. Rptr. 725; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 2092



October 7, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 22, 1976, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 2, 1976.

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employment agency challenged a decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied appellant's petition for mandate and held that respondent Bureau of Employment Agencies had properly suspended appellant's license for unprofessional conduct pursuant to the Employment Agency Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 9993(a), (c), (g), (h).


OVERVIEW: Appellant employment agency's license was suspended by respondent Bureau of Employment Agencies for unprofessional conduct based on violations of the Employment Agency Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 9993(a), (c), (g), (h). The trial court denied appellant's petition for mandate. Appellant sought review. Mannetter v. County of Marin


Civ. No. 37018


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


62 Cal. App. 3d 518; 133 Cal. Rptr. 119; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1928; 41 Cal. Comp. Cases 1060



October 4, 1976

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant deputy sheriff challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which held that respondent county properly denied appellant the additional holiday compensation rate under the collective bargaining agreement while appellant was on a disability leave of absence pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code § 4850.


OVERVIEW: Appellant deputy sheriff was assigned to work a regular shift of duty on July 4, 1973. However, appellant suffered an injury on July 3, 1973, that resulted in a temporary disability. Appellant was on a disability leave of absence pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code § 4850 and was unable to work on July 4, 1973. Appellant was paid the regular salary for July 4, 1973, but requested respondent county to pay the additional holiday compensation rate at time and one half under the terms of the collective bargaining agreement. Respondent refused to pay appellant at the time and one half rate for the holiday. The trial court denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandamus. Appellant sought review. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court that denied appellant holiday pay. The court held that appellant was not entitled to compensation under § 4850 for holiday pay not earned while on a disability leave of absence. Appellant was not entitled to pay for work that was not performed. The public policy to fully indemnify an employee for loss from an industrial injury did not include indemnification for benefits that the employee might have received during the leave of absence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court that held respondent county properly denied appellant deputy sheriff the additional holiday compensation rate under the collective bargaining agreement because appellant was on disability leave and did not actually perform any work on the holiday, although appellant had been scheduled to work on the holiday.

Findley v. Justice Court


Civ. No. 2836


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


62 Cal. App. 3d 566; 133 Cal. Rptr. 241; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1932



October 4, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 2, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 136778, Marvin E. Ferguson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which denied his petitioner for writ of prohibition seeking to compel defendant district court to dismiss criminal charges filed against him for throwing a rock at a vehicle pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 23110(a). Plaintiff contended that § 23110(a) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was charged in a criminal complaint with throwing a rock at a vehicle in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23110(a). Defendant district court denied plaintiff's motion to dismiss and plaintiff filed a petitioner for a writ of prohibition. The trial court denied plaintiff's writ of prohibition and plaintiff sought review. The court affirmed the trial court's decision that denied plaintiff's writ. The court held that § 23110(a) was not impermissibly vague or overbroad and provided fair notice of the proscribed conduct. The meaning of the word "vehicle" was defined in Cal. Veh. Code § 670, and the word "highway" was defined in Cal. Veh. Code §§ 360, 590. Under § 23110(a), the throwing of any physical matter at any automobile, truck, motorcycle, or similar type of vehicle while it was moving or parked on a highway or street publicly maintained and open to the use of the public for the purposes of vehicular travel was proscribed. The proscribed conduct was within the state's police power because it was a reasonable regulation of the state's highways, even though traditionally innocent activity was incidentally prohibited.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court that denied plaintiff's writ of prohibition seeking to compel defendant district court to dismiss criminal charges against him for throwing a rock at a vehicle. The court held that the statute that plaintiff was charged under was not impermissibly vague or overbroad, and the proscribed conduct was a valid exercise of the police power of the state.

Stork v. State of California


Civ. No. 15236


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


62 Cal. App. 3d 465; 133 Cal. Rptr. 207; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1922



September 30, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 246084, Hugh A. Evans, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The cause is remanded to the trial court, which is directed to enter a declaratory judgment consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff member of the Public Employees Retirement System appealed an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted summary judgment to defendant state of California in plaintiff's declaratory judgment action challenging the constitutionality of Cal. Gov't Code § 20017.9, which altered his retirement benefits.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff member of the Public Employee Retirement System brought declaratory judgment action against defendant state of California challenging the constitutionality of Cal. Gov't Code § 20017.9, which altered his retirement benefits by reclassifying him from a warden member to a state safety member. The trial court granted summary judgment to defendant, apparently declaiming § 20017.9 valid. In reversing, the court held that § 20017.9 changed the retirement law in such a way as to diminish plaintiff's earned pension rights, and therefore violated constitutional prohibitions against contract impairment. Because the law, although valid in its general application, was unconstitutional when applied to plaintiff, the court remanded with directions to issue a declaratory judgment for plaintiff entitling him to his original retirement benefits as a warden member.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded with directions to enter a declaratory judgment that plaintiff was entitled to his original retirement benefits, because as applied to plaintiff, forced alteration of his retirement benefits offended the constitutional prohibition against contract impairment.

People v. Cramblit


Crim. No. 8656


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


62 Cal. App. 3d 475; 133 Cal. Rptr. 232; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1923



September 30, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 24, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 47988, Joseph A. DeCristoforo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court erred in setting aside the information. The order is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted defendants' motion to set aside an information charging defendants with violations of Cal. Penal Code § 496a, failing to use due diligence to ascertain the authority of persons seeking to sell metal parts ordinarily used by public utilities.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with violating Cal. Penal Code § 496a, which imposed criminal penalties on secondhand and junk dealers who failed to use due diligence to ascertain the authority of persons seeking to sell items ordinarily used by public utilities. The trial court granted defendants' motion to set aside the information based on defendants' contentions that § 496a should have required proof that the property was stolen because it would be void for vagueness if construed to apply to goods not stolen. The court held that § 496a did not require property to be stolen or any knowledge thereof, but that the gravamen of the violation was failure to exercise due diligence to ascertain before purchase the seller's authority to sell. Further, ignorance of § 496a was not an excuse and the statute language was not vague, therefore the court held that the trial court erred in setting aside the information.


OUTCOME: The court reversed. Failure to exercise due diligence to ascertain the authority of sellers before buying property that did not require knowledge of the stolen character of the property was a valid basis for the charges against defendants.

 

2109 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Yoshimura


Crim. No. 15078, Civ. No. 39891


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


62 Cal. App. 3d 410; 133 Cal. Rptr. 228; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1917



September 29, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of respondent Yoshimura for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 18, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 52904, Lionel J. Wilson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The purported appeal is dismissed. A peremptory writ will issue directing respondeat superior court to set aside its order sustaining the demurrer.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution challenged by a writ of mandate the order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which sustained defendant's demurrer on grounds of vagueness to a count charging her with possessing substances and materials with the intent to make explosive or destructive devices.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged under Cal. Penal Code § 12312 with possessing a "substance or material" with the intent to make a destructive device or explosive. The trial court granted defendant's demurrer based on the vagueness of "substance" and "material," terms undefined in the statute. The prosecution sought a writ of mandate. The court issued a writ directing the trial court to set aside its order sustaining the demurrer. The court held that § 12312 punished possession of substances and materials only with the specific intent to make a destructive device or explosive. The court held that the statute did not punish innocent possession, but only possession that was an integral part of a person's specific intent to make a prohibited device. The count adequately placed defendant on notice of the nature of the offense and was not void for vagueness or violative of due process.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate requiring the trial court to set aside its grant of defendant's demurrer. The terms "substances" and "materials" in the indictment were not too vague to put defendant on notice of the nature of the charge.

People v. Chapman


Crim. No. 8336


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION One


62 Cal. App. 3d 251; 132 Cal. Rptr. 831; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1903



September 24, 1976 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Nov. 24, 1976 (see 21 C.3d 124)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of a California trial court, which denied his motion for destruction of records pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11361.5(b). Defendant pleaded guilty to possession of marijuana and was on probation at the time his motion was denied.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to possession and was given probation. While on probation he brought a motion pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11361.5(b) for destruction of records that related to his conviction. The trial court denied his motion on the ground that the legislative intent of saving him from the stigma of his past violation did not intend to save him from the penalty that was imposed that he had not yet served. Defendant appealed and on review the court held that § 11361.5(b) was unconstitutional insofar as it was interpreted to compel the destruction of any court record that pertained to a conviction, judgment, or commitment in which there was an unexpired sentence or a grant of probation that had not yet been completed. The court affirmed and held that defendant was estopped from asserting any right under § 11361.5(b) until his probation sentence was completed or if he violated probation, until he completed that sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of defendant motion for destruction of records. The court found that the statute was unconstitutional insofar as it was interpreted to compel the destruction of defendant's records that pertained to his unexpired probation sentence for his conviction of possession.

In re Stanley


Crim. No. 28341


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


62 Cal. App. 3d 71; 131 Cal. Rptr. 608; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1881



September 16, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 6, 1976, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Ashby, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 24, 1976. Clark, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The writ is granted. Petitioner is remanded to the custody of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County for purposes of sentencing in conformity with the provisions of section 707.2 of the Welfare and Institutions Code and with views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner juvenile sought a writ of habeas corpus, arguing that he would suffer irreparable harm if forced to remain in prison in contravention of the newly enacted Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707.2 pending a lengthy appeal. The superior court (California) refused to commit petitioner to the California Youth Authority, sentencing him instead to state prison pursuant to petitioner's guilty plea for second degree murder and grand theft.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner juvenile was charged with murder and robbery. Pursuant to a plea bargain, petitioner pled guilty to second degree murder and to grant theft from a person. As a juvenile, petitioner was subject to the provisions of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707.2, a newly enacted law protecting juveniles. The trial court refused to commit petitioner to the California Youth Authority, and sentenced him instead to state prison for the term prescribed on each count. Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus contending that he would suffer irreparable harm if forced to remain in prison pending an appeal. The court granted the writ and petitioner was remanded to the custody of the superior court for purposes of sentencing in conformity with the provisions of § 707.2. The court concluded that § 707.2 was not unconstitutional insofar as it prohibited a judge from sentencing a juvenile criminal defendant to state prison. As such, it did not constitute an infringement on the judicial sentencing function. The court held that the trial court could not, following a negotiated plea, impose a sentence in excess of the maximum allowable for the plea entered.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner juvenile a writ of habeas corpus and petitioner was remanded to the custody of the superior court for resentencing. The court concluded that the trial court had no alternative at the sentencing hearing but to pronounce sentence in accordance with the petitioner's plea agreement and the court could not impose a sentence in excess of that plea.

 

2112 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Yoshimura


Crim. No. 15078


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Four


61 Cal. App. 3d 832; 132 Cal. Rptr. 894; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1862



September 14, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Withdrawn from the Bound Volume.
Vacated and set aside, new opinion filed Sept. 29, 1976; See 62 Cal. App. 3d 410 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant people sought review of an order from the trial court (California), that sustained a demurrer to a count of an indictment which charged respondent and three codefendants with violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12312, possession of materials with the intent to make explosive or destructive devices. The trial court sustained the demurrer on the basis that the words "substance" and "material" were too vague effectively to give notice.


OVERVIEW: Respondent charged with possession of materials with the intent to make explosive or destructive devices, demurrer was sustained on the basis that the words "substance" and "material" were too vague effectively to give notice. Appellant people sought review and contended that the trial court erred in sustaining the demurrer because Cal. Penal Code § 12312 was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad. On review the court found that when the statute was read as a whole, the vagueness of the meaning of "substance" and "material" was eliminated, and the terms were seen to refer to constituent or necessary items in the construction of non-licensed destructive devices and explosives. The court held that the transcript of the testimony before the grand jury provided abundant specific identification of the materials and substances alleged to have been in the possession of respondent. The court held that there was adequate notice of the charge of violation of § 12312. The court concluded that respondent correctly pointed out, that the order sustaining the demurrer was not an appealable order within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 1238. The appeal was dismissed.


OUTCOME: The appeal was dismissed because the order sustaining the demurrer as to a count of an indictment which charged respondent with possession of materials with the intent to make explosive or destructive devices was not an appealable order.

Eden v. Van Tine
