CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homebuyers appealed from two judgments of dismissal entered by the trial court (California) following motions for summary judgment by defendant developers.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff homebuyers sought review from the trial court's summary judgment in favor of defendant developers. After plaintiffs had taken possession of the subject premises, they discovered that a substantial section of a patio wall had failed and toppled down a slope, a fact which sellers and brokers had concealed from them. Plaintiffs filed an action seeking damages against based on theories of negligence and strict liability in the development and construction of the subject property. The trial court granted summary judgment for defendants because plaintiffs' cause of action was barred by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 337.15(a). Levy v. Cohen


Civ. No. 47777


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


61 Cal. App. 3d 788; 132 Cal. Rptr. 594; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1857



September 8, 1976 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Nov. 18, 1976 (see 19 C.3d 165)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant debtors challenged a judgment of the superior court entered in favor of plaintiff creditor in an action seeking payment of two promissory notes.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff creditor loaned money to defendant debtors, general partners in a limited partnership. The limited partnership subsequently filed for bankruptcy and a final order of the bankruptcy court released defendants from all debts, liabilities, and obligations incurred by them on behalf of the limited partnership. Plaintiff, though listed as an unsecured creditor in the bankruptcy schedule, did not challenge the bankruptcy court's jurisdiction to discharge defendants' liability for the obligations of the limited partnership. The court found that the doctrine of res judicata applied to this case because the action sought to enforce a liability which was extinguished by the bankruptcy proceeding, the order of the bankruptcy court was final, and all parties were parties to the bankruptcy proceeding. Because plaintiff had the opportunity to litigate the issues in the bankruptcy court and failed to do so, the court held that the order of the bankruptcy court was conclusive insofar as it determined that defendants' were not liable for the obligations of the limited partnership. The court reversed the judgment of the superior court in favor of plaintiff.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment entered in favor of plaintiff creditor because the order of the bankruptcy court releasing defendant debtors from all debts, liabilities, and obligations incurred by them on behalf of the limited partnership was res judicata in this case.

 

2115 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kuhns


Crim. No. 14439


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


61 Cal. App. 3d 735; 132 Cal. Rptr. 725; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1854



September 8, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 4, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Santa Cruz Judicial District of Santa Cruz County, Nos. CR 24014, CR 24015, CR 24034, CR 24035, CR 24142, CR 24143, Harry F. Brauer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments of the municipal court in actions numbered 24014, 24015, 24034 and 24142 are affirmed, those in actions numbered 24035 and 24143 are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, store owner and clerk appealed convictions on distribution and exhibition of obscene matter under Cal. Penal Code §§ 311, 311.2a in the Municipal Court for Santa Cruz County Judicial District (California). After two U.S. Supreme Court reviews, the first of which resulted in reversal and remand in light of Miller, intermediate state appeal had been deferred until the California Supreme Court decided an identical case regarding § 311.2.


OVERVIEW: Defendants store owner and clerk were convicted for distributing and exhibiting obscene material under Cal. Penal Code §§ 311, 311.2 after undercover police purchased magazines depicting graphic sexual conduct with teenagers and animals. The case twice went to the U.S. Supreme Court, the first time being reversed and remanded. After the California Supreme Court decided a similar case regarding § 311.2 in which it eventually ruled adversely to defendants' interests, the intermediate appeals court rejected appellants' argument that the evidence standard for obscenity in § 311 was unconstitutional. The court found the standard to be utterly without redeeming social importance. The court concluded that materials were obscene when they were being commercially exploited for the sake of prurient appeal and the purveyors' sole interests were in the publications' provocative aspects. The court affirmed four of defendants' six adverse judgments and reversed two judgments against the clerk because merely packaging already purchased materials was not aiding and abetting a crime.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed four of six judgments of conviction against defendants store owner and clerk from the municipal court on three counts of distributing and exhibiting obscene material because the subject of the appeals fell within statutory descriptions of patently offensive representations of hard core sexual conduct. The court reversed two judgments against the clerk because only packaging purchased goods was not aiding and abetting.

People v. Serrata


Crim. No. 14184


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


62 Cal. App. 3d 9; 133 Cal. Rptr. 144; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1875; 84 A.L.R.3d 952



August 30, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified September 23, 1976. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 24, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 56554, Peter Anello, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which convicted defendant on two counts of theft of trade secrets in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 499c(b) and two counts of acceptance of a bribe by an employee in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 499c(c).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted on charges of theft of trade secrets, Cal. Penal Code § 499c(b), and accepting a bribe by an employee, Cal. Penal Code § 499c(c). On appeal, defendant challenged the trial court's denial of a subpoena duces tecum for all notes and reports produced by the corporate victim's private investigators that led to his prosecution, and all formulas and drawings related to 16 computer devices, allegedly to show the trade secrets involved were not truly secret. The court held that discovery could be had against a nonparty witness, but that defendant's requests amounted to a fishing expedition. Defendant's argument that the trade secret statute permitted a monopoly on unpatented technical developments was meritless, as the statute did not prohibit use of trade secrets gained honestly. The court confirmed the statute's constitutionality against defendant's assertion of vagueness, and upheld the statutory presumption that information is secret if its owner takes measures to protect it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

2117 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Armenta v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 48414


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


61 Cal. App. 3d 584; 132 Cal. Rptr. 586; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1837



August 27, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified September 1, 1976.



DISPOSITION: The petition for a peremptory writ of mandate is denied and the alternative writ discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of mandate against the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California) to suppress certain evidence allegedly seized pursuant to an unconstitutional and illegal search under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5(o).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was enrolled in a drug clinic. Another enrollee was used as an informant by the police to conduct a drug sale in which petitioner purchased drugs. Petitioner alleged that because the informant was also enrolled in the clinic, the informant's activities violated state and federal law and deprived petitioner of state and federal constitutional rights. Petitioner alleged that the evidence obtained should have been suppressed. The court held that the search was illegal but not unconstitutional and denied the peremptory writ because the violation did not merit suppression of the evidence. The court held that the informant's conduct did not violate Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5328 because the confidentiality provision related only to records and not to a drug purchase. The court then concluded that the federal statute, 21 U.S.C.S. § 1175, and its regulations, 42 C.F.R. § 2.19, regarding confidentiality of drug abuse patient records, applied to the clinic, and found that the sheriff's department violated § 2.19 when it instructed the informant to make a drug purchase from petitioner, but that such violation did not warrant suppression of the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's writ of mandate to suppress evidence obtained during a drug sale. The court held that although the seller/informant's conduct violated a federal regulation prohibiting enrollment of informants in drug abuse treatment programs by police, the violation did not merit suppression of the evidence.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer appealed from the decision of a California trial court, which dismissed her claim against defendant county regarding the implementation of a solid waste disposal plan by defendant. The trial court held that plaintiff's complaint failed to comply with the validating statutes pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 860- 870.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer filed an action on behalf of herself and others against defendant county alleging that defendant's awarding of a disposal franchise was a conflict of interest, that the notice inviting bids was ambiguous, that the successful bidders were not financially qualified to have been awarded a franchise, that the solid waste management plan was not in compliance with Cal. The court reversed the trial court's ruling on the other causes of action because the validating statutes did not apply to those causes of action.

In re S.

Penal Code § 594, and declared him a ward of the juvenile court pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602.


OVERVIEW: Defendant juvenile went to trial or willful destruction of real property after it was discovered that he had damaged railroad signal boxes. Defendant alleged at trial that Cal. In re Robert P.


Civ. No. 37731


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


61 Cal. App. 3d 310; 132 Cal. Rptr. 5; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1809



August 19, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 17, 1976, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 28, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Mendocino County, No. 4479-J, Timothy W. O'Brien, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed with directions to the trial court to make the necessary finding in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Mendocino County (California), which deprived her of the custody of her minor son and declared him to be a ward of the court, as a dependent child pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 600(b), 600(d).


OVERVIEW: The minor son of appellant mother was placed in foster care by respondent department of social services. The juvenile court declared appellant's son to be a ward of the court, as a dependant child pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 600(b), 600(d). On appeal, the court reversed the juvenile court's judgment. The court held that there was substantial evidence on the record to support the juvenile court's findings that appellant maintained an unsuitable place of abode under § 600(b) and that she neglected her child under § 600(d) by leaving him with a seventeen year old boy for two days. The proper standard for determining the sufficiency of such evidence was the clear and convincing standard, and the court found that there was ample evidence on the record to satisfy the standard. However, the judgment was reversed because the juvenile court failed to make the necessary finding that an award of custody of the minor child to appellant would be detrimental to the child. In addition, the court held that the exclusionary rule was not properly applied in Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 600 proceedings because its application could result in the suffering or deprivation of innocent children.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment declaring appellant mother's son to be a ward of the court because the juvenile court failed to make a finding as required by law that the award of custody of the minor child to appellant would be detrimental to the child.

Long Beach Police Officers Assn. v. City of Long Beach


Civ. No. 47151


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


61 Cal. App. 3d 364; 132 Cal. Rptr. 348; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1814



August 19, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C65989, William H. Levit, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, police officers association, challenged the ruling of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found in favor of defendants, municipality, city manager, and police chief. Plaintiff had filed a declaratory relief action challenging regulations by defendants in an officer's manual that related to display and discharge of firearms by officers.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, police officers association, filed a declaratory relief action challenging a regulation by defendants, municipality, city manager, and police chief, in an officer's manual that related to display and discharge of firearms by officers. Plaintiff appealed the trial court's judgment in favor of defendants, and the court affirmed the judgment. In determining preemption of the regulation by the state, the court looked to the pattern of legislation, the language used in the statutory provisions, and the nature of the subject matter. The court found nothing that indicated that the state intended the statutory provisions to preclude a more cautious use of deadly force by local police agencies than permitted by statute. The court was not persuaded that the regulation conflicted with state law, or that it actually subjected the officers to more restrictive standards than would apply to a private citizen effecting an arrest. The regulation in the police manual was reasonable and within the powers of defendant city manager to prescribe, was not in conflict with or preempted by state law, and did not deny constitutional rights to plaintiff.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of defendants, municipality, city manager, and police chief, because the regulation in the police manual was reasonable within the powers of the city manager to prescribe. The regulation was not in conflict with or preempted by state law, and it did not deny constitutional rights to officers.

 

2122 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Thomson v. City of Glendale


Civ. No. 47265


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


61 Cal. App. 3d 378; 132 Cal. Rptr. 52; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1815



August 19, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 14, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C5437G, J. Wesley Reed, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured party sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held in favor of respondent city in her personal injury and negligence action based on respondent's affirmative defense of design immunity under Cal. Gov't Code § 830.6.


OVERVIEW: Appellant injured party argued that the construction of the central handrail in a city-owned and managed stairway did not meet the requirements of Cal. Gov't Code § 830.6. Appellant contended that § 830.6 did not immunize respondent city from liability caused by negligence independent of design, even though the independent negligence was only a concurring proximate cause. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent because, under § 830.6, respondent was immune from liability for injuries caused by a dangerous condition of its property if the condition was created as a result of a plan or design approved by the city council, which conferred immunity on the city. The evidence supported respondent's contention that the construction was in accordance with the plan or design approved either by the city council or the appropriate employee. Appellant did not plead or prove that respondent had actual or constructive notice of a dangerous condition and failed to warn of that condition or that her injury was caused by negligence independent of the design of the stairway and center handrail.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent city based on the defense of design immunity. The evidence supported the trial court's conclusion that, to the extent the plan or design of the northern stairway, approaching walkway, and central handrail to the city-owned building resulted in the fall that appellant injured party sustained, neither respondent nor any of its employees were liable.

 

2123 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Arp v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


Civ. No. 14730


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION One


61 Cal. App. 3d 296; 132 Cal. Rptr. 112; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1808



August 18, 1976 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified Aug. 25, 1976
Hg. granted Oct. 14, 1976 (See 19 C.3d 395)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent surviving spouse requested a writ of review of the decision of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (California), which denied respondent the benefit of the conclusive presumption of dependency afforded widows under similar circumstances by Cal. Lab. Code § 3501(a) when respondent's wife died from injuries she sustained in the course and scope of her employment.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner surviving spouse's wife died from injuries sustained in the course and scope of her employment. Petitioner requested a writ of review after respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board denied him the benefit of the conclusive presumption of dependency afforded widows under similar circumstances by Cal. Lab. Code § 3501(a). The court vacated respondent's decision, and found § 3501(a) unconstitutional. The court held that the reason that § 3501(a) unconstitutionally discriminated was because it placed an additional burden upon surviving spouses of women workers. The court concluded that Cal. Lab. Code § 3501, as written was an unconstitutional denial of the right of petitioner to equal protection and that, by denying petitioner the right to the conclusive presumption of dependency granted to similarly situated widows, the statute perpetuated gender-based discrimination without a compelling reason and constituted a denial of petitioner's right to equal protection. The court held that it was appropriate for widowers and female workers to be entitled to the benefits of the conclusive presumption contained in Cal. Lab. Code § 3501(a).


OUTCOME: The court vacated the decision of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board which denied petitioner surviving spouse the conclusive presumption of dependency in a workers' compensation action. The court held that petitioner was unconstitutionally discriminated against in that the statute placed an additional burden on surviving spouses of women workers.

Carl v. City of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 46223


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Five


61 Cal. App. 3d 265; 132 Cal. Rptr. 365; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1805



August 18, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Hg. den. Nov. 12, 1976


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered summary judgment in favor of defendant city. Plaintiff filed a taxpayer's action for declaratory and injunctive relief that challenged the constitutionality of Los Angeles City Ordinance No. 145,948, which forbade the use of unattended newsracks to sell material with any adult nudity.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city enacted an ordinance that prohibited the use of newsracks for the sale of material with any adult nudity. Plaintiff taxpayer filed a taxpayer's action for declaratory and injunctive relief and challenged the constitutionality of the ordinance. The trial court granted defendant's motion for summary judgment. The court reversed and held that the trial court focused on two subsections of the statute, one that prohibited the sale of "harmful matter" as defined in Cal. Penal Code § 313, and one that prohibited any depiction of adult nudity. The court held that the "harmful matter" subsection of the ordinance was preempted by Cal. Penal Code § 313.1 and, therefore, the subsection was invalid. The court held that the adult nudity subsection was a major incursion into constitutionally protected expression because it categorically prohibited nudity, which was not enough by itself to render material obscene.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment in favor of defendant city and directed the trial court to enter judgment in favor of plaintiff taxpayer because defendant's ordinance was preempted by state law prohibiting obscenity, and constituted a major incursion into constitutionally protected expression.

 

2125 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


O'Brien v. Superior Court


Civ. Nos. 15715, 15729, 15740, 15786


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


61 Cal. App. 3d 62; 132 Cal. Rptr. 13; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1796



August 16, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 2, 1976, and the petition of the real parties in interest in No. 15715 and application of the petitioners in Nos. 15729, 15740 and 15786 for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 14, 1976.



DISPOSITION: Consistent with the views stated above, we make the following dispositions: In number 4 Civil 15715 the writ of prohibition is amended to delete all references to proceedings in the superior court other than case number 167328 and as so amended the petition's request for a peremptory writ of prohibition is granted; in numbers 4 Civil 15729, 4 Civil 15740, and 4 Civil 15786, the peremptory writs are denied, the alternative writs and orders to show cause heretofore issued are discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This opinion decides four cases. In one case, petitioners, a medical director and the Department of Health, sought a writ of prohibition to prevent respondent Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) from granting jury trials to mentally retarded persons involuntarily confined at a state hospital. In the other cases, petitioner mental patients sought release from custody via writs of habeas corpus, which were denied by respondent.


OVERVIEW: Four writ proceedings were decided in a consolidated opinion, each of which involved the issue of whether there was a right to jury trial in a habeas corpus proceeding brought by a person civilly committed as mentally retarded pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6500 et seq. After issuing alternative writs in each case, the court first noted that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6500.1 provided that no allegedly mentally retarded person could be committed for a period of more than one year without a recommitment proceeding. The court then held that such persons were entitled, upon request, to a jury trial at their annual recommitment proceeding pursuant to § 6500 et seq and that the right to a jury trial at that proceeding satisfied the allegedly mentally retarded persons' equal protection rights under the law. However, because there was no statutory basis for ordering jury trials in special habeas corpus proceedings under Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 38120- 38121 or in traditional habeas corpus proceedings and because equal protection did not mandate it, the court concluded that such proceedings were to be decided without a jury.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition for writ of prohibition and denied the petitions for writ of habeas corpus in the four respective cases. The court found that the mental patients in the first case and petitioner mental patients in the other three cases did not have the right to a jury trial in habeas corpus proceedings other than their annual recommitment proceeding.

 

2126 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Manson


Crim. Nos. 22239, 24376


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION One


61 Cal. App. 3d 102; 132 Cal. Rptr. 265; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1800



August 13, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Hg. den. Dec. 9, 1976



DISPOSITION: The petition of defendant Charles Manson for rehearing is denied September 10, 1976.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Three defendants automatically appealed their judgments of conviction and death penalties imposed in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for seven counts of first degree murder and one count of conspiracy to commit murder. One defendant automatically appealed her judgment of conviction and death penalty for two counts of first degree murder and one count of conspiracy to commit murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendants conspired to murder and did murder seven victims. Defendants were charged with and convicted of one count of conspiracy to murder and seven counts of first degree murder for which all defendants were sentenced to death. The death sentences were voided on automatic appeal and the case was transferred to the appellate court. The court reversed one defendant's conviction because her counsel's late entry into the case precluded counsel's impeaching witness credibility during closing argument or establishing a continuity of representation. The remaining defendants' convictions were affirmed. Evidence as to each defendant was sufficient under Cal. Penal Code § 1111 to corroborate an immunized witness' testimony and thereby support defendants' convictions. Defendants were properly precluded from testifying over their attorneys' objections because defendants refused to proffer their testimony outside of the jury's presence to prevent the introduction of inadmissible evidence. Extensive publicity did not deprive defendants of a fair trial because there was no evidence to support the finding under either a presumptive prejudice or totality of the circumstances standard.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of conviction for three defendants because sufficient evidence corroborated an immunized witness' testimony and publicity did not deprive defendants of a fair and impartial trial; one defendant's conviction was reversed because her counsel entered the proceedings so late as to deny defendant effective representation of counsel.

 

2127 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Yoshimura


Crim. No. 15078


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Four


60 Cal. App. 3d 879; 132 Cal. Rptr. 148; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1781



August 10, 1976 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing granted Sept. 8, 1976 (see 62 C.A.3d 410)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from an order of the trial court (California), that sustained a demurrer to a count of an indictment which charged defendant with possession of materials with the intent to make explosive or destructive devices in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12312.


OVERVIEW: The state charged defendant with possession of materials with the intent to make explosive or destructive devices in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12312. The indictment alleged that defendant had possessed a substance, material, and combination of substances and materials with the intent to make a destructive device and explosive. The trial court sustained a demurrer by defendant on the basis that the words "substance" and "material" were too vague effectively to give defendant notice of the charge against her. On appeal, the court reversed the order sustaining the demurrer. The court held that the count charging violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12312 used the exact language of the statute and referred specifically to the section. The transcript of the testimony before the grand jury provided abundant specific identification of the materials and substances alleged to have been in the possession of defendant. Defendant was given notice. Where punishment imposed was only for an act knowingly done with the purpose of doing that which the statute prohibited, defendant could not be said to suffer from lack of warning or knowledge that the act which she committed was a violation of law.


OUTCOME: The order sustaining the demurrer was reversed. The court held that defendant had adequate notice of the charge because the count charging defendant with violation of the applicable statute used the exact language of the statute and referred specifically to the section, and the testimony before the grand jury provided abundant specific identification of the materials and substances alleged to have been in defendant's possession.

Cowing v. City of Torrance


Civ. No. 47002


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


60 Cal. App. 3d 757; 131 Cal. Rptr. 830; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1769; 87 A.L.R.3d 137



August 5, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied October 21, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SWC26765, Abraham Gorenfeld, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent city, in an action involving appellant's rights against unreasonable searches and seizures under the U.S. Const. amend. IV.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owner brought an action against respondent city for alleged violation of appellant's constitutional right against unreasonable searches and seizures under the U.S. Const. amend. IV. Appellant claimed that respondent had no authority to inspect appellant's premises without a warrant. Respondent argued that it had the authority to inspect appellant's premises to determine whether appellant had the appropriate permit or license from respondent for the vending machines. The lower court granted summary judgment in favor of respondent. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent, holding that there was no constitutional violation by respondent when it inspected appellant's premises to check for appropriate permits. The court found that by operating vending machines, even if limited to the use by occupants and guests of the apartment house, appellant was carrying on a vending machine business and became liable to inspection under the city ordinance. The court found that the city ordinance was reasonable and constitutional. The court found that the inspector was given and had authority to inspect such business for revenue purposes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent city. The court held that under the applicable ordinance, respondent was given and had authority to inspect appellant property owner's business to determine whether he had the appropriate license for vending machines, and appellant's constitutional right against unreasonable searches and seizures was not violated.

Newby v. Alto Riviera Apartments


Civ. No. 36556


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


60 Cal. App. 3d 288; 131 Cal. Rptr. 547; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1725



July 20, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. P25396, John E. Longinotti, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed as to the cause of action for intentional infliction of emotional distress. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed. Appellant will recover her costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant tenant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court, Santa Clara County (California), which granted the motions for judgment of nonsuit and judgment, under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 631.8, by respondents, apartment complex, its owners, and managers, against appellant's suit seeking damages as well as declaratory and injunctive relief in respondents' attempt to evict appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant tenant filed suit against respondents, apartment complex and its owners and managers, seeking damages and injunctive relief related to respondents' attempt to evict appellant. The court held that the service upon appellant of a notice to quit the premises, without more, did not implicate a state action that would give appellant constitutional protection from eviction for exercising her right to speech. Additionally, the court held that, absent an unlawful detainer action, respondents' efforts to evict appellant did not implicate a state action that would render them liable under the Unruh Civil Rights Act, Cal. Civ. Code §§ 51, 52. However, the court held that appellant presented substantial evidence to support her claim for intentional infliction of emotional distress based on respondent's outrageous conduct and emotional damage appellant suffered. Thus, the court held the trial court erred in failing to submit the issue to the jury. The court then held that the trial court's error in failing to render findings of fact in denying appellant's motion for injunctive relief was harmless, as there was not substantial evidence to support appellant's position on the claim.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order granting the motion for nonsuit by respondents, apartment complex, owners, and managers, against appellant tenant's claim for intentional infliction of emotional distress because appellant had presented substantial evidence to support the claim. However, the court affirmed the trial court's order granting judgment against appellant on her other causes of action related to respondents' attempt to evict appellant.

 

2131 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Reyes


Crim. No. 27742


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


60 Cal. App. 3d 227; 131 Cal. Rptr. 340; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1716



July 16, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied September 8, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-437662, William E. McGinley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed insofar as it adjudicated defendant's guilt. The sentence imposed is vacated and the case is remanded to the trial court for resentencing in light of this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction and sentence from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for first degree murder. Defendant asserted that the evidence did not support his conviction, and that the trial court erroneously concluded that he was ineligible for sentencing as a youthful offender under Cal. Penal Code § 1202b.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder. The court affirmed the adjudication of guilt, but vacated the sentence. First, the court held that the evidence was more than adequate to support the conviction. The court rejected defendant's assertion that he entered the premises out of curiosity, rather than with the intent to commit a felony, and that he was under the influence of alcohol and drugs. While the evidence argued might have persuaded the trial court to have a reasonable doubt concerning defendant's intent at the time he entered the premises and his capacity to harbor any such intent, it was not compelling. Next, the trial court erred when it ruled that it had no discretion to sentence defendant as a youthful offender under Cal. Penal Code § 1202b, because the last sentence of that section was inapplicable because no special circumstances were alleged and the only punishment faced by defendant was life imprisonment. Thus, the court remanded for resentencing under § 1202b.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's adjudication of guilt, and held that the evidence was sufficient to support his conviction for first degree murder. However, the court vacated the sentence and remanded for resentencing. The court held that the trial court erred when it concluded that it could not sentence defendant as a youthful offender because the only punishment defendant faced was life imprisonment, and not death.

 

2132 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Homola v. City Products


Civ. No. 46155


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Four


60 Cal. App. 3d 69; 131 Cal. Rptr. 291; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1702



July 14, 1976 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] Hg. den. July 14, 1976 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in Official Appellate Reports (Const., art VI, § 14, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant creditor appealed a judgment, based on a jury verdict, of a California superior court, which found in favor of plaintiff debtor and awarded compensatory and punitive damages in plaintiff's action for wrongful attachment and malicious prosecution.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff debtor agreed to buy furniture from a private party and assume the debt, but an assign-and-assume form was never executed. Defendant creditor added plaintiff's name to the account and assigned it to a collection agency, which sued plaintiff. The municipal court found for plaintiff, who then filed a malicious prosecution and wrongful attachment action against defendant and the collection agency. The jury awarded plaintiff compensatory and punitive damages. On appeal, the court affirmed after it modified the amount of punitive damages because the award was excessive. The evidence supported the elements of malicious prosecution because defendant had the right to control the collection agency and malice in fact was inferred from the lack of probable cause. The lack was shown by the fact that the collection manager who assigned the account had authority to bind defendant and the assignment was without any reasonable or sincere belief that plaintiff was a debtor of defendant. Plaintiff's showing in opposition to defendant's motion to dismiss was not entirely absent of any showing that constituted good cause for delay and did not mandate a finding of abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of malicious prosecution after it was modified because the amount of punitive damages was excessive. The court found that the lack of probable cause was shown by the fact that defendant creditor made the assignment that plaintiff debtor owed defendant when it had full knowledge of the fact that plaintiff had not signed the form which was necessary to create any liability, and malice in fact was inferred.

 

2133 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Layton v. Merit System Commission


Civ. No. 47831


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


60 Cal. App. 3d 58; 131 Cal. Rptr. 318; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1701



July 14, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C133448, Norman R. Dowds, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order of dismissal) is reversed. Since the answer to the petition raises no issue of fact, the trial court is instructed to grant the petitioner the relief prayed for in the petition.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee challenged the order of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed petitioner's claim to set aside her suspension and reimburse her withheld salary. The pre-litigation grievance procedure placed the burden of proof on petitioner to prove her innocence, and respondent merit commission found in favor of the employer.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employee was suspended from her job for two days. The grievance procedure placed the burden of proof on petitioner to prove her innocence, and respondent merit commission found in favor of the employer. Petitioner filed suit to set aside the suspension and reimburse withheld salary. The trial court dismissed the claim pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 581. The court reversed and held that the city administrator or respondent should have conducted a hearing in which the appointing authority had the burden of proof to justify petitioner's suspension. The court held that petitioner did not receive a hearing before respondent or the city administrator at which evidence was adduced and at which the burden of proof was assigned to the appointing authority; therefore, petitioner's suspension could not be sustained. The court held that the letter to petitioner by her department head that informed her of the charges was not within the department head's personal knowledge, but had been relayed to him by others. Therefore, the letter was hearsay twice removed and not admissible as evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that dismissed petitioner employee's claim to set aside her suspension and reimburse her withheld salary. Respondent merit commission's finding in favor of the employer could not be sustained because petitioner was denied a hearing in which the burden of proof was on the employer to substantiate the charges. The letter informing petitioner of the charges was hearsay within hearsay and not admissible.

Meredith v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


Civ. No. 15709


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Two


60 Cal. App. 3d 26; 131 Cal. Rptr. 449; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1697



July 13, 1976 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Oct. 21, 1976 (see 19 C.3d 777)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought review of an opinion and order denying reconsideration by the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (California). Petitioner was a convicted felon that was injured and became a paraplegic when a tree that was being freed after cutting fell on petitioner.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was a convicted felon was injured when a tree fell on him rendering him a paraplegic. He petitioned for workers compensation, but was denied. On appeal, the court found that no case had held that prisoners comprised a suspect class nor had the right to receive workmen's compensation. Hence, the strict scrutiny analysis was not applicable to the denial of workmen's compensation to prisoners. Because there was a rational basis for the distinction between state prisoners and other persons deemed public employees, the court held that the statutory provisions disallowing the payment of compensation to a state prisoner during the period of his incarceration were constitutional. The board's opinion and order that denied reconsideration was annulled, and the matter remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the order that denied reconsideration of workers' compensation benefits. There was a rational basis for the distinction between state prisoners and other persons deemed public employees. The court held that the statutory provisions disallowing the payment of compensation to a state prisoner during the period of his incarceration were constitutional, and remanded the matter.

Bussell v. Union Bank


Civ. No. 15651


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


59 Cal. App. 3d 990; 131 Cal. Rptr. 207; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1691



July 12, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 4, 1976, and Respondents' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied October 6, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of El Dorado County, No. 24767, Prentiss Moore, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The summary judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court for trial on the merits. 4



4 In reaching our decision in this case we have not been unmindful of the recent decision of the California Supreme Court in the case of Becker v. Lindsay (1976) 16 Cal.3d 188 [127 Cal.Rptr. 348, 545 P.2d 260]. Based upon supplemental letter briefs filed by the parties at our request, and upon our own study of the Becker case, we have concluded that it has no application to any issue in the instant case for two reasons: (1) Becker involves no issue of retroactivity of the Randone decision, and (2) Becker applies only to cases in which a declaration of homestead is involved. (See 16 Cal.3d at p. 193, fn. 6, and p. 194, fn. 7.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant judgment creditor challenged an order from the Superior Court of El Dorado County (California), where respondent property owners' motion for summary judgment was granted. The court found that appellant's judgment lien was null and void.


OVERVIEW: Respondent property owners brought a quiet title action upon discovering that a lien was placed on property purchased by respondents. Respondents filed a motion for summary judgment asserting that the lien was void because Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 537 was unconstitutional. Respondents also asserted that the lien applied only to the seller's interest because equitable title had passed prior to the attachment. The trial court granted respondents' motion finding the attachment void because of the unconstitutional nature of section 537. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment and remanded the case. According to the court, the trial court erred by retroactively applying the ruling that all prejudgment attachments under section 537 were void. The court held that while section 537 was found to be unconstitutional, it did not mean that all prejudgment attachments that had ripened into a judgment lien were invalid. The court stated that the nullity rule of prejudgment attachments did not apply retroactively so as to nullify prior attachment procedures which culminated in judgment liens. Thus, the trial court erred in finding the judgment lien void.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment in respondent property owners' favor and remanded the case because the trial court erred in finding that the judgment lien was void merely because the statutory prejudgment attachment procedures were declared unconstitutional.

Smith v. Municipal Court for Los Angeles Judicial Dist.


Civ. No. 47012


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Two


59 Cal. App. 3d 935; 131 Cal. Rptr. 200; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1686



July 7, 1976 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] opinion deleted on direction of Supreme Court by order dated September 3, 1976.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged the issuance of a writ by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), directing defendant municipal court to vacate plaintiff's prior judgment of conviction for driving while intoxicated pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 23102(a), based on his plea of guilty.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sought to invalidate his prior conviction for driving while under the influence of intoxicating liquor in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23102(a) because he faced another charge for the same offense. The superior court granted plaintiff's request and issued a writ directing defendant municipal court to vacate plaintiff's prior judgment of conviction based on his plea of guilty. The state appealed. The court reversed and remanded, holding that plaintiff was fairly and properly treated by constitutional standards when he entered his plea of guilty in the prior case and that his plea was valid. The court held that the record belied plaintiff's claims of alleged constitutional deficiencies in the municipal court proceedings. The court found that the record showed that plaintiff clearly, knowingly, and intelligently waived the right to counsel. His allegation of the absence of counsel was without merit. The record of the proceedings showed defendant municipal court carefully asked plaintiff if he understood that by his plea he was waiving his right to a jury trial, to confrontation of witnesses, and against self-incrimination. The superior court was ordered to recall the writ.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the judgment of the superior court, which issued a writ of mandate directing defendant municipal court to vacate plaintiff's prior conviction for driving while intoxicated, and ordered the superior court to recall the writ. The court found that the municipal proceedings showed no violations of plaintiff's constitutional rights and that he knowingly and freely entered his guilty plea.

Civil Service Employees Ins. Co. v. Klapper


Civ. No. 37350


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


59 Cal. App. 3d 918; 130 Cal. Rptr. 921; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1684



July 6, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Petition of the Defendants and Respondents for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied September 3, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 142849, Jackson C. Davis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurer sought review of a judgment of the Contra Costa County Superior Court (California), which entered judgment in favor of respondent insured and his children in appellant's declaratory action for a determination of whether it was obligated to defend respondent insured in an action pending against him following an accident in which the insured's wife was fatally injured.


OVERVIEW: Respondent insured obtained an automobile insurance policy from appellant insurer. Respondent's wife was killed following an automobile accident for which respondent was allegedly partially responsible because of his negligence. Respondent's children sued respondent, the other driver, and various other parties in a wrongful death action. Appellant refused to defend and contended that the family and household exclusion in respondent's policy precluded recovery. Appellant then filed an action for declaratory relief against respondent insured and his children and sought a judicial determination that it did not have to cover the liability of the insured. The lower court entered judgment in respondents' favor. On appeal, the court reversed. The court reasoned that the exclusion was not ambiguous and that the statute authorizing the exclusion, Cal. Ins. Code § 11580.1(c)(5), was not unconstitutional. The court determined that respondents failed to meet their burden to show that the legislation was irrational or that the purpose furthered by the legislation was not a legitimate legislative concern.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the lower court, which entered judgment in respondents' favor, was reversed, because the contested exclusion policy was not ambiguous, nor was it unconstitutional for equal protection reasons. There was no requirement that all liability for personal injury be covered by insurance.

 

2138 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Regents of University of Cal. v. Hartford Acci. & Indem. Co.


Civ. No. 37480


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION One


59 Cal. App. 3d 675; 131 Cal. Rptr. 112; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1664



June 30, 1976 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Sept. 15, 1976 (see 21 C.3d 624)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, owner of student housing, challenged a decision of the trial court (California), which granted summary judgment for defendant surety company pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 337.15, in an action to recover damages for a latent deficiency in the design and construction of the property. Plaintiff contended that § 337.15 was inapplicable and that there were triable issues of fact concerning defendant's obligation on performance bond.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, owner of student housing, sought damages from defendant surety company because of latent defects in the design and construction of the apartments. The trial court granted summary judgment to defendant under the provisions of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 337.15, which prescribed a 10-year period of limitation for an action to recover damages for latent deficiency. Plaintiff appealed, contending that § 337.15 was inapplicable because the statute by its terms did not expressly include causes of action that had accrued prior to its effective date, because application of the statute failed to allow plaintiff a reasonable time within which to sue, and because the obligation of defendant on its bond is severable and independent. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, finding that § 337.15 was applicable and noted that the only effect of the statute was to shorten the time in which the cause of action could be asserted. The court, rejecting the attack on the constitutionality of the statute, stated that § 337.15 was controlling because, although the action pleaded was not barred under prior law, the action was not brought until § 337.15 was in effect.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision, finding that the statute prescribing a 10-year period of limitation for an action to recover damages for latent deficiency in the design or construction of the property was applicable. The court stated that the action that was pleaded was not barred under prior law, but the action was not filed prior to the effective date of the new statute and therefore, the new statute was applicable.

 

2139 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Pinheiro v. County of Marin


Civ. No. 37819


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


60 Cal. App. 3d 323; 131 Cal. Rptr. 633; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1728



June 30, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 19, 1976, and Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 26, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 75368, E. Warren McGuire, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which granted respondent county's demurrer without leave to amend and dismissed a complaint for inverse condemnation in which appellant alleged that the rezoning of their property for the purpose of acquiring open space reduced its value and was a taking for which compensation was due.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landowners filed an inverse condemnation action against respondent county, asserting that the rezoning of their property was for the purpose of acquiring open space and that the effect of the ordinance constituted was a taking for which compensation was due. At no time did appellants indicate that they wished to amend the complaint. The trial court sustained the respondent's demurrer without leave to amend. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that appellants failed to allege precondemnation activities, or the lack of any remaining reasonable beneficial use, or any public use of their property, as required for consideration of the relief requested. There was no set formula to determine where regulation ended and taking began. A cause of action for inverse condemnation would not lie where there was a mere reduction in the value of property resulting from a zoning ordinance, as there was not a compensable injury. The purpose or motive of the city officials in passing the ordinance was irrelevant to any inquiry concerning the reasonableness of the ordinance.


OUTCOME: A judgment to sustain a demurrer without leave to amend was affirmed because a mere reduction in the value of property resulting from a downsizing ordinance did not create a compensable injury, and a cause of action for inverse condemnation would not lie.

 

2140 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Mikkelsen v. State of California


Civ. No. 47540


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


59 Cal. App. 3d 621; 130 Cal. Rptr. 780; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1659



June 29, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied September 15, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. NW C 25425, NW C 26152, Raymond R. Roberts, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) declining to allow plaintiffs to proceed on the theory of nuisance after finding in favor of the state with respect to the issue of design immunity pursuant to the Tort Claims Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 830.6.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs sued the state after certain individuals were killed when a bridge collapsed during an earthquake. The trial court entered judgment in the state's favor on the affirmative defense of immunity pursuant to the Tort Claims Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 830.6 (Act), and refused to permit plaintiffs to proceed on the theory of nuisance. The court of appeals affirmed. Plaintiffs conceded that their nuisance claim rested solely upon alleged negligence with respect to the design of the bridge. Immunity granted by the Act could not be avoided by pleading a cause of action in nuisance rather than in negligence. The Act was a specific statute regulating the tort liabilities and immunities of public entities while the nuisance statute, Cal. Civ. Code § 3479, was a general statute providing for damages in mandamus proceeding which embraced a great variety of legal actions. The special statute controlled and took priority over the general.


OUTCOME: The order declining to allow plaintiffs to proceed on the theory of nuisance was affirmed because plaintiffs' nuisance claim was merely a veiled negligence claim as to the city's allegedly defective plan or design of a bridge.

 

2141 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Cansdale v. Board of Administration


Civ. No. 46462


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


59 Cal. App. 3d 656; 130 Cal. Rptr. 880; 41 Cal. Comp. Cases 998; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1662



June 29, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 79262, August J. Goebel, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed; Cansdale's attempted appeal from the order denying his motion for a new trial is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that denied a peremptory writ of mandate and affirmed a decision of appellee Board of Administration, Public Employees' Retirement System that terminated appellant's disability retirement.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee received a disability retirement and remained inactive for three years. After reexamination, appellee board found appellant was capable of resuming his duties. Appellant's retirement was cancelled pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 21029. Appellant sought review of that order and requested a hearing. After consideration, appellee cancelled appellant's disability retirement. Appellant's petition for a writ of mandate was denied by the lower court. Appellant sought review. The court was required to determine whether appellee's decision was supported by substantial evidence. Cal. Lab. Code § 5952. The court found substantial evidence to conclude appellant's disability benefits were properly cancelled because there was testimony that appellant could perform all of the duties of his employment and that there was no evidence of a disability. Appellant was not entitled to recover benefits from the time they were cancelled to the time appellee made its final decision because appellant was entitled to reinstatement and nothing in the record suggested that appellant was prevented from appearing at work and performing tasks consistent with the assessment of his condition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order of the lower court that denied a peremptory writ of mandate and affirmed appellee board's termination of appellant's disability retirement benefits because there was substantial evidence to support the decision that appellant could return to work. Appellant was not entitled to payment of benefits until appellee's final order was entered because he did not present evidence that he was prevented from returning to work.

 

2142 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Adoption of Coffee


Civ. No. 47701


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


59 Cal. App. 3d 593; 130 Cal. Rptr. 887; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1634



June 28, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. AD 87717, Lester E. Olson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with the foregoing instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) approving the adoption of his minor son by respondent, the boy's stepfather. The court found that for more than one year, appellant willfully failed to pay for child support when able to do so; therefore, the father's consent to the adoption was not necessary under Cal. Civ. Code § 224. Appellant argued that § 224 denied him equal protection of the law.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, prison inmate, challenged decision approving adoption of his minor son by respondent, the boy's stepfather. The trial court found that for more than one year, appellant willfully failed to pay for child support when able to do so; therefore, appellant's consent to adoption was unnecessary under Cal. Civ. Code § 224. Appellant argued § 224 denied him equal protection of the law. On appeal, the court reversed. The notion that men were more likely than women to be the primary supporters of their spouses and children was not without empirical support. A statute designed to cushion the financial impact of spousal loss upon the sex for which that loss imposed a disproportionately heavy burden could survive an equal protection attack. Appellant argued that his failure to pay for his son's care, support and education was not "willful" under § 224. The court held that a father who was unable to comply with an order for the payment of child support could not be adjudged in contempt for such noncompliance unless he had voluntarily created the disability for the purpose of avoiding payment. Appellant's failure to make child support payments was not "willful."


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed in an appeal by prison inmate from decision approving the adoption of his minor son by respondent, the boy's stepfather. The court concluded that appellant's failure to make child support payments was not willful because he had not voluntarily created the disability for the purpose of avoiding payment.

 

2143 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Cotton v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 14344


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


59 Cal. App. 3d 601; 130 Cal. Rptr. 876; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1656



June 28, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 12, 1976, and Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 26, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. 355705, 355706, 355707, 355708, William T. Low, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders appealed from are reversed and the superior court is directed to issue the writs as prayed for.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant fathers challenged an order from the lower court, the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied their petitions for writs of prohibition that claimed that Cal. Penal Code § 270 was unconstitutional and thus respondent municipal court's order finding them guilty pursuant to the statute was improper.


OVERVIEW: Appellant fathers were separately charged in the municipal court with violating Cal. Penal Code § 270, willful failure to provide for minor children. After their challenges to the constitutionality of § 270 were rejected by the municipal court, appellants petitioned the superior court for writs of prohibition; again claiming the statute was unconstitutional. The superior court denied the writs, and appellants sought review. The matters were consolidated on appeal. The court reversed, and the superior court was directed to issue the writs. Section 270, from the time of its enactment in 1872 until it was amended in 1923, made no distinctions between mothers and fathers, and treated parents of minor children equally. The 1974 amendment to the section again made its provisions declaring it unlawful to willfully fail to support a minor child equally applicable to both parents. Section 270, before its amendment in 1974, unconstitutionally discriminated on its face against fathers of minor children as a class. Appellants, as members of the class discriminated against, were entitled to dismissal of the criminal charges.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and directed the superior court to grant the writ of prohibition to the trial court, prohibiting appellant fathers' criminal prosecution for willful failure to provide for minor children because the charging statute was unconstitutional. Prior to the statute's amendment, it treated mothers and fathers who willfully failed to support a minor child differently; thus, it violated appellants' equal protection rights.

State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co. v. Hartle


Civ. No. 37535


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


59 Cal. App. 3d 852; 131 Cal. Rptr. 141; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1678



June 28, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 313770, Peter Anello, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, with directions to enter judgment in favor of appellant State Farm Mutual Automobile Insurance Company.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action for declaratory relief in the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), appellant insurance company challenged a judgment that decreed that it was obligated to provide coverage to the driver as a permissive user of an automobile under a policy issued in favor of respondent, the insured.


OVERVIEW: Appellant issued a policy of automobile liability insurance to respondent. Another driver was driving respondent's automobile with her permission and consent, and respondent was riding as a passenger at the time. The car collided with an unoccupied parked automobile, causing personal injuries to respondent, who subsequently filed an action for personal injuries against the driver. Appellant commenced an action for declaratory relief, contending that its policy did not afford coverage to the driver, as a permissive user of the vehicle, for injury to the owner of the vehicle. The court determined that appellant was obligated to provide coverage to the driver, and appellant filed an appeal. The court reversed the judgment, holding that in view of the statutory definition contained in Cal. Ins. Code § 11580.1(c) permitting exclusions of liability for bodily injury to an insured, and the language of the policy itself, the exclusion clause was clear and unambiguous and fell within the ambit of the permitted exclusion contained in the section referred to. The policy was effective to exclude coverage to the permissive user for bodily injury to the named insured, respondent.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the ruling of the trial court that appellant insurance company was obligated to provide coverage to driver because the policy's exclusionary language expressly excluded from coverage any liability of the driver for injuries sustained by respondent, the named insured.

People v. Superior Court (Felmann)


Civ. No. 48302


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


59 Cal. App. 3d 270; 130 Cal. Rptr. 548; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1642



June 18, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 14, 1976. Hanson, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted. Petitioner's Application for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 18, 1976.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ and stay order are discharged. Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the trial court to rehear and reconsider defendant's conditional plea and to accept it only if to do so involves no consideration of more lenient treatment solely because of the plea.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state petitioned for a writ of mandate challenging an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that allegedly entered into a sentencing bargain with defendant in return for a plea of guilty without the state's consent contrary to Cal. Penal Code § 1192.5.


OVERVIEW: On the day of defendant's trial on several charges of grand theft and forgery, the trial court allegedly engaged in sentencing negotiations with defendant in return for a conditional plea. The prosecutor objected to the trial court's order on the grounds his consent was required pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1192.5. The trial court disregarded the objection and entered the sentencing order. The state petitioned for a writ of review, prohibition or mandate. The court granted the writ of mandate. The court held that under the separation of powers doctrine the trial court had no authority to substitute itself as the representative of the state in the negotiation process and under the guise of "plea bargaining" to "agree" to disposition of a case over prosecutorial objection. A trial court could not offer any inducement in return for a plea of guilty or nolo contendere. Because it was not clear from the record whether the trial court actually engaged in plea negotiations or was simply exercising its sentencing discretion, the matter was remanded for rehearing on defendant's conditional plea.


OUTCOME: The court granted the state's petition for a writ of mandate and remanded the matter for a rehearing on defendant's conditional plea. If the trial court engaged in plea negotiations with defendant, this action was unconstitutional under the separation of powers doctrine as this was a matter reserved for the executive branch. However, the trial court had exclusive discretion to enter a sentencing order.

People v. Williams


Crim. No. 28563


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


59 Cal. App. 3d 225; 130 Cal. Rptr. 460; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1637



June 17, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 12, 1976. Tobriner, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Long Beach Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. M 118549, Kenneth E. Sutherland, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As to the above issues, the judgment is affirmed. However, as noted above, defendant raised several other issues on his appeal. The appellate department of the superior court never considered those issues on their merits because of its determination that instructional error existed. Since we have determined that there was no error in the instructions and in order to facilitate the orderly consideration of this misdemeanor appeal, the cause is hereby retransferred to the appellate department of the superior court for disposition of the remaining issues. (Cf. Taylor v. Union Pac. R. Corp. (1976) 16 Cal.3d 893 [130 Cal.Rptr. 23, 549 P.2d 855].)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the decision of the Municipal Court for the Long Beach Judicial District of Los Angeles (California) that convicted appellant after a jury trial of violating Cal. Penal Code § 647 subsection (a), for lewd or dissolute conduct in any public place. The appellate court of the superior court, upon review, found that an instructional error existed, and the court ordered transfer of the matter under Cal. R. Ct. 62(a).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was arrested in a public restroom in Long Beach, California, after two police officers observed appellant engaging in sexual behavior in the restroom. The jury convicted appellant of a misdemeanor under Cal. Penal Code § 647. The appellate division of the superior court reversed the judgment on the grounds that the jury instructions improperly defined lewd and dissolute conduct and did not instruct the jury on the sexual motivation element of the offense. The court ordered transfer of the matter pursuant to Cal. R. Ct. 62(a) and held that the instructions used by the trial court were not erroneous. The court held that Cal. Jury Instructions Criminal No. 16.402 adequately described the type of behavior proscribed under Cal. Penal Code § 647 subsection (a) and were adequate when there were not First Amendment issues involved in the behavior. The court held that the trial court did not need to instruct specifically upon sexual motivation as an element of the offense because the instructions given informed the jury of the sexual aspects of the offense. The court remanded the case to the appellate division for determination of appellant's other issues raised on appeal.


OUTCOME: The court held that the jury instructions given by the trial court on lewd and dissolute behavior were adequate to describe the sexual nature of the offense and the type of behavior proscribed. The court transferred the case back to the appellate department of the superior court for determination of the other issues on appeal.

Boren v. Department of Employment Dev.


Civ. No. 14138


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


59 Cal. App. 3d 250; 130 Cal. Rptr. 683; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1640



June 17, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 225639, B. Abbott Goldberg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded to the trial court with a direction to enter a judgment for plaintiff consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) that sustained defendants' demurrer against her complaint alleging that denial of her claim for unemployment benefits pursuant to Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1264 was improper because § 1264 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was replaced at the restaurant where she worked after she told her employer she could not work a different shift because she could not find a babysitter. Plaintiff applied for unemployment compensation and was denied benefits pursuant to Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1264, which prohibited compensation for those who left their job for "domestic reasons." Plaintiff filed suit claiming § 1264 was unconstitutional because it discriminated against women. The trial court sustained defendants' demurrer, and plaintiff appealed. The court reversed and remanded with direction to enter a judgment for plaintiff. The court applied a strict scrutiny analysis to § 1264 because it established suspect classifications. For its legality to be sustained, defendants had to show a compelling state interest which necessitated the distinction. A seemingly neutral statute which disqualified a disproportionate number of one sex was discriminatory and vulnerable to the strict scrutiny test; it was enough if statistics showed that the standard affected women only. Absent a showing of a compelling state interest, defendants failed to show that § 1264 was not an invalid denial of equal protection.


OUTCOME: Judgment sustaining defendants' demurrer against plaintiff's complaint alleging that a statute which prohibited unemployment compensation to workers who left their job for domestic reasons was reversed and remanded with direction to enter a judgment for plaintiff. The statute could not withstand a strict scrutiny analysis because defendants failed to produce a compelling state interest which necessitated the classification.

Gibbs v. City of Napa

Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


59 Cal. App. 3d 148; 130 Cal. Rptr. 382; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1619



June 16, 1976

* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, unincorporated association and city, sought review of judgment granting a writ directing a proposed initiative election to not proceed under the Community Redevelopment Law, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 33000 et seq., in favor of respondents in the Superior Court of Napa County (California).


OVERVIEW: Appellants, unincorporated association and city, sought review of judgment which granted a writ prohibiting proceeding with a proposed initiative election to nullify a city redevelopment agency under the Community Redevelopment Law, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 33000 et seq., in favor of respondents. The court affirmed the issuance of the writ, explaining the legislatively created redevelopment agency functioned as an administrative arm of the state that was beyond the reach of a municipal referendum or initiative election. * Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.

Jefferson v. Unemployment Ins. Appeals Bd.

June 14, 1976

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former employee challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied his petition for a writ of mandate seeking to set aside a determination of respondent California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board disqualifying him from unemployment benefits.
