2154 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Channell v. Anthony


Civ. No. 2323


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


58 Cal. App. 3d 290; 129 Cal. Rptr. 704; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1516



May 14, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 155922, Hollis G. Best, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant buyer appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), denying defendant's motions for a new trial and for a judgment notwithstanding the verdict, and entering judgment for plaintiff sellers, after the jury found in favor of plaintiffs in their action for conspiracy to defraud and actual fraud and oppression.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sellers filed an action for fraud, alleging overpersuasion by defendant buyer in the sale of plaintiffs' farm. The jury found in favor of plaintiffs, and the trial court denied defendant's motion for judgment notwithstanding the verdict and motion for a new trial. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that defendant's appeal was timely, because it was within 60 days of the mailing of the notice of the denial of defendant's motion for judgment notwithstanding the verdict. There was no waiver by plaintiffs of fraud, even though after discovery of the fraud they entered into a new contract concerning the same subject matter. This would normally constitute a waiver of the original fraud, but here the act of entering into the second contract was itself induced by defendant's fraud. It was proper to instruct the jury on the right to use and the right to profit from the land, because the contract itself created these rights. Cal. Civ. Code § 3343 (a) 2 and (a)(3) were neither unconstitutionally vague nor uncertain. The damages awarded were well within the allowable additional figure and the award of punitive damages was permissible under Cal. Civ. Code § 3343.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of plaintiff sellers in their action for conspiracy to defraud and actual fraud and oppression. The court found that plaintiffs' action of entering into a new contract with defendant after they discovered the original fraud did not constitute waiver of the original fraud, because the second contract was induced by fraud as well.

 

2155 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Flores


Crim. No. 8337


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


58 Cal. App. 3d 222; 128 Cal. Rptr. 847; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1565



May 13, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 8, 1976.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged, and the petition for a writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus from his conviction by the trial court (California), alleging that the requirement that he serve a minimum of 10 years in prison without the possibility of parole for the crime of sale of heroin to a minor, with one prior felony narcotic conviction, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11502, was cruel and unusual punishment.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of the crime of sale of heroin to a minor and sentenced to a minimum of 10 years in prison without the possibility of parole pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11502. Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus, contending that his sentence was cruel and unusual punishment. The court stated that, in the baseness of crimes involving drug traffic, the sale or furnishing of drugs to minors ranked at the very top. The court did not find that the 10-year minimum sentence was cruel or unusual; the only cruelty the court detected was that inflicted upon the minor victim when heroin was made available for his use. The difference in minimum prison time between a Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11353 sentence, 10 years, and a § 11352 sentence, 3 1/3 years, great as it was, was justified as necessary for the protection of minors. The court denied the writ of habeas corpus.


OUTCOME: The court denied a writ of habeas corpus to petitioner. Petitioner's sentence was justified under statute because the statute served the purpose of protecting minors.

2157 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Bradley v. Doherty


Civ. No. 36100


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION One


57 Cal. App. 3d 963; 129 Cal. Rptr. 496; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1510



May 4, 1976 


NOTICE:

Opinion deleted on direction of Supreme Court by order dated Jul. 8, 1976  NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff gambler appealed the decision of the Superior Court (California), which granted defendant thieves' motion for judgment on the pleadings and dismissed the action. The trial court held plaintiff was in pari delicto with defendants in their illicit gambling game, and he was therefore denied access to the courts in recovering sums taken through the use of crooked gambling paraphernalia .


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff gambler brought suit against defendant thieves admitting that he had engaged with them in prohibited gambling games impliedly represented by them as, and which he believed to be, fairly conducted. In his complaint plaintiff alleged that by the use of certain paraphernalia, which defendants controlled and manipulated, plaintiff was defrauded of a large sum of money. Defendants filed a motion for judgment on the pleadings which the trial court granted. Plaintiff appealed. The court reversed and remanded for further proceedings, finding that the law in California was that one in the position of plaintiff, intending but to gamble, who was defrauded by another through the use of dishonest paraphernalia, or methods, was not in pari delicto with his swindler. Thus, plaintiff had standing to recover the amount of his losses.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court order granting defendant thieves' motion for judgment on the pleadings and dismissing the complaint. The cause was remanded for further proceedings consistent with the court's judgment. The court held plaintiff gambler was not in pari delicto with the defendants, and plaintiff had standing to recover his losses.

 

2158 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gilmore


Civ. No. 27607


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Three


57 Cal. App. 3d 828; 129 Cal. Rptr. 388; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1496



April 29, 1976 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted July 7, 1976 (Cr. 19477)
On Aug. 29, 1977, this cause retrans. to Ct. of App., 2d Dist., Div. 3, for the refiling of its opn. with an appropriate reference to People v. Thomas (1977) 19 Cal.3d 630.
Opn. filed Sept. 7, 1977, not for publication.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the order of the trial court committing her to the California Rehabilitation Center following a jury trial where 11 of 12 jurors found that she was a narcotic addict. She claimed that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3108, under which she was committed, violated constitutional guarantees of due process of law because it did not require a pre-commitment unanimous jury verdict by proof beyond a reasonable doubt.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of possession of heroin. She requested and received a jury trial to determine whether or not she was a narcotic addict under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3108. She requested and was denied a jury instruction that the jury had to be convinced beyond a reasonable doubt that she was a narcotic addict and that any such finding must be unanimous. The trial court instructed the jury according to the provisions of § 3108 that the finding had to be by a preponderance of the evidence and signed by nine or more jurors. Eleven of twelve jurors decided that appellant was a narcotic addict, and she was committed to the California Rehabilitation Center (CRC). On appeal, the court reversed, holding that § 3108 unconstitutionally deprived appellant of due process of law. The court found that CRC commitment sometimes resulted in longer curtailment of personal freedom than that which occurred following many criminal convictions and that trial judges often regarded CRC commitment as the harsher penalty. The court held that a jury had to unanimously find beyond a reasonable doubt that appellant was a narcotic addict, and declared § 3108 to be unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment committing appellant to the California Rehabilitation Center was reversed and the case was remanded because due process required that a jury unanimously find beyond a reasonable doubt that appellant was a narcotic addict.

 

2159 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Lorco Properties, Inc. v. Department of Ben. Payments


Civ. No. 47202


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


57 Cal. App. 3d 809; 129 Cal. Rptr. 312; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1493



April 29, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 113 308, Harry L. Hupp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employer challenged the judgment by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his petition for writ of mandate and affirmed the determination of the California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board which refused to transfer unemployment reserves under its former account to its new account.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employer became subject to the Unemployment Insurance Code and paid taxes into an account. His account was cancelled by an automatic data processing operation when he stopped showing taxable wages under Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1029. Appellant started to report taxable wages and pay unemployment contributions and was assigned a new account. Appellant initiated administrative proceedings to transfer reserves credited under its former account to its new account under Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1051. His request was denied by respondent Department of Benefit Payments and by the California Unemployment Appeals Board. Appellant challenged the denial and filed a petition for writ of mandate. The trial court discharged the writ and the court affirmed. The court found the closing of the old account was administrative and not discretionary in nature. Therefore, appellant was not entitled to a hearing. The procedural requirements of notice and hearing were not applicable to statutes specifically authorizing a mechanical or administrative procedure, such as the cancellation of a reserve account. Appellant had an adequate remedy at law or by payment and making claim for refund.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant employer's petition for writ of mandate to transfer unemployment reserves under its former account to its new account because the closing of his old account was administrative and not discretionary in nature. Appellant had a remedy at law to question the validity of the action by making payment and a claim for refund.

Eldridge v. City of Palo Alto


Civ. Nos. 33517, 34134


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


57 Cal. App. 3d 613; 129 Cal. Rptr. 575; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1480



April 23, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Hearing Granted January 21, 1976, and Cause Retrans. to Ct. of App., 1st Dist., Div. 1, for Reconsideration in the Light of HFH, Ltd. v. Superior Court (1975) 15 Cal.3d 508. (See 57 C.A.3d 613. The Petition of Respondent City of Palo Alto for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 15, 1976. Wright, C. J., Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, Nos. 282965, P 22974, Richard W. Rhodes and Edward L. Brady, * Judges.


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgments of dismissal are reversed. The superior court will set aside its orders sustaining the City of Palo Alto's demurrers without leave to amend. It will grant plaintiffs leave to amend their complaints if they shall be so advised, and will otherwise take further proceedings not inconsistent with the views we have expressed. Plaintiffs Beyer and Eldridge will recover their costs of appeal from the City of Palo Alto.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowners sought review of the judgments of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which dismissed their actions for damages in inverse condemnation after sustaining general demurrers by defendant city. Plaintiffs contended that the zoning ordinances, which classified their property for permanent open space and conservation lands, were so restrictive that they amounted to a taking.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city enacted zoning ordinances that classified plaintiff landowners' property as open space and conservation lands. In inverse condemnation actions for damages, plaintiffs contended that the ordinances denied them any substantial or reasonable use of their property. The trial court sustained defendant's general demurrers, holding that no cause of action was stated. Plaintiffs appealed the judgments of dismissal. The court observed that a valid zoning ordinance may operate so oppressively as to amount to a taking, thus giving an aggrieved landowner a right to damages in inverse condemnation. The court found that determining whether a zoning restriction was so arbitrary and unreasonable to constitutionally require compensation was a question of fact that could not properly be resolved by demurrer. The court rejected defendant's claim that plaintiffs failed to exhaust administrative remedies, finding that applying for variances with the amount of land involved was useless. Reversing the judgments of dismissal, the court held that plaintiffs stated a cause of action for damages in inverse condemnation and directed the orders sustaining the demurrers be set aside.


OUTCOME: The court reserved the judgments that dismissed plaintiff landowners' action for damages in inverse condemnation against defendant city. Holding that a cause of action was stated, the court directed the orders sustaining defendant's demurrers be set aside because whether the zoning ordinances reclassifying plaintiffs' property for open space and conservation lands were so restrictive to require just compensation was an issue of fact.

 

2161 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Aden v. Younger


Civ. No. 14407


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


57 Cal. App. 3d 662; 129 Cal. Rptr. 535; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1482



April 23, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified April 28, 1976; A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 20, 1976, and the Opinion and the Judgment were Modified on May 20, 1976, and June 18, 1976, to Read as Printed Above.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, let a peremptory writ of mandate issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, mentally ill patients and their doctors, sought a writ for peremptory mandate from a California trial court to prevent respondents, attorney general, health director, and board of medical examiners, from enforcing amendments made to the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5000- 5404. Petitioners alleged the amendments to the law were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, mentally ill patients and their doctors, were possibly in need of voluntary treatments of electroconvulsive therapy and psychosurgery. The Lanterman-Petris-Short Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5000- 5404 changed the conditions under which psychosurgery and shock treatment could be performed. Petitioners asserted the changes were unconstitutional in certain respects and filed a peremptory writ of mandate permanently preventing respondents, attorney general, health director, and board of medical examiners, from enforcing the amendments. Petitioners alleged the amendments denied equal protection because of unreasonable classifications of mental patients and violated substantive and procedural due process because the patient's constitutional rights of privacy, freedom of thought, and access to adequate medical treatment were infringed upon without adequate procedural safeguards or sufficient relation to a compelling state interest. The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate finding that various provisions of § 5000 et seq. were unconstitutional for vagueness and thus invalid.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate to petitioners, mentally ill patients and their doctors, because there was no justification for infringing upon a patient's right to privacy in selecting and consenting to treatment. The statutory amendments relative to a mentally ill patient's rights were unconstitutional for vagueness and did not serve a compelling state interest.

 

2162 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Cooke


Civ. No. 15480


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


57 Cal. App. 3d 595; 129 Cal. Rptr. 354; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1478



April 22, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 13, 1976, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 1, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. A-77360, Frank Domenichini, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant trustee challenged an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which determined the existence and the amount of inheritance tax liability resulting from the death of a decedent.


OVERVIEW: Decedent established a revocable inter vivos trust under the terms of which appellant trustee was granted a power of appointment with respect to the principal and any undistributed income of the trust estate. Appellant executed a disclaimer. Respondent State Controller refused to give effect to the disclaimer for inheritance tax computation purposes and treated appellant as the transferee of all of the trust's assets. The trial court decided that the disclaimer executed by appellant did not satisfy the requirements of Cal. Prob. Code §§ 190(c), and 190.6 and was not a legally valid disclaimer. Appellant argued on appeal that Cal. Prob. Code § 190.9 was intended to protect creditors and bona fide purchasers of a beneficiary who might disclaim his interest but not the state's intervening right to inheritance taxes. The court held that inheritance taxes are vested and determined in accordance with the law in effect on the date of transfer or death and that rights so fixed cannot be surrendered, diminished or impaired by subsequent action of the Legislature since to do so would constitute a gift of public funds in violation of the state Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order determining the existence and amount of inheritance tax liability, on the basis that the applicable tax statute could not be construed to apply retroactively to disclaimers of interests transferred before the effective date of the section even if the Legislature had so intended.

People ex rel. Camil v. Buena Vista Cinema


Civ. Nos. 46918, 46919


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


57 Cal. App. 3d 497; 129 Cal. Rptr. 315; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1469



April 21, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied June 17, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C 107347, C 107771, Max F. Deutz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal as amended in case No. C107347 is affirmed. The judgment of dismissal in case No. C107771 as to the cause of action based on the Red Light Abatement Act (Pen. Code, § 11225 et seq.) is also affirmed. The judgment of dismissal in case No. C107771 as to the cause of action based on the public nuisance statutes (Civ. Code, §§ 3479, 3480) is reversed and the trial court is directed to enter its order overruling the demurrer to that cause of action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that sustained defendant adult theater's demurrers and dismissed plaintiff's nuisance abatement actions based upon the Red Light Abatement Act, Cal. Penal Code § 11225 et seq., public nuisance statutes pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code §§ 3479 and 3480, and a city ordinance, and denied plaintiff's motion for a preliminary injunction.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff city instituted proceedings to abate the public nuisance created by defendant adult theater and sought a preliminary injunction. Plaintiff's actions were based upon a city ordinance, the Red Light Abatement Act, Cal. Penal Code § 11225 et seq., and general public nuisance statutes pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code §§ 3479 and 3480. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrer and dismissed both actions and denied the motion for a preliminary injunction. Plaintiff sought review. The appellate court affirmed the dismissal of the nuisance abatement action based upon the Red Light Abatement Act because its provisions did not apply to obscene films. Likewise, the appellate court affirmed the dismissal of the nuisance abatement action based upon the city ordinance because the ordinance did not create a cause of action for plaintiff nor establish the liability of defendant nor prescribe any particular form of relief. The appellate court reversed the dismissal that was based upon the general public nuisance statutes because the exhibition of obscene material, including films, could be restrained as a public nuisance under §§ 3479 and 3480.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the dismissal in favor of defendant adult theater in plaintiff city's nuisance abatement actions based upon the Red Light Abatement Act because it did not apply to obscene films and the city ordinance did not create a nuisance abatement cause of action. The dismissal based upon public nuisance statutes was reversed because the exhibition of obscene films could be restrained as a public nuisance under the statutes.

People v. Strohl


Crim. No. 27581


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


57 Cal. App. 3d 347; 129 Cal. Rptr. 224; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1459



April 19, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 14, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 106697, John A. Westwick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for giving and offering a bribe to a chief deputy coroner, with the intent of influencing his acts, decisions and opinion involving the disposition of the remains of indigent deceased, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 67, as entered by the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California).


OVERVIEW: Defendant attempted to bribe the county coroner, in order to obtain an exclusive contract with the county for the disposal of unclaimed bodies of deceased indigents for his cremation business. An undercover agent and the coroner taped statements of defendant related to this purpose. Defendant was convicted of giving and offering to give a bribe to an executive officer, with the intent of influencing his acts, decisions and opinion under Cal. Penal Code § 67. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the term "executive officer" was not unconstitutionally vague and that the county coroner qualified as an "executive officer" based on his discretionary powers and his executive co-position as sheriff. Further, defendant had no constitutional right to counsel at the pre-arraignment stage, and the admission of the tape recordings into evidence was proper under federal and state law under the one-party rule, since the agent and coroner consented to the tapings. Finally, the evidence sufficiently supported the jury's finding that the criminal intent to commit the bribery originated in the mind of defendant, not the agent's or coroner's, and thus, there was no unlawful entrapment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for attempting to bribe the county coroner, holding that the statute under which defendant was convicted was not unconstitutionally vague, that the coroner was an "executive officer" under the statute, that the taped statements evidencing defendant's criminal intention were properly admitted, and that defendant was not unlawfully entrapped.

People v. Quinn


Crim. No. 14530


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


57 Cal. App. 3d 251; 129 Cal. Rptr. 139; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1449



April 13, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Marin County, No. 5364, Joseph G. Wilson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction for possessing and exploding a destructive device entered by the Superior Court of Marin County (California). Defendant contended that his homemade bomb was not a "destructive device" under Cal. Penal Code § 12303.3 and that the statutory term "bomb" was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant set off a homemade explosive device in the victim's automobile. Defendant sought review of his conviction by the trial court for possessing and exploding a destructive device in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12303.3. The court affirmed, holding that the statutory terms "explosive" and "destructive device" were not mutually exclusive. The court held that the statutory term "bomb" was not unconstitutionally void for vagueness and that defendant's device was a "destructive device" under § 12303.3. The court held that where parts of the bomb sent to a laboratory were known to the defense but never requested, and not shown to be exculpatory, there was no violation of the Brady requirement to make them available to the defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for possessing and exploding a destructive device. The court held that the term "destructive device" included the explosive made by defendant, and the term "bomb" was not unconstitutionally vague.

Natural Resources Defense Council, Inc. v. California Coastal Zone Conservation Com.


Civ. No. 37225


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


57 Cal. App. 3d 76; 129 Cal. Rptr. 57; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1432



April 6, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 4, 1976, and Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied June 2, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 677375, Thomas J. Dandurand, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The motions of the real parties in interest, or certain of them, to abate, and stay proceedings on, this appeal are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant environmental groups challenged an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which upheld a decision of respondent California Coastal Zone Conservation Commission to allow interim permits to real parties in interest, home builders, under the California Coastal Zone Conservation Act of 1972, Cal. Pub. Res. Code §§ 27000- 27650 (1972).


OVERVIEW: Real parties in interest, home builders, applied for interim permits to put 15 units in an existing subdivision pursuant to the California Coastal Zone Conservation Act of 1972, Cal. Pub. Res. Code §§ 27000- 27650 (1972). There was conflicting evidence about how severely the 15 units would affect coastal erosion, traffic congestion, waste disposal, and local wildlife. Respondent Coastal Zone Conservation Commission granted the interim permits on certain conditions, such as monitoring effects of waste diversion on wildlife. Appellant environmental groups sought judicial review of respondent's decision and filed a petition for a writ of mandate, which the trial court denied. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment on appeal, holding that reasonable inferences from the evidence supported respondent's conclusion that the impact of the 15 units would be minimal and that respondent gave adequate fact findings to support its decision. The findings served the same purpose as a report under the California Environmental Quality Act, Cal. Pub. Res. Code §§ 21061 and 21100. The conditions for the permits were reasonable, enforceable, and supported by substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the request by appellant environmental groups for a writ of mandate and held that respondent Coastal Zone Conservation Commission did not abuse its discretion in granting the interim permits. Respondent considered that environmental consequences from the 15 units would be minimal and gave adequate findings to support its decision, which findings served the purpose of an environmental impact report.

Gould v. People


Civ. No. 46133


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


56 Cal. App. 3d 909; 128 Cal. Rptr. 743; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1416



April 1, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 29, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 99083, Campbell M. Lucas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order (of dismissal) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs taxpayers challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained the demurrer of defendants, government officials and judges, denied plaintiffs' request under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 526a for an injunction, and dismissed plaintiffs' complaint. Plaintiffs sought to enjoin defendants from prosecuting them from certain obscenity cases brought under Cal. Penal Code § 311.2.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayers were charged with the sale or distribution of obscene matters under Cal. Penal Code § 182, by conspiring to violate Cal. Penal Code § 311.2. While the action was pending, plaintiffs sought to enjoin defendants, government officials and judges, pursuant to Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 526a, from prosecuting them. Plaintiffs argued that § 311.2, was unconstitutional and that the action was an illegal expenditure of public funds. The trial court sustained defendants' demurrer without leave to amend and dismissed the complaint. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment and held that § 311.2, did not violate U.S. Const. amend. XIV, because it tended to promote rather than inhibit dissemination of speech as protected by U.S. Const. amend. I. The court held that U.S. Const. amend. I, did not protect obscenity and that § 311.2, was not unconstitutionally vague or violative of freedom of speech. The court held that an injunction was not permitted to prevent the execution of a public statute, by officers of the law, for the public benefit because Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 526a, was not maintainable against judicial officers because it violated principles of judicial immunity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order sustaining the demurrer of defendants government officials and judges, denial of plaintiffs taxpayers' request for injunctive relief, and dismissal of plaintiffs' complaint and held the statute regulating obscene matters was constitutional and not violative of the Fourteenth Amendment because it was not unconstitutionally vague and the proscribed matter was specifically defined as authoritatively construed.

Anderson v. County of Santa Barbara


Civ. No. 47401


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


56 Cal. App. 3d 780; 128 Cal. Rptr. 707; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1402



March 30, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied April 22, 1976, and the petitions of all the parties for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied May 26, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 108439, Ralph M. Drummond, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizens challenged the order of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which granted summary judgment, under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 437c, in favor of defendants, county and corporation, in response to plaintiff's challenge, under Cal. Elec. Code § 20020 et seq., to a referendum in favor of a rezoning ordinance allowing defendant corporation to build oil and gas facilities on coastal land.


OVERVIEW: Defendant county held a referendum regarding a rezoning ordinance passed by the board of supervisors that permitted defendant corporation to construct oil and gas facilities on coastal property. Plaintiff citizens contested the vote approving the ordinance. Defendants filed motions for summary judgment with declarations establishing that votes cast in the challenged precincts were overwhelmingly against the proposition, that if all disqualified ballots were counted against the proposition, it still prevailed, and that statements asserted to constitute improper influence to vote in favor of the proposition were included in environmental impact data gathered by the county and in the official voter information pamphlet. Plaintiffs challenged summary judgment granted in favor of defendants. The court held that because of inherent inconsistency between the procedural requirements of summary judgment under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 437c, and the statutory scheme governing election contests, Cal. Elec. Code § 20020 et seq., summary judgment was not an available remedy. Therefore, the judgment was reversed, and the court made several conclusions to guide the trial court on remand.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgment granted in favor of defendants, county and corporation, because summary judgment conflicted with the statutory scheme of election contests thus making it an improper remedy.

 

2169 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Blackburn


Crim. No. 27576


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


56 Cal. App. 3d 685; 128 Cal. Rptr. 864; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1393



March 29, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 9, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A187155, Newell Barrett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of rape and kidnapping.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of his conviction of rape and kidnapping. The court affirmed the convictions and concluded that the evidence barred by Cal. Evid. Code § 1103(2) did not concern the credibility of a witness so as to affect the right of confrontation of witnesses, and limited the exclusion of evidence of the victim's sexual conduct only when it was offered to prove consent. Policy considerations dictated that the evidence be excluded, and those policy considerations were deemed incorporated within the definition of a fair trial. Cal. Evid. Code § 352 empowered the trial judge to bar impeachment of a witness by reference to collateral testimony from the preliminary hearing. Cal. Evid. Code § 782's requirement of an offer of proof that evidence of sexual conduct was relevant and admissible and could not have tended to incriminate appellant. The evidence offered at the preliminary hearing that the victim was forcibly moved a substantial distance was sufficient to hold appellant for trial on kidnapping.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction of rape and kidnapping because the bar on evidence of the victim's sexual conduct was constitutional and there was sufficient evidence to hold appellant for trial on kidnapping.

People v. Superior Court of Alameda County


Civ. No. 37851


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Two


56 Cal. App. 3d 608; 128 Cal. Rptr. 519; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1386



March 26, 1976 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted June 9, 1976 (See 19 C.3d 338)
Opinion omitted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought by extraordinary writ to prevent respondent superior court, the Superior Court for Alameda County (California), from enforcing its order that a peremptory writ of prohibition issue restraining the municipal court from proceeding with the prosecution of real parties in interest, women charged pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 647(b) because it denied equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV; Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a).


OVERVIEW: Respondent superior court issued a peremptory writ of prohibition directing the municipal court not to proceed with the prosecution of real parties in interest, women charged with violations of Cal. Penal Code § 647(b). Respondent held that real parties were denied equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV; Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a). Respondent based its decision on evidence showing the manner of enforcement by the police department and statistics showing the great imbalance in arrests of women as opposed to men under § 647(b). The state sought an extraordinary writ preventing respondent from enforcing its order. The court held § 647(b), was fair on its face and impartial and by its terms applied to every person who solicits or who engages in any act of prostitution and further that real parties established that the prosecuting authorities, in administering the statute, had followed an enforcement pattern deliberately based upon an unjustifiable standard, and that a denial of equal protection had been demonstrated. The court also held the word solicits in § 647(b) was not unconstitutionally vague and denied the state's writ.


OUTCOME: The court held state could not proceed with prosecution of real parties in interest, women charged with prostitution or solicitation, because of the unconstitutional manner in which the statute charging real parties had been applied by the police department. The court held real parties were denied equal protection of the laws and denied the state's request for a peremptory writ of prohibition.

 

2171 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Swanson v. Marin Mun. Water Dist.


Civ. No. 36316


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


56 Cal. App. 3d 512; 128 Cal. Rptr. 485; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1377



March 25, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 23, 1976, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 19, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 67962, E. Warren McGuire, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The trial court is directed to enter judgment for defendant Marin Municipal Water District.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Marin Municipal Water District challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which granted plaintiff landowner a peremptory writ of mandate compelling defendant to grant him a pipeline extension and to provide water service to his real property.


OVERVIEW: Defendant Marin Municipal Water District adopted a resolution, finding that water consumption was outpacing water capacity in the district, and declared a water shortage emergency. Plaintiff landowner, who had already obtained approval of his plans and a building permit, applied for a pipeline extension to bring water to the home he planned to build in the district. Defendant then amended the water code to provide that no new water service would be granted or installed, and rejected plaintiff's application. Plaintiff filed for a writ of mandate to compel defendant to grant him water service, which the trial court granted. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that because defendant had acted in a legislative, rather than adjudicatory, capacity in enacting the moratorium on new water service, its actions were subject to a limited review. The court held that defendant did not act fraudulently, arbitrarily or capriciously in declaring a water shortage emergency condition to exist and in enacting a water moratorium on new water service connections; hence, the trial court exceeded the limited scope of its review in granting plaintiff the writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order compelling defendant Marin Municipal Water District to grant plaintiff landowner water service, holding that defendant did not act fraudulently, arbitrarily or capriciously in declaring a water shortage emergency and in enacting a moratorium on new water service connections, and consequently, the trial court exceeded its scope of review in granting plaintiff the writ.

 

2172 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. K. Sakai Co.


Civ. No. 37226


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


56 Cal. App. 3d 531; 128 Cal. Rptr. 536; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1379



March 25, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 19, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 669515, John A. Ertola, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant grocery store owner sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which enjoined appellant from selling or possessing with intent to sell whale meat or other food products in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 653o and Cal. Penal Code § 653r.


OVERVIEW: The trial court entered a judgment that enjoined appellant grocery store owner from selling or possessing with intent to sell whale meat or other food products in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 653o and Cal. Penal Code § 653r. Appellant argued, on appeal that § 653o and § 653r were unconstitutional as an unreasonable exercise of the state's police power. The court held that protection of endangered species of wildlife was within the ambit of the state's police power and was in the interests of the public in light of the recent federal and state legislation concerning endangered species. The court held that a rational basis existed for prohibiting the sale of whale meat, and § 653o and § 653r constituted a reasonable exercise of the state's police power. The court held that § 653o and § 653r did not violate appellant's due process rights because appellant was not deprived of a vested property right, and there was no taking for which compensation was required. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment because the law that enjoined appellant grocery store owner from selling or possessing with intent to sell whale meat or other food products was a rational exercise of the state's police power.

Ralston Purina Co. v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 46489


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


56 Cal. App. 3d 547; 128 Cal. Rptr. 556; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1381



March 25, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 2, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 991624, Charles H. Older, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant County of Los Angeles sought review from Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment in favor of plaintiff company in its cause of action for refund of ad valorem personal property taxes levied and collected under protest for goods stored in plaintiff's warehouses.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff company was taxed for goods canned in American Samoa. Plaintiff initiated action against defendant County of Los Angeles for refund of ad valorem personal property taxes levied and collected under protest. The trial court entered judgment in favor of plaintiff and held that American Samoa was not governmentally united with the U.S. under U.S. Const. art. I, § 9, cl. 2, for purposes of determining what was an "import." Pursuant to 19 U.S.C.S. § 1401(h), American Samoa was not included in the United States and its territories. The court held that the trial court was correct. However, since the trial court's decision, a supreme court decision determined that nondiscriminatory ad valorem property taxes were incorrectly deemed "imposts" or "duties." The court held that the nondiscriminatory general ad valorem personal property taxes levied upon plaintiff's good were not an "impost" or "duty" and were not invalid property taxes upon imports. The court held that the decision was intended to apply retrospectively under the circumstances and that there was no reason that it should not have been so applied. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which held that plaintiff company was entitled to refund for ad valorem personal property taxes levied and collected by defendant County of Los Angeles. The court held that a supreme court decision determining that nondiscriminatory ad valorem property taxes were incorrectly deemed "imposts" or "duties" was intended to apply retrospectively under the circumstances and should be so applied.

California Retail Liquor Dealers Institute v. United Farm Workers


Civ. No. 35643


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


57 Cal. App. 3d 606; 129 Cal. Rptr. 407; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1479



March 24, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 14, 1976, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 19, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 70708, Edmund J. Leach, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting a preliminary injunction as to the named plaintiffs is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant labor organization sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which granted a preliminary injunction that placed limits on appellant's picketing of stores owned by respondent liquor dealers.


OVERVIEW: Appellant labor organization picketed stores owned by respondent liquor dealers to induce them to cease selling certain brands of wine. The trial court granted a preliminary injunction, and found that appellant was deliberately interfering with the public's right of free access to and from respondents' stores. On appeal, the court found that the record adequately justified the restrictions imposed by the trial court. The court held that appellant's picketing of respondents' stores by blocking entrances and driveways, harassing and intimidating customers of the boycotted stores revealed a pattern of unlawful activity. The court held that the number and distance restrictions that the trial court imposed on appellant's picketing were not an impediment on peaceful informational picketing, and did not violate appellant's rights to free speech. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that granted a preliminary injunction, and placed restrictions on appellant's picketing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment for a preliminary injunction because the limits placed on appellant labor organization's picketing of stores owned by respondent liquor dealers did not violate appellant's rights to free speech.

 

2175 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Solberg v. Superior Court of San Francisco


Civ. No. 37987


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Four


56 Cal. App. 3d 422; 128 Cal. Rptr. 502; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1366



March 23, 1976 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Hearing ordered and cause trans. to this court on court's own motion April 15, 1976 (See 19 C.3d 182)

Opinion omitted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, alleged prostitutes, sought a writ of mandate to compel a judge of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), to withdraw from participation in a cause pursuant to a challenge under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.6. The judge had refused to disqualify himself and was accused of being in excess of jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, alleged prostitutes, were dissatisfied with the judge who had been assigned to their case and sought to disqualify him from hearing it. The court held that the assertion of the judge that petitioners lacked standing to exercise a challenge against him was incorrect. The court disagreed that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.6 only applied to contested issues of law, since the statute also clearly applied to issues of fact. The court also opined that the statute had been widely abused to keep judges out of certain courts, but that the proper way to challenge the judge was on appeal., where the matters could be reviewed and corrected. The court held that the wholesale challenge of a particular judge, on all cases of a particular type, effectively prevented that judge from hearing any cases of that type and was an unwarranted interference with the powers of the court. The court concluded that the judge did not err when he rejected the challenge, even though his reasons were not sound, since his action could be upheld if it was correct on another basis. The writ was denied.


OUTCOME: The judge discharged an alternative writ and denied the petition. The court concluded that petitioners, alleged prostitutes, were simply looking for a more favorable judge, but that they had no basis for accusing the current judge of bias.

 

2176 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Frank v. Board of Administration


Civ. No. 14309


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


56 Cal. App. 3d 236; 128 Cal. Rptr. 378; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1343; 41 Cal. Comp. Cases 937



March 15, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 227100, Joseph A. DeCristoforo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant board of administration appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), granting plaintiff employee certain disability retirement benefits. Defendant claimed that plaintiff did not acquire vested rights to a disability pension until he was actually disabled and thus he was not entitled to the larger disability pension to which he would have been entitled had the law not changed 23 days before his injury.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff employee worked for the California Department of Corrections as a plumber-foreman for over five years. He was injured on the job and incapacitated by the injury. His injury occurred 23 days after the effective date of legislative changes that defendant administrative board argued resulted in a diminution of the disability benefits to which he was entitled from $ 475 to $ 90 per month. Plaintiff petitioned the trial court for writ of mandate seeking review of the defendant's decision. The trial court directed defendant to pay plaintiff the greater benefits. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's decision. The court held that plaintiff acquired a vested contractual right to a reasonable disability retirement pension when he entered upon performance of his employment contract. The court held further that the validity of attempted changes in plaintiff's pension rights was dependent upon any resulting disadvantage being offset by comparable new advantages. Because that was not the case where one formula resulted in the possibility of self-support and the other did not, plaintiff was entitled to the benefits requested.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court against defendant board of administration granting plaintiff employee disability benefits to which he would have been entitled prior to a change in the law that became effective 23 days before his injury. The court held that plaintiff had a vested right in the disability benefits and that any modification of the benefits to his disadvantage had to be offset by a comparable new advantage.

 

2177 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Shelton v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 37400


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


56 Cal. App. 3d 66; 128 Cal. Rptr. 454; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1326



March 8, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 6, 1976.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged and the petition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, husband and wife, sought review from respondent, Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which denied petitioners' notice of motion to amend complaint to include damages for loss of consortium in addition to general and special damages resulting from injuries suffered as a result of an automobile accident. Petitioners filed a peremptory writ of mandate to order respondent to permit them to amend their complaint.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, husband and wife, were injured in an automobile accident and filed a complaint for general and special damages. Petitioners filed a notice of motion to amend to include damages for loss of consortium. Respondent, Superior Court of Santa Clara County, denied petitioners' notice, and petitioners filed a peremptory writ of mandate to order respondent to permit them to amend their complaint. The court issued an alternative writ of mandate to review. The court held that, petitioners' original claims were silent with respect to any injury or damages suffered by reason of injury to the other. The court held that petitioners could not amend their complaint because only those persons whose cause of action for loss of consortium arose within one year prior to decision allowing consortium claims could amend their complaint. The court held that petitioners attempted to state an entirely different cause of action, which defeated amendment of complaint. Pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 911.4(b), more than one year had expired since accrual of the cause of action, and time within which to file the late claim had expired. The court discharged the alternative writ and denied the petition.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the alternative writ and denied petitioners', husband and wife, cause of action. The court held that petitioners could not amend their complaint for injuries in automobile accident to include loss of consortium because they had attempted to state an entirely different cause of action, which defeated amendment of the complaint, and because cause of action had not risen within one year of decision allowing consortium claims.

People v. Gardner


Crim. No. 8101


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


56 Cal. App. 3d 91; 128 Cal. Rptr. 101; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1328



March 8, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 6, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 25824, Norman C. Sullivan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his convictions for assault with a deadly weapon by a life prisoner and possession of a sharp instrument while a prisoner in a state prison by the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), on the ground that Cal. Penal Code § 4500 was unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. I and U.S. Const. amends. VII and XIV.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of assault with a deadly weapon by a life prisoner and possession of a sharp instrument while a prisoner in a state prison. On appeal, the court affirmed his convictions, holding that Cal. Penal Code § 4500 was constitutional as it was not cruel and unusual punishment prohibited by Cal. Const. art. I, U.S. Const. amend. VII, and U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court held that the facts of appellant's crime revealed a violent, malicious, and unprovoked assault with a knife upon a correctional officer, resulting in a serious injury; that § 4500 contained a rational gradation in punishment based on the injury to the victim; and that there were penological purposes for the punishment. The court held defendant was not being punished for his status as a life prisoner, but rather his acts in committing the crimes. The court held that compared to similar statutes in other states, the punishment was lenient. The court held that defendant waived his issue as to the admission of testimony as to an unproven prior assault by failing to object to it at trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for assault with a deadly weapon by a life prisoner and possession of a sharp instrument while a prisoner in a state prison. The statute under which defendant was convicted did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment as there was a rational gradation in punishment, there were penological purposes for the punishment, and the punishment was comparatively lenient.

Schneider v. Standard Oil Co.


Civ. No. 37215


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


55 Cal. App. 3d 1018; 128 Cal. Rptr. 141; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1311



March 3, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 637280, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff widow sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted summary judgment to defendant company. Plaintiff alleged that she was the owner of shares defendant's stock which her deceased husband had acquired under defendant's "Stock Plan", and that this stock had been sold by defendant and the proceeds of the sale had been distributed to her husband's creditors.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff widow alleged that she was the owner of certain shares of defendant company's stock, which her deceased husband had acquired under a "Stock Plan." Defendant companyhad sold this stock and the proceeds of the sale had been distributed to decedent husband and through him to certain of his creditors prior to his death. Plaintiff was unaware of the settlement with the creditors. On learning of the facts after her husband's death, she demanded of defendant the proceeds of the stock sale and thereafter filed this action. The court held that decedent's interest in the stock plan was community property under Cal. Civ. Code § 5110. Further, at the time defendant sold the stock and decedent distributed it to his creditors, Cal. Civ. Code § 5105 vested the power of management and control of the community property in the husband. The court held that decedent's debts were properly settled out of the proceeds of the stock plan. The court noted that defendant owed no fiduciary duty to plaintiff, nor that it was in any way a trustee of either an express or implied trust of which she was a beneficiary. Thus, the trial court did not err in awarding summary judgment to defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment because decedent husband's interest in the stock plan was community property over which he had the power of management and control under the statute. Therefore, decedent's debts were properly settled out of the proceeds of the stock plan. Defendant company owed no fiduciary duty to plaintiff widow, nor was it in any way a trustee of either an express or implied trust of which she was a beneficiary.

Los Angeles v. Hall


Civ. No. 45591


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Five


55 Cal. App. 3d 907; 127 Cal. Rptr. 768; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1302



February 27, 1976 Hg. granted April 28, 1976


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] See 18 C.3d 860 
Opinion omitted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner sought review of the decision of the trial court (California), which denied appellant's motion for a new trial in respondent city's condemnation action.


OVERVIEW: The airport commissioners requested that respondent city enact a condemnation ordinance, and in 1971, appellant landowner's property was condemned by respondent for an airport expansion. However, by 1970, the department of airports had already acquired a number of houses in the area, which made commercial rezoning improbable. In 1973, a condemnation trial determined the fair market value of appellant's parcel. Appellant filed a motion for a new trial, which was denied. The court affirmed the denial of the motion for a new trial. The court determined that respondent was under no duty to disclose the proposed use and that any project enhancement evidence was inadmissible. The court held that the lower court correctly determined the highest and best use of appellant's property because redevelopment and rezoning were improbable, and, therefore, the denial of appellant's motion for a new trial was proper. The court found the lower court did not err when it instructed the jury, pursuant to a modified version of Cal. Jury Instructions-Civil No. 11.98, that appellant had the burden of proving the property's value.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of appellant landowner's motion for a new trial in respondent city's condemnation action and held that the highest and best use of appellant's property was properly determined because evidence of a proposed use of the condemned property was not admissible. The court determined that the lower court did not err when it instructed the jury that appellant had the burden of proving the property's value.

Cuevas v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 2893


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


58 Cal. App. 3d 406; 130 Cal. Rptr. 238; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1526



February 27, 1976 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue restraining the Stanislaus County Superior Court from taking any further proceedings other than dismissals against petitioners.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought a writ of prohibition to restrain the lower court from taking any further proceedings against them on the ground that their motion to set aside the information under Cal. Penal Code §995 was erroneously denied because defendants were prevented from challenging a search warrant pursuant to § 1538.5 at the preliminary hearing.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged by information with criminal violations and they attempted to file a motion to suppress the evidence obtained through the search warrant. The lower court refused to entertain the motion to suppress and defendants were forced to answer on all three counts charged. Defendants thereafter sought a writ of prohibition to restrain the lower court from taking further action against them on the ground that their motion, filed under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5, was erroneously denied. The court issued the writ of prohibition and restrained the lower court from further proceedings other than dismissals against defendants. The court held that §1538.5(f) provided for a motion to suppress to be heard at a preliminary hearing in the case of a felony offense initiated by a complaint. The court further held that because defendants sought to challenge the evidence obtained, as they were authorized to do pursuant to statute, they were illegally committed and the motion to dismiss was denied in error.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of prohibition to restrain the lower court from taking any further proceedings, other than dismissals against defendants, because defendants were not allowed their opportunity to present their motion to suppress at the preliminary hearing as provided by statute.

Bell v. Board of Supervisors


Civ. No. 13998


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


55 Cal. App. 3d 629; 127 Cal. Rptr. 757; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1274



February 24, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 111587, Bill L. Dozier, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to the trial court to dismiss the action. Plaintiff is to recover his costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff judge appealed a judgment granted to defendant, the Board of Supervisors of San Joaquin County, by the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), in his action that challenged the constitutionality of the legislation that created a new municipal court. The court had initially held that the original enabling legislation was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff judge was the incumbent judge of a justice court. The legislature authorized defendant, the Board of Supervisors of San Joaquin County, to consolidate two contiguous judicial districts into a single municipal court district consisting of two divisions. Plaintiff, a nonlawyer, could not serve as a municipal court judge. He challenged the constitutionality of the legislation creating the municipal court. The trial court granted defendant's motion for judgment on the pleadings. That decision was initially reversed on appeal to the court. Thereafter, the legislature enacted a new enabling statute, which created a unified district with no divisions. The court subsequently reversed its earlier decision. The court held that the new statute rendered the appeal moot because it now questioned the constitutionality of repealed legislation. Moreover, the court noted that the abolishment of plaintiff's office by the legislature failed to offend the constitutional rights of plaintiff or the public. The court further held plaintiff could not seek the difference between a clerk's salary, his new position, and a judge's salary because that issue was not before the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed its earlier decision and held that a new enabling statute, enacted after the court's initial decision, rendered plaintiff judge's appeal of his action, which challenged the constitutionality of legislation that abolished a justice court and created a new municipal court, moot. The trial court was directed to dismiss plaintiff's complaint.

Chilton v. Contra Costa Community College Dist.


Civ. No. 34959


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


55 Cal. App. 3d 544; 127 Cal. Rptr. 659; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1264



February 23, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 24, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 140369, Norman A. Gregg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant applicant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which denied a writ of mandate sought to compel respondent employers, a community college, to employ appellant without requiring her to take and subscribe to an oath swearing to uphold the United States and California Constitutions. Appellant contended that the oath would infringe her rights under U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: Appellant applicant sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent employers to employ her as a cafeteria worker at a state college without being required to take an oath to uphold the United States and California Constitutions. Appellant contended that by requiring her to sign the oath as a condition of employment, respondents were requiring her to surrender her rights under U.S. Const. amend. I by bringing into effect Cal. Gov't Code § 3109, which made it a crime for anyone who had taken the oath to join an organization that advocated overthrow of the government. The trial court denied the requested relief on the ground that appellant's cause was not ripe for review, as she did not allege that she ever intended to join a subversive organization and, therefore, § 3109 was not implicated. Appellant challenged the decision. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment, finding that, although the trial court's reasoning for denial of the writ was flawed, the judgment would be upheld because part of the oath in question had already been declared unconstitutional, and thus appellant could not be prosecuted under § 3109, even if she were to later advocate overthrow of the government.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, finding that part of the oath appellant applicant was required to take had been previously declared unconstitutional, thus precluding prosecution under the relevant statute. Accordingly, the court determined that appellant could be compelled to take the oath as a condition of employment because she did not risk later prosecution under the statute for exercise of her First Amendment rights.

Natural Resources Defense Council, Inc. v. California Coastal Zone Conservation Com.

OVERVIEW: Respondent state coastal zone conservation commission granted construction permits to respondent housing association pursuant to its authority under the Coastal Zone Conservation Act of 1972, Cal. Eberle v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 46574


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


55 Cal. App. 3d 423; 127 Cal. Rptr. 594; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1253



February 19, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 15, 1976. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 106575, Arthur K. Marshall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court granting the writ of prohibition is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted respondent publishers' writ of prohibition in respondents' charge for publication of criminal libel in violation of former Cal. Penal Code § 249, holding the state's criminal libel statutory scheme unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Respondent publishers were charged by a complaint with the publication of criminal libel in violation of former Cal. Penal Code § 249 and their demurrer to the complaint was overruled. Respondent publishers then sought a writ of prohibition from respondent court on the grounds that California's criminal libel statutes, former Cal. Penal Code §§ 248- 257, were unconstitutional. Upon respondent court's grant of the writ the people sought review. On review, the court affirmed. The court first noted that although respondent publishers were only charged under § 249, the entire scheme was required to be examined to ensure that any voided provisions of the scheme did not taint the remainder. The court held that § 250 was unconstitutional because it presumed malice, which was one of the elements of the crime. The court held § 251 unconstitutional because it impermissibly limited the defense of truth. The court held that §§ 248, 249 were unconstitutional because they relied upon impermissible definitions and further, with the striking of §§ 250, 251, no guidelines remained to assist in interpreting the statute as to truth or falsity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed respondent court's writ of prohibition as sought by respondent publishers, because the writ properly found that California's criminal libel statutory scheme was constitutionally infirm as it presumed malice, an element of the crime, impermissibly limited the defense of truth, and the remaining statutes could not stand alone given the unconstitutionality of the other statutes in the scheme.

 

2186 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


O. G. Sansone Co. v. Department of Transportation


Civ. No. 45232


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


55 Cal. App. 3d 434; 127 Cal. Rptr. 799; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1256



February 19, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 15, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C41025, Robert A. Wenke, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff contractors challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which penalized plaintiffs for violation of the prevailing wage laws, and which plaintiffs challenged as being unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff contractors were hired under a government contract, and entered into a contractual agreement that they would comply with Cal. Lab. Code § 1775; and, that they would forfeit to the state $ 25 per day for each workman paid less than the prevailing wage by either plaintiffs or by their subcontractors. Plaintiffs' subcontractors failed to pay prevailing wages and plaintiffs became contractually liable to pay the penalty to the state as well as to pay the wronged workmen the difference in wages. Plaintiffs challenged the constitutionality of the statute's disparate treatment to government contracts and the penalties. The court affirmed because there was a rational basis for the legislature to treat public contracts differently from private contracts, and Cal. Lab. Code § 1775 provided fair notice that violations of the prevailing wage law would result in the imposition of certain penalties. The court held that there were no constitutional violations of either due process or the equal protection of the law, and, held that no unconstitutional burdens had been imposed on plaintiffs.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the penalties against plaintiff contractors because prevailing wage laws for government contracts did not violate either due process or the equal protection of the law. No unconstitutional burdens had been imposed on plaintiffs.

 

2187 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Chi Ko Wong


Crim. No. 25832


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Four


55 Cal. App. 3d 347; 127 Cal. Rptr. 552; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1246



February 18, 1976 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] HEARING GRANTED Apr. 15, 1976 ( SEE 18 C.3d 698)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a juvenile, appealed a judgment of a California superior court, which found him guilty of robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211, murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187, and using a firearm in the offenses in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a juvenile, challenged a juvenile court's decision to try him as an adult, and his convictions. Defendant also argued that during his criminal trial improper evidence was introduced, namely, a pistol, a nylon stocking mask, and photographs. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of conviction and held that the order of the juvenile court under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707 that declared defendant unfit, was not one of those listed in Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 800, which was the sole statutory authority for appeal from a juvenile court order. The court found that a juvenile dissatisfied with the ruling of the juvenile court was not free to await the decision on the criminal charges before seeking a review of the juvenile court proceeding. At trial, defendant did not voice an objection to the items of evidence when offered for identification. When they were offered, defendant's attorney simply objected without stating a basis for the objection. The court found ample evidence of defendant's guilt. The evidence established defendant's identity, the nature of the murder and the robbery charged, and the weapons were discovered during defendant's arrest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendant, a juvenile, of robbery, murder, and that he used a firearm in committing the offenses. The court held that defendant could not appeal the results of the juvenile court's findings declaring him unfit as a juvenile, and that ample evidence supported the conviction.

Ulmer v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 36380


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


55 Cal. App. 3d 263; 127 Cal. Rptr. 445; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1238



February 17, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 16, 1976, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 452847, George W. Phillips, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment granting the writ is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which issued a writ prohibiting the municipal court from entertaining criminal charges against defendant for accosting another in a public place for the purpose of begging or soliciting for alms in violation of Cal. Penal Code §647 (c) and soliciting contributions for a charitable purpose without a permit in violation of Oakland, Cal., Mun. Code § 3-2.09.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for violation of Cal. Penal Code § 647(c) (accosting another in a public place for the purpose of begging or soliciting for alms) and violation of Oakland, Cal. Mun. Code § 3-2.09 (soliciting contributions for a charitable purpose without a permit) while engaged in soliciting funds. The purpose of the solicitation was a factual issue. The superior court issued a writ prohibiting the municipal court from entertaining the charges against defendant, holding that depending on the definition of "accost," § 647(c) was either invalid on U.S. Const. amend I grounds or it was unconstitutionally vague and indefinite as to what conduct it prohibited. The state appealed with regard to the constitutionality of § 647(c) but not with regard to the constitutionality of the municipal ordinance. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that § 647(c) did not impinge on freedoms guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend. I or Cal. Const. art. I, § 9, regardless of which definition of "accost" was used. The court also held that the meaning of the statute was sufficiently clear to give warning of the conduct that was proscribed and was thus not unconstitutional on vagueness grounds.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order granting a writ of prohibition, holding that the statute stating that a person who accosted others in a public place or in a place open to the public for the purpose of begging or soliciting alms was guilty of disorderly conduct did not violate the First Amendment and was not unconstitutionally vague.
