In re Short


Crim. No. 27398


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


55 Cal. App. 3d 268; 127 Cal. Rptr. 498; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1239



February 17, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. 9570, Richard C. Kirkpatrick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As so modified, the order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant director of corrections sought review of an order of the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California), which granted respondent prisoner's petition for a writ of habeas corpus to attack the validity of a regulation adopted by appellant relating to mail privileges of prisoners.


OVERVIEW: Upon the petition of respondent prisoner, the trial court issued a writ of habeas corpus which declared a prisoner mail regulation to be constitutionally invalid and dictated the exact language of an amendment to be enacted by appellant director of corrections in order to render the regulation constitutional. The regulation in question provided that correspondence in a court envelope to an inmate was to be opened only by a designated employee, who would read the correspondence only to the extent necessary to determine that the contents were, in fact, from the court. Appellant challenged the issuance of the writ of habeas corpus. On appeal, the court affirmed the merits of the writ, holding that both appellant and respondent benefited from a requirement that, so far as possible, assured prisoners their right to the privacy of their mail. The court modified the portion of the trial court's order which dictated exact language of an amendment to provide simply that appellant's policy shall be amended so as to provide that correspondence from a court or judicial officer shall be opened by a designated employee only in the presence of the inmate to whom it is addressed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the writ of habeas corpus insomuch as it declared that the language of the regulation unconstitutionally infringed upon respondent prisoner's First Amendment rights, but modified the order to allow appellant director of corrections to draft an amendment to provide that correspondence from a court or judicial officer shall be opened by a designated employee only in the presence of the inmate to whom it is addressed.

People v. Haskin


Crim. No. 26987


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


55 Cal. App. 3d 231; 127 Cal. Rptr. 426; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1235



February 13, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 3, 1976, and Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 28, 1976. Wright, C. J., Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 301220, Sherman W. Smith, Harold J. Ackerman and Betty Jo Sheldon, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted them of conspiracy to distribute obscene matter in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 182(1) and 311.2.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were arrested and charged with conspiracy under Cal. Penal Code § 182(1) to distribute obscene matter in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 311.2 after police officers observing defendants' place of business saw pornographic labels being affixed to film canisters. At trial, defendants filed motions to suppress evidence under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5. The trial court denied defendants' motions and defendants appealed. The court affirmed, finding that the search warrant pursuant to which the obscene materials were seized was valid. The court rejected the argument that the magistrate should have personally viewed the films prior to issuing the warrant, finding that probable cause existed that the films were pornographic without viewing them. The court also found that the police officers' initial entry onto defendants' premises was lawful, although entry was arranged to investigate a possible forged check charge, since there was no intent to search the premises at that time. Defendants' assertion that the police officers seized property before they obtained a search warrant, because two officers were left in control of the location, was also rejected by the court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' conviction for conspiracy to distribute obscene matter, finding that the search warrant which was used to seize pornographic movies was based on probable cause.

Aas v. Avemco Ins. Co.


Civ. No. 34649


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


55 Cal. App. 3d 312; 127 Cal. Rptr. 192; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1243



February 4, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied March 31, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 431039, Robert L. Bostick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which dismissed their action against defendant insurer by granting defendant's motion for judgment on the pleadings, and denying plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment. Plaintiffs' action sought payment of a judgment previously obtained against defendant's insured resulting from an airplane accident.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs' decedent was killed in an airplane crash and plaintiffs obtained a judgment against the estate of the pilot. In this action, plaintiffs sought to recover under a general liability policy covering the airplane. The policy contained an explicit exclusion of liability for injuries to occupants of the airplane, which was permitted by Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 21406 (repealed 1973). Plaintiffs sought to recover under the policy because that statute was subsequently repealed and was declared unconstitutional. The trial court granted defendant's motion for judgment on the pleadings and denied plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment. Plaintiffs appealed and the court affirmed, based on the unambiguous language of the policy exclusion. The court distinguished these facts from a case in which a policy insured against any liability imposed by law, which was then broadened by a change in the law. In this case, the language of the policy imposed the limitation on liability and the contract provision was not changed by the change in the law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order granting defendant's motion for judgment on the pleadings and denying plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment because the policy limitation as to liability for airplane occupants was unambiguous. The policy was not affected by a judicial decision declaring unconstitutional a statute which permitted the policy limitation or the subsequent repeal of the statute.

 

2192 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Isitt


Crim. No. 8001


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


55 Cal. App. 3d 23; 127 Cal. Rptr. 279; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1212



February 2, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied March 31, 1976. Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Butte County, No. 58163, Lucian B. Vandegrift, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Butte County (California), which sentenced him to life imprisonment without parole after a conviction of kidnaping for robbery with bodily harm. Defendant claimed that the sentence constituted cruel and unusual punishment under Cal. Const. art. I, § 6, and a denial of equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of kidnaping for robbery with bodily harm and lesser offenses of robbery in the first degree, and assault to rob. Because all of the offenses involved the same victim and were part of the same transaction, the trial court ordered the sentences for the lesser offenses suspended. The court rejected defendant's claims of cruel and unusual punishment under Cal. Const. art. I, § 6, and denial of equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, finding no abuse of discretion at the lower court level for the life imprisonment sentence. After considering the penological and social objectives of the challenged punishment in the light of the evil to which that punishment was directed, the court concluded that the penalty of life imprisonment without parole was not clearly, positively, or unmistakably disproportionate to the offense of kidnaping for robbery with bodily harm. The court affirmed the judgment, but modified the sentence to include a stay of sentences for the lesser offenses, instead of a suspension, until defendant had served the sentence for kidnaping for robbery with bodily harm.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and sentence because the penalty of life imprisonment without parole was not clearly, positively, or unmistakably disproportionate to the offense of kidnaping for robbery with bodily harm.

People v. Navarette

Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


54 Cal. App. 3d 1064; 127 Cal. Rptr. 55; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1203



January 30, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 27, 1976, and the Petition of the Defendant and Respondent and the Petitioner for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied March 24, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 57546, Robert D. Carter, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The order quashing the indictment against defendant Navarette is vacated and set aside with directions to reinstate criminal proceedings therein. Defendant Peraza's petition for a writ of prohibition is denied. The alternative writ of prohibition heretofore granted is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) which granted the motion of defendant female to quash her indictment on grounds that women were discriminated against in selecting grand jurors. In a separate action, defendant male petitioned for a writ of prohibition ordering that no further action be taken on the criminal charges against him other than to quash the indictment against him.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, male and female, were indicted for selling heroin and offering to sell marijuana in violation of Cal. Health and Safety Code §§ 11352 and 11360. Alleging the systematic exclusion of women from the grand jury, both defendants moved to quash the indictment. The trial court granted the motion for defendant female because she was a member of the class underrepresented on the grand jury; however because defendant male was not a member of the class the court held that he had failed to show any bias to him from such underrepresentation. Plaintiff state appealed the trial court's ruling as to defendant female, and defendant male filed a petition for writ of prohibition. The court vacated and set aside the trial court judgment quashing the indictment against defendant female, with directions to reinstate criminal proceedings; defendant male's petition for writ was denied. Although the court held that women were sufficiently numerous and distinct from men and when systematically eliminated from jury panels, the fair-cross-section requirement of U.S. Const. amend. VI was violated, application of this principal could not be retroactively applied.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order of the trial court which quashed the indictment against defendant female with directions to reinstate the criminal proceedings against her. The court denied defendant male's petition for a writ of prohibition. The court held that court decisions holding that the fair-cross-section requirement was violated by the systematic exclusion of women, did not have retroactive application.

Faix, Ltd. v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 46434


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


54 Cal. App. 3d 992; 127 Cal. Rptr. 182; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1195



January 29, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 4062, C 38278, and C 51630, Edward J. O'Connor, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted defendant county's motion for judgment on the pleadings and denied plaintiff's action for a refund and declaratory relief regarding taxes assessed on certain prescriptive water rights.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer sought a tax refund from defendant county and declaratory relief from property taxes paid based on the valuation assessed for certain prescriptive water rights previously granted to plaintiff. The court affirmed the lower court's order granting defendant judgment on the pleadings, holding that because plaintiff claimed interest in the water rights, including prescriptive rights, and because the separate assessment and taxation of appropriative or prescriptive water rights was not double taxation, the lower court did not err in granting defendant's motion for judgment on the pleadings. In upholding the lower court's ruling, the court also noted that plaintiff's partial payment of taxes under protest, with no payment of the tax balance, did not entitle plaintiff to an action for redress. Similarly, addressing plaintiff's last claim, the court held plaintiff was not allowed to seek declaratory relief when it had not yet paid the taxes or met the statutory requirements for accrual of a cause of action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's ruling, which granted defendant's motion for judgment on the pleadings, because plaintiff taxpayer had only made a partial payment under protest for taxes assessed upon prescriptive water rights and was therefore not entitled to redress, and because plaintiff had not met the statutory requirements for accrual of a cause of action.

People v. Hashimoto


Crim. No. 27227


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


54 Cal. App. 3d 862; 126 Cal. Rptr. 848; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1180



January 27, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 11, 1976, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied March 24, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 309917, Billy G. Mills, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him in a nonjury trial for pandering in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 266i, which statute condemned among other things, the inducing, persuading or encouraging a female to become a prostitute.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of pandering in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 266i. The statute condemns, among other things, the inducement, persuasion, or encouragement of a female to become a prostitute. An officer represented herself to be a model. Defendant asked for a telephone number that he would provide to clients of the Japan Travel Bureau that desired to obtain her services as a prostitute. According to defendant's proposal, the officer would have sex with Japanese tourists. Defendant's interest in the plan was not the receipt of any remuneration but rather the satisfaction of the travel agency's clientele by augmenting the usual services that were offered. The court affirmed because the absence of financial gain was not fatal to the conviction because the statute covered all ramifications of the evil of the crime. The court found that the evidence supported the conviction and that the statute was not vague or overbroad because the statute was not susceptible to being interpreted to cover any activity beyond that of pandering and defendant's conduct met the commonly accepted definitions of the words of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court convicting defendant of pandering because it found that the evidence at trial was sufficient for the conviction even though defendant was not to receive any money from prostitution. The court also found that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague or over broad because it was not reasonably susceptible of being interpreted to cover any activity beyond that involved in the evil of pandering.

Bowland v. Municipal Court for Santa Cruz Judicial Dist.


Civ. No. 35739


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION One

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which denied appellant's petition for a peremptory writ of mandate to restrain respondent municipal court from proceeding in a criminal action against appellant for violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 2141.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged with violating Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 2141 for holding herself out to treat and treating a woman in childbirth without a valid license. Appellant sought a peremptory writ of mandate to restrain respondent municipal court from proceeding with the criminal charges on the grounds that § 2141 was unconstitutional because of overbreadth and vagueness. The trial court denied appellant's petition and overruled her demurrer. Appellant sought review and the court reversed and held that Cal. OUTCOME: The court reversed and directed the trial court to issue the peremptory writ of mandate because although the statute under which appellant was arrested was neither vague nor overbroad, the complaint against appellant did not properly seek to apply the statute to appellant's actions, and pregnancy and childbirth was not an ailment or disease, and unlicensed childbirth assistance was not prohibited at the time of appellant's arrest.

Philippart v. Hotchkiss Tract Reclamation Dist. 799


Civ. No. 35684


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


54 Cal. App. 3d 797; 127 Cal. Rptr. 42; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1174



January 26, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 141073, Jackson C. Davis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment finding Water Code section 50704 violative of the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution is reversed. The contestant's purported appeal is dismissed. Costs to defendants-appellants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) finding that Cal. Water Code § 50704 was unconstitutional, because it apportioned votes in district elections on the value of land in the district as determined from the equalized tax roll of the county.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, who was an unsuccessful candidate for election to the office of the trustee of the water reclamation district, argued that defendant received more votes than he did because the election was conducted in compliance with Cal. Water Code § 50704. He filed an election contest. The trial court held that the provisions of § 50704 that apportioned votes in district elections on the value of land in the district as determined from the equalized tax roll of the county was constitutionally impermissible. The court held that § 50704 did not violate the equal protection clause of the 14th amendment. Because the evaluation and the determination of the value of benefits derived from reclamation was made by valuation commissioners and the board of supervisors, not the trustees, the legislative decision was reasonable, rational, and necessary to further a compelling state interest.


OUTCOME: The judgment finding that the water code violated the equal protection clause of the 14th amendment was reversed because the legislation was reasonable, rational, and necessary to further a compelling state interest. Plaintiff's successful election contest was therefore overturned.

 

2199 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Mandel v. Hodges


Civ. No. 33947


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


54 Cal. App. 3d 596; 127 Cal. Rptr. 244; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1158; 12 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 627; 90 A.L.R.3d 728; 11 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P10,891



January 21, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied March 18, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 427816, Robert L. Bostick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The trial court is authorized to act upon an application by respondent, for an award of attorneys' fees on appeal, when the remittitur has been filed herein. The writ of supersedeas heretofore issued by Division Two of this court under the designation 1 Civil 33042, and which stayed "enforcement" of the judgment hereby affirmed, is recalled forthwith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Upon petition by appellant state officials, the court granted a rehearing of its previous order affirming a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which enjoined the closure of state offices on Good Friday and the payment for time taken off from work during the period and which awarded attorneys' fees and costs to respondent employee.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found in favor of respondent employee and enjoined the governor from ordering state offices to be closed and from paying workers for the period of noon to three p.m. on Good Friday, finding the practice violative of the Establishment Clause, U.S. Const. amend. I, and Cal. Const. art. 1, § 4. On appeal, the court initially affirmed but granted appellant state officials a rehearing. The court again affirmed, holding that the governor's closure of state offices for the identical three-hour period designated as a "holiday" in Cal. Gov't Code § 6700(l) could not be ascribed to coincidence or executive caprice; it unmistakably indicated that his purpose was to give practical effect to that statute and as to state employees. The court held that because it wholly lacked any "secular purpose," the governor's order failed the first aspect of the tripartite test of the Establishment Clause. This failure alone was conclusive of its unconstitutionality, but the governor's order failed the other two aspects of the test as well. The court held that because the conclusion was absolutely necessary to a disposition of the appeal, § 6700(l) was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent employee, holding that the Good Friday order did not reflect a clearly secular purpose because it was a religious day rooted in Christian theology, its primary effect was one which advances religion, and excessive government entanglement with religion was found. The court also deemed that it was necessary to the disposition of the appeal to hold the statute unconstitutional.

People v. Sperl


Crim. No. 26259


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


54 Cal. App. 3d 640; 126 Cal. Rptr. 907; 1976 Cal. App. LEXIS 1160



January 21, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 19, 1976, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied March 24, 1976.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-304507, Richard A. Gadbois, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant marshal challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him for three counts of violating Cal. Penal Code § 424 by keeping false accounts and misappropriating public moneys, Cal. Gov't Code § 6200, which outlawed destruction and falsification of public records, Cal. Gov't Code § 31114, which outlawed tampering with a county civil service examination.


OVERVIEW: The reviewing court affirmed the convictions of defendant marshal. The court held that the subpoenaed records were official records of the county and none were private property, and there was no requirement that defendant, as the head of a county department, be personally served. The court further held that there was overwhelming evidence to support the determination that defendant's systematic pattern of directing false entries, which related to the disbursement of public moneys, to be made fell within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 424(3). The court held that defendant did not meet the burden of showing an intentional discriminatory enforcement. The court affirmed the trial court's finding that the transportation of the public official's family and staff was not related to county business, and it was a misappropriation of public funds to use publicly owned vehicles for unauthorized use. The court also held that there was substantial evidence that defendant violated Cal. Gov't Code § 31114(3) by furnishing special and secret information to the oral examiners of county civil service system.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed; the record supported the trial court's conclusion that defendant marshal had implemented a system for altering records, misappropriating public money, and using county property for private use. The court further held that defendant altered and destroyed public records in an attempt to conceal evidence and dispersed confidential information in order to influence the county civil service examination.
